## Detailed Research Results

### Category: Adhesion/Surface

#### Compatibilizer Selection for Blends
**Japanese**: ブレンド用相溶化剤の選定 | **Maturity**: established

> HSP-guided selection of block copolymer or graft copolymer compatibilizers for immiscible polymer blends. The compatibilizer must have segments that are HSP-compatible with each immiscible phase: one block matching polymer A and the other matching polymer B. By calculating Ra between each compatibilizer segment and each blend component, the optimal compatibilizer architecture can be identified. This reduces interfacial tension, stabilizes the morphology, and improves mechanical properties of the blend. The approach enables rapid screening of compatibilizer candidates from databases before expensive compounding trials.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Immiscible polymer blends have high interfacial tension leading to coarse phase morphology and poor mechanical properties. A compatibilizer (typically a block or graft copolymer A-b-B or A-g-B) acts as a macromolecular surfactant, localizing at the interface between the two phases. For effective compatibilization, block A of the compatibilizer must be miscible with polymer phase 1 (low Ra and RED < 1) and block B must be miscible with polymer phase 2 (low Ra and RED < 1). The HSP distance between each compatibilizer segment and its target phase determines the thermodynamic driving force for interfacial segregation. The interfacial tension reduction is approximately proportional to exp(-Ra^2), meaning small increases in HSP mismatch lead to rapidly diminishing compatibilization efficiency. Random copolymer compatibilizers can also be evaluated by calculating their blend-averaged HSP and comparing with both phases.
- **key_equation**: Ra(block_A, polymer_1) = sqrt(4*(dD_A-dD_1)^2 + (dP_A-dP_1)^2 + (dH_A-dH_1)^2); Ra(block_B, polymer_2) = sqrt(4*(dD_B-dD_2)^2 + (dP_B-dP_2)^2 + (dH_B-dH_2)^2); Effectiveness criterion: RED_A1 < 1 AND RED_B2 < 1
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) for polymer phase 1
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) for polymer phase 2
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) for each block/segment of the candidate compatibilizer
- Interaction radius R0 for each polymer phase
- Processing temperature (for melt blending)
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database (polymer and copolymer HSP values)
- Group contribution methods (Van Krevelen, Hoftyzer-Van Krevelen) for novel copolymer segments
- Literature HSP compilations for common polymers
- Copolymer HSP estimation via volume-fraction weighted averaging of homopolymer HSP
- Inverse gas chromatography for custom or synthesized compatibilizers
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Molecular weight of compatibilizer (affects interfacial activity)
- Block ratio and architecture (diblock, triblock, graft)
- Processing conditions (temperature, shear rate, mixing time)
- Blend composition ratio
- Melt viscosity ratio of the two phases
- Compatibilizer loading level (typically 1-10 wt%)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- HSP matching provides thermodynamic screening but does not account for kinetic aspects of compatibilizer migration to the interface during melt processing
- Block copolymer HSP is estimated from homopolymer values; actual segment HSP may differ due to confinement and chain connectivity effects
- Does not predict optimal compatibilizer molecular weight or block ratio, which are critical for interfacial activity
- Reactive compatibilization (in-situ formation of graft copolymers) involves chemical reactions not captured by HSP thermodynamics
- Crystallization effects in semicrystalline blend components can override thermodynamic compatibilization
- Volume-fraction averaging for random copolymer HSP is approximate and fails for alternating or gradient copolymers
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Systems where reactive compatibilization is dominant (e.g., PA/PP with maleic anhydride grafted PP) and the chemical reaction provides compatibility regardless of HSP match
- Blends where the compatibilizer forms micelles in one phase instead of migrating to the interface (excess compatibilizer)
- High-viscosity blends where kinetic barriers prevent compatibilizer from reaching the interface within processing time
- Blends with strong specific interactions (e.g., hydrogen bonding) that create compatibility beyond HSP prediction
- **validation_method**: 
- TEM or SEM morphology analysis (dispersed phase size reduction indicates effective compatibilization)
- Mechanical testing (tensile strength, impact strength, elongation at break)
- Rheological measurements (storage modulus, relaxation spectra for interfacial contribution)
- DSC (Tg shifts indicating partial miscibility at interface)
- Interfacial tension measurement (pendant drop or breaking thread method)

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: 
- calculateRa
- calculateRed
- classifyRisk
- optimizeBlend (for copolymer composition optimization)
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- Paul, D.R.; Bucknall, C.B. 'Polymer Blends', Wiley-Interscience, 2000
- Hansen, C.M. 'Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook', 2nd Ed., CRC Press, 2007
- Utracki, L.A.; Wilkie, C.A. 'Polymer Blends Handbook', 2nd Ed., Springer, 2014
- Van Krevelen, D.W.; Te Nijenhuis, K. 'Properties of Polymers', 4th Ed., Elsevier, 2009
- Taguet, A.; Cassagnau, P.; Lopez-Cuesta, J.M. 'Structuration, selective dispersion and compatibilizing effect of (nano)fillers in polymer blends', Progress in Polymer Science, 2014, DOI: 10.1016/j.progpolymsci.2014.04.002
- **regulatory_relevance**: Compatibilized polymer blends used in food-contact packaging must comply with FDA 21 CFR regulations and EU 10/2011 framework. The compatibilizer itself must be on approved substance lists. HSP screening supports REACH compliance by identifying safer compatibilizer alternatives during substitution assessments.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Automotive (PP/PA blends for under-hood parts, TPO bumper systems)
- Packaging (PE/PA barrier film blends, recycled polymer compatibility)
- Electronics (PPO/PS blends, engineering plastic alloys for housings)
- Recycling (mixed plastic waste stream compatibilization)
- Consumer goods (ABS/PC blends, impact-modified blends)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (polymer blend and compatibilizer screening module)
- Materials Studio (Synthia for copolymer HSP estimation)
- Aspen Polymers (blend thermodynamics with chi parameter)
- PolyInfo / NIMS database (polymer property database with blend data)
- COSMOtherm (COSMO-RS based compatibility prediction)

**Uncertain fields**: first_reported_year, typical_red_threshold

---

#### Primer-less Adhesion Design
**Japanese**: プライマーレス接着設計 | **Maturity**: emerging

> HSP optimization of the substrate-adhesive pair to eliminate the primer layer in automotive and aerospace assembly processes. Primers are traditionally used to bridge the HSP gap between substrates (metals, composites, plastics) and structural adhesives. By systematically matching adhesive HSP to substrate surface HSP, or by modifying adhesive formulation to shift its HSP toward the substrate, direct bonding without primer becomes feasible. This reduces process steps, cost, VOC emissions, and cycle time while maintaining bond performance. HSP-guided adhesive reformulation targets the optimal balance of cohesive strength and adhesive compatibility.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: A primer functions as an HSP intermediary: its HSP is close to both the substrate and the adhesive, bridging the thermodynamic gap. To eliminate the primer, the adhesive formulation must be modified so that its HSP directly matches the substrate surface HSP. This is achieved by adjusting adhesive resin chemistry, adding tackifiers, or incorporating functional monomers that shift dP and dH toward the substrate values. The HSP optimization target is to minimize Ra(adhesive, substrate) while maintaining the adhesive's bulk mechanical properties (modulus, Tg, elongation). For dissimilar-material joints, the adhesive HSP must be optimized as a compromise between both substrate surfaces. The RED framework provides the design criterion: post-reformulation RED < 1.0 for each interface, without primer. Additionally, HSP analysis of the primer system reveals which HSP components the primer was compensating, guiding the direction of adhesive modification.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_adh-dD_sub)^2 + (dP_adh-dP_sub)^2 + (dH_adh-dH_sub)^2); RED = Ra/R0; Target: RED(adhesive_new, substrate) < RED(adhesive_old, substrate) and RED(adhesive_new, substrate) < 1.0
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- Surface HSP (dD, dP, dH) of substrate A (as-received or treated surface)
- Surface HSP (dD, dP, dH) of substrate B (for dissimilar joints)
- HSP (dD, dP, dH) of current adhesive formulation
- HSP (dD, dP, dH) of current primer (to understand the bridging function)
- R0 of the adhesive system
- Target mechanical property constraints (modulus, Tg, elongation)
- **data_sources**: 
- Contact angle measurements with probe liquid series (substrate surface HSP)
- HSPiP database (adhesive resin and additive HSP values)
- Group contribution methods for new adhesive formulation HSP estimation
- Adhesive supplier technical documentation
- Inverse gas chromatography (surface energy characterization)
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Surface roughness and morphology of substrates
- Substrate surface energy (dispersive and polar components)
- Adhesive rheology (flow and wetting behavior)
- Cure kinetics and degree of conversion
- Environmental durability requirements (humidity, thermal cycling, UV)
- Surface contamination levels (oils, release agents, oxidation products)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- Surface HSP determination is challenging and less precise than bulk HSP measurement, leading to uncertainty in the optimization target
- Modifying adhesive HSP to match substrate may compromise cohesive strength, requiring multi-objective optimization
- Metal surface HSP is strongly influenced by oxide layer thickness, contamination, and surface treatment, making it a moving target
- Long-term durability of primer-less joints may be inferior even when initial bond strength is adequate, as primers often provide corrosion protection
- Does not account for chemical bonding contributions (silane coupling agents, adhesion promoters) that primers may provide
- Cure shrinkage stresses in the adhesive bondline may cause failure at interfaces that are marginally HSP-compatible
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Substrates with weak boundary layers (oxidized polymer, contaminated metal) where primer removal exposes the weak layer
- Joints exposed to hot-wet conditions where moisture ingress at the unprotected interface causes adhesive failure
- Low-surface-energy substrates (PP, PE, PTFE) where HSP matching alone cannot overcome the inherently low work of adhesion
- Dissimilar-material joints where the optimal HSP for one substrate is far from optimal for the other, making compromise impossible
- **validation_method**: 
- Lap shear strength testing (ASTM D1002) with and without primer comparison
- Wedge crack test (Boeing wedge test, ASTM D3762) for durability assessment
- Environmental aging (cyclic corrosion, humidity exposure, thermal shock)
- Failure mode analysis (adhesive vs. cohesive) as the primary success criterion
- Salt spray exposure (ASTM B117) for corrosion-related delamination

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: high
- **ui_pattern**: optimization
- **existing_modules_reusable**: 
- calculateRa
- calculateRed
- classifyRisk
- optimizeBlend (for adhesive formulation HSP tuning)
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- Hansen, C.M. 'Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook', 2nd Ed., CRC Press, 2007
- Abbott, S.; Hansen, C.M. 'Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice (HSPiP)', 5th Ed., 2015
- Kinloch, A.J. 'Adhesion and Adhesives: Science and Technology', Chapman & Hall, 1987
- Davis, G.D.; Venables, J.D. 'Surface Treatment of Metal Adherends', in Adhesion Science and Engineering, Elsevier, 2002
- Comyn, J. 'Adhesion Science', RSC Paperbacks, Royal Society of Chemistry, 1997

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Automotive (body-in-white multi-material bonding, closure panel hemming)
- Aerospace (composite-to-metal secondary structure bonding)
- Electronics (display lamination, camera module assembly)
- Renewable energy (solar panel frame bonding, wind blade assembly)
- Consumer appliances (glass-to-metal bonding in home appliances)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (adhesive-substrate HSP matching and reformulation guidance)
- COSMOtherm (surface interaction energy prediction)
- Henkel and Sika adhesive selection guides (primer-less product lines)
- Dow BETAFORCE and BETAMATE primer-less adhesive systems
- Materials Studio (molecular-level adhesion simulation)

**Uncertain fields**: first_reported_year, typical_red_threshold, regulatory_relevance

---

#### Structural Adhesive Joint Design
**Japanese**: 構造用接着継手設計 | **Maturity**: established

> HSP matching between a structural adhesive and both adherend surfaces is used to predict adhesive bond strength and long-term durability. The principle is that thermodynamic work of adhesion correlates with HSP proximity between adhesive and substrate. By calculating Ra between the adhesive and each adherend, engineers can screen adhesive candidates for optimal compatibility with dissimilar-material joints (e.g., metal-to-composite, aluminum-to-CFRP). Low Ra values indicate favorable intermolecular interactions at the interface, leading to higher bond strength and better resistance to environmental degradation (moisture, thermal cycling).

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The underlying principle is that adhesion strength at a bonded interface depends on the thermodynamic work of adhesion, which is maximized when intermolecular interactions between adhesive and adherend are well-matched. The HSP distance Ra between adhesive and substrate surface quantifies this interaction mismatch. For a joint with two different adherends (A and B), the adhesive should minimize both Ra(adhesive-A) and Ra(adhesive-B). The work of adhesion Wa is related to surface energies via Wa = gamma_A + gamma_adhesive - gamma_AB, and HSP proximity minimizes the interfacial energy gamma_AB. In practice, RED values for both interfaces should be low. For structural joints under load, the cohesive strength of the adhesive and mechanical interlocking also contribute, but HSP matching ensures the interfacial (adhesive) failure mode is suppressed in favor of cohesive failure, which is the preferred failure mode in structural design.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_adh-dD_sub)^2 + (dP_adh-dP_sub)^2 + (dH_adh-dH_sub)^2); RED = Ra/R0; Wa ~ 2*sqrt(gamma_d1*gamma_d2) + 2*sqrt(gamma_p1*gamma_p2) + 2*sqrt(gamma_h1*gamma_h2)
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) for the structural adhesive (cured state)
- Surface HSP values (dD, dP, dH) for adherend A
- Surface HSP values (dD, dP, dH) for adherend B
- Interaction radius R0 for the adhesive
- Service temperature range
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database (adhesive and polymer HSP values)
- Contact angle measurements with probe liquids (surface HSP determination)
- Inverse gas chromatography (surface energy and HSP of adherends)
- Adhesive manufacturer technical data sheets
- Literature values for common substrates (metals, CFRP, glass)
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Surface roughness (Ra, Rz) of adherends
- Surface free energy components (dispersive, polar)
- Adhesive modulus and Tg (mechanical performance)
- Joint geometry (overlap length, bondline thickness)
- Environmental conditions (humidity, temperature cycling range)
- Surface pretreatment details (abrasion, chemical etch, primer)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- HSP matching addresses only the thermodynamic component of adhesion; mechanical interlocking, chemical bonding (covalent, ionic), and diffusion bonding are not captured
- Surface HSP may differ significantly from bulk HSP due to surface reconstruction, contamination, or oxidation layers
- Cured adhesive HSP depends on cure schedule and degree of conversion, introducing variability
- Does not predict failure load directly; only indicates relative ranking of adhesive-substrate compatibility
- Environmental degradation mechanisms (hydrolysis, oxidation) require kinetic modeling beyond HSP
- Metal adherend surface HSP is difficult to define and highly dependent on oxide layer and surface treatment
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Highly reactive adhesives (cyanoacrylates, structural acrylics) where chemical bonding dominates over physical adsorption, making HSP matching less relevant
- Joints with mechanical interlocking as primary adhesion mechanism (rough or porous substrates)
- Low-surface-energy substrates (PTFE, polyolefins) where HSP matching is necessary but not sufficient without surface activation
- High-temperature joints where adhesive degradation limits performance regardless of initial HSP match
- **validation_method**: 
- Lap shear strength testing (ASTM D1002, ISO 4587)
- Peel testing (ASTM D903, T-peel, climbing drum)
- Failure mode analysis (adhesive vs. cohesive vs. mixed failure)
- Environmental aging tests (humidity exposure, thermal cycling, salt spray)
- Cross-cut adhesion testing (ASTM D3359) for coating-like thin adhesive layers

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: optimization
- **existing_modules_reusable**: 
- calculateRa
- calculateRed
- classifyRisk
- optimizeBlend (for adhesive formulation adjustment)
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- Hansen, C.M. 'Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook', 2nd Ed., CRC Press, 2007, Chapter 11: Adhesion
- Packham, D.E. 'Handbook of Adhesion', 2nd Ed., John Wiley & Sons, 2005
- Abbott, S.; Hansen, C.M. 'Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice (HSPiP)', 5th Ed., 2015, Chapter on Adhesion
- Kinloch, A.J. 'Adhesion and Adhesives: Science and Technology', Chapman & Hall, 1987
- Della Volpe, C.; Siboni, S. 'Acid-base surface free energies of solids and the definition of scales in the Good-van Oss-Chaudhury theory', Journal of Adhesion Science and Technology, 2000
- **regulatory_relevance**: Structural adhesive joints in aerospace must meet certification requirements (FAA AC 20-107B, EASA CS-25). While HSP is not directly mandated, it is used as a screening tool during adhesive qualification programs. Automotive structural bonding follows ISO 11339 and OEM-specific qualification protocols where material compatibility assessment (including HSP analysis) supports design rationale.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Aerospace (metal-to-composite bonding, CFRP structural joints)
- Automotive (multi-material lightweight body construction, BIW bonding)
- Wind energy (blade root bonding, spar cap adhesion)
- Marine (hull panel bonding, composite-to-metal joints)
- Construction (structural glazing, curtain wall adhesive selection)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (adhesive-substrate matching module)
- Henkel LOCTITE Design Guide (proprietary adhesive selection tool)
- 3M Adhesive Selector (web-based tool with compatibility data)
- Dassault SIMULIA (FEA with cohesive zone models for joint analysis)
- COSMOtherm (surface interaction energy estimation)

**Uncertain fields**: first_reported_year, typical_red_threshold, sensitivity_to_hbond_split

---

#### Surface Treatment Effect Quantification
**Japanese**: 表面処理効果の定量化 | **Maturity**: established

> This application uses HSP to quantify the change in surface properties after corona, plasma, flame, or chemical surface treatments. By measuring contact angles with a series of probe liquids before and after treatment, the surface HSP can be determined via the Owens-Wendt or van Oss-Chaudhury-Good framework extended to Hansen's three parameters. The shift in surface HSP (delta-dD, delta-dP, delta-dH) provides a quantitative metric for treatment effectiveness, enabling optimization of treatment intensity, comparison of treatment methods, and prediction of adhesion improvement for specific adhesive-substrate combinations.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Surface treatments (corona, plasma, flame, UV/ozone, chemical etching) modify the surface chemistry of polymers by introducing polar functional groups (hydroxyl, carboxyl, carbonyl, amine) and increasing surface roughness. These chemical changes shift the surface HSP, primarily increasing dP and dH components while dD remains relatively unchanged. By performing contact angle measurements with a series of probe liquids spanning Hansen space, the surface HSP before and after treatment can be determined. The HSP shift vector (delta-dP, delta-dH) directly quantifies the polar and hydrogen-bonding enhancement. Larger shifts in dP and dH toward the adhesive's HSP indicate more effective treatment for that adhesive system. The magnitude of the HSP shift also correlates with treatment aging (decay of surface activation over time), enabling shelf-life estimation for treated surfaces.
- **key_equation**: cos(theta) = -1 + 2*sqrt(gamma_d_s*gamma_d_l)/gamma_l + 2*sqrt(gamma_p_s*gamma_p_l)/gamma_l; Surface HSP from contact angle series inversion; Delta_HSP = sqrt(4*(dD_after-dD_before)^2 + (dP_after-dP_before)^2 + (dH_after-dH_before)^2)
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: low

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- Contact angle measurements with minimum 5-6 probe liquids (before and after treatment)
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) and surface tension components of each probe liquid
- Treatment parameters (power, speed, distance, gas composition for plasma/corona)
- HSP of the target adhesive or coating (for RED calculation)
- Time between treatment and measurement (aging effect)
- **data_sources**: 
- Contact angle goniometry (sessile drop method)
- HSPiP database (probe liquid HSP values)
- Literature compilations of liquid surface tension components
- XPS data (for correlation with chemical group introduction)
- FTIR-ATR spectra (for surface functional group identification)
- **data_availability_score**: 4
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Surface energy components (dispersive, polar, acid-base)
- Surface roughness before and after treatment (AFM, profilometry)
- XPS elemental composition (O/C ratio as treatment indicator)
- Water contact angle (quick screening metric)
- Dyne level (surface energy classification)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- Contact angle measurements are sensitive to surface roughness, heterogeneity, and contamination, introducing scatter in derived HSP values
- The inversion from contact angles to surface HSP is ill-conditioned; different probe liquid sets can yield different surface HSP values
- Surface HSP represents an average over a heterogeneous treated surface; local variations in treatment intensity are not captured
- Surface activation decays over time (minutes to weeks depending on substrate); HSP measurements are time-dependent
- The Owens-Wendt and van Oss-Chaudhury-Good models have theoretical limitations in mapping surface energetics to bulk HSP framework
- Surface restructuring (migration of treated groups into bulk) complicates long-term predictions
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Over-treated surfaces where polymer degradation (chain scission, pitting) reduces adhesion despite favorable HSP shift
- Substrates with blooming additives or slip agents that re-contaminate the surface after treatment
- High-crystallinity polymers (HDPE, POM) where surface treatment penetration is limited by crystalline domains
- Probe liquid spreading or partial dissolution of treated surface during contact angle measurement
- **validation_method**: 
- Peel adhesion testing before and after treatment (ASTM D903)
- Cross-cut adhesion test (ASTM D3359)
- Tape adhesion test (ASTM D3330)
- XPS correlation (O/C ratio vs. HSP shift)
- Wetting envelope construction and comparison with adhesive surface tension

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: pairwise
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: binary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- Hansen, C.M. 'Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook', 2nd Ed., CRC Press, 2007
- Owens, D.K.; Wendt, R.C. 'Estimation of the surface free energy of polymers', Journal of Applied Polymer Science, 1969, DOI: 10.1002/app.1969.070130815
- van Oss, C.J.; Chaudhury, M.K.; Good, R.J. 'Interfacial Lifshitz-van der Waals and polar interactions in macroscopic systems', Chemical Reviews, 1988, DOI: 10.1021/cr00088a006
- Strobel, M.; Lyons, C.S. 'An essay on contact angle measurements', Plasma Processes and Polymers, 2011, DOI: 10.1002/ppap.201000041
- Abbott, S.; Hansen, C.M. 'Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice (HSPiP)', 5th Ed., 2015

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Automotive (polyolefin bumper painting, TPO adhesion improvement)
- Packaging (PE/PP film corona treatment for printing and lamination)
- Medical devices (polymer surface activation for bonding and biocompatibility coatings)
- Electronics (plasma treatment for encapsulant adhesion on PCBs)
- Aerospace (CFRP peel ply removal and surface activation monitoring)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (surface HSP estimation from contact angle data)
- Kruss ADVANCE software (contact angle analysis with surface energy models)
- DataPhysics OCA series software (automated contact angle with HSP output)
- Tantec surface energy measurement systems
- Surface Analyst (Brighton Science) for real-time surface quality monitoring

**Uncertain fields**: first_reported_year, typical_red_threshold, regulatory_relevance

### Category: Coatings/Inks

#### Anti-Graffiti Coating Design
**Japanese**: 防落書きコーティング設計 | **Maturity**: established

> Anti-graffiti coatings are designed to maximize HSP mismatch between the protective topcoat and common graffiti materials (spray paints, markers, inks). By ensuring a large HSP distance (high Ra or RED > 1) between the coating surface and graffiti media, the graffiti cannot wet, penetrate, or bond strongly to the surface, enabling easy removal with mild solvents or pressure washing without damaging the underlying coating.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The principle is inverted compared to adhesion optimization: the goal is to maximize incompatibility between the topcoat and graffiti materials. A coating with HSP far from common graffiti solvents and binders (typically aromatic hydrocarbons, ketones, and alkyd/acrylic resins) will resist penetration and adhesion of graffiti. Fluoropolymers and silicone-based coatings achieve this naturally due to their very low dP and dH values, creating large Ra distances from most graffiti formulations. The coating must simultaneously maintain adhesion to the underlying substrate (low Ra with substrate) while repelling graffiti (high Ra with graffiti).
- **key_equation**: Ra_graffiti = sqrt(4*(dD_coat - dD_graffiti)^2 + (dP_coat - dP_graffiti)^2 + (dH_coat - dH_graffiti)^2); RED_graffiti = Ra_graffiti / R0_coat >> 1 desired
- **red_direction**: high_is_good
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: low

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- Topcoat HSP (dD, dP, dH)
- HSP of common graffiti materials (spray paint binders, marker solvents, permanent ink resins)
- Substrate HSP for adhesion verification
- Topcoat interaction radius R0
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for fluoropolymers, silicones, polyurethanes
- Literature HSP for common spray paint formulations (alkyd, acrylic, lacquer)
- Manufacturer data for anti-graffiti coating resins
- Contact angle measurements for surface characterization
- Group contribution methods for novel coating chemistries
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Surface energy and contact angle (water and diiodomethane)
- Surface roughness
- Coating hardness and crosslink density
- Chemical resistance to cleaning agents
- UV and weathering stability
- Porosity of substrate (for sacrificial systems)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- Graffiti materials vary widely in composition; a single HSP target for 'graffiti' is an oversimplification
- Surface texture and porosity effects are not captured by HSP alone
- Sacrificial anti-graffiti systems work by a different mechanism (removal of coating layer) not well described by HSP mismatch
- Weathering and UV degradation change coating surface HSP over time
- Permanent anti-graffiti coatings may lose performance after repeated cleaning cycles
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Aggressive solvent-based graffiti (e.g., bitumen markers) that can penetrate even low-energy surfaces
- Porous substrates (bare concrete, brick) where graffiti penetrates below the coating
- Coatings that degrade under UV exposure, increasing surface energy and graffiti adhesion over time
- Etching-type vandalism (acid-based) that operates by chemical attack rather than adhesion
- **validation_method**: Graffiti application and removal testing per ASTM D6578 or EN 1062-11, contact angle measurement before and after cleaning cycles, cross-cut adhesion testing of coating to substrate, accelerated weathering (QUV) followed by graffiti resistance re-testing

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: optimization
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk, optimizeBlend
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- Hansen, C.M. 'Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook', 2nd ed., CRC Press, 2007
- Rabea, A.M., Mohseni, M. 'Anti-graffiti coatings: a review', Journal of Coatings Technology and Research, 2015, Vol. 12, pp. 1-15
- Lettieri, M., Masieri, M. 'Surface characterization and effectiveness of anti-graffiti coatings on cultural heritage stone materials', Applied Surface Science, 2014
- Abbott, S. 'Chemical resistance and Hansen Solubility Parameters', HSPiP documentation, 2015
- **regulatory_relevance**: VOC content regulations (EPA, EU Directive 2004/42/EC) apply to anti-graffiti coating formulations. No specific regulatory framework references HSP-based design, but municipal and transportation authorities specify performance standards for anti-graffiti systems.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: Public infrastructure and transportation, Cultural heritage and monument preservation, Commercial building facades, Public transit vehicles (trains, buses), Urban furniture and signage
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice)
- COSMOtherm (for novel coating molecular design)
- DataPhysics OCA (contact angle measurement)
- Proprietary formulation tools from coating manufacturers (e.g., Teknos, Protectosil)

**Uncertain fields**: first_reported_year, typical_red_threshold, data_availability_score

#### Coating Defect Prediction
**Japanese**: 塗膜欠陥予測 | **Maturity**: established

> Surface tension differences combined with HSP mismatch between coating layers or between coating and substrate are used to predict common defects such as cratering, dewetting, fish eyes, and orange peel. When a contaminant or adjacent layer has significantly different surface energy and HSP from the wet film, Marangoni flow driven by surface tension gradients creates visible defects. HSP matching between successive coating layers ensures interlayer compatibility and prevents delamination, while HSP proximity between solvent blend and resin maintains film uniformity during drying.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Coating defects arise from two interacting mechanisms: (1) surface tension gradients (Marangoni effect) that drive lateral flow in the wet film, and (2) thermodynamic incompatibility between components quantified by HSP distance. Cratering occurs when a low-surface-tension contaminant locally reduces surface tension, pulling surrounding liquid away. The severity correlates with both the surface tension difference and the HSP mismatch (which prevents re-dissolution of the contaminant into the bulk film). Dewetting occurs when the coating HSP is far from the substrate surface energy characteristics, reducing adhesive forces below cohesive forces. Orange peel results from surface tension-driven flow that cannot level before viscosity rise during drying, exacerbated by solvent blend HSP drift as fast-evaporating components leave. HSP is used to design solvent blends that maintain consistent surface tension and polymer solvency throughout the evaporation process.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD1-dD2)^2 + (dP1-dP2)^2 + (dH1-dH2)^2); RED = Ra/R0; Marangoni number Ma = -(d_gamma/d_c) * (L*Delta_c) / (eta*D)
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: RED < 1.0 between all coating components for defect-free films; RED > 1.5 between contaminant and film strongly predicts cratering
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP of coating resin/binder (dD, dP, dH)
- HSP of solvent blend (dD, dP, dH, volume-weighted)
- Surface tension of wet coating
- HSP and surface tension of substrate or previous coat
- Solvent evaporation rate profile
- HSP of potential contaminants (silicones, oils, etc.)
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for solvents, resins, and common contaminants
- Surface tension handbooks and databases
- Coating manufacturer technical data sheets
- Published solvent evaporation rate tables (relative to butyl acetate)
- Contact angle measurements for substrate surface energy estimation
- **data_availability_score**: 4
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Surface tension of all components and blends
- Solvent evaporation rates (relative and absolute)
- Wet film thickness and application method
- Substrate surface energy (polar and dispersive components)
- Application and drying temperature
- Film viscosity profile during drying
- Additive (leveling agent, defoamer) concentration

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- Defect formation is highly kinetic: HSP and surface tension predict thermodynamic driving forces but not whether the defect actually forms before film immobilization
- Marangoni flow modeling requires fluid dynamics simulation beyond simple HSP calculations
- Substrate surface heterogeneity (dust, oil, roughness) introduces variability not captured by average surface energy
- Application method (spray, roll, dip, curtain) affects defect types and thresholds in ways HSP cannot predict
- Multi-layer systems require tracking HSP compatibility at each interface during dynamic drying
- Solvent blend HSP changes continuously during evaporation, requiring time-resolved modeling
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Silicone contamination causing craters even at trace levels (ppm) that are below HSP modeling resolution
- Water-borne coatings where complex surfactant-polymer interactions dominate over simple HSP matching
- High-solids coatings where viscosity effects dominate and solvent HSP role is minimal
- UV-cure coatings where photoinitiator compatibility rather than solvent evaporation drives defect formation
- Powder coatings where melt flow rather than solution thermodynamics governs leveling
- **validation_method**: 
- Visual inspection and grading of coated panels (ASTM D714 for blistering, custom scales for cratering)
- Surface profilometry for orange peel quantification (wavescan DOI)
- Contact angle measurement before and after coating
- Cross-cut adhesion test (ASTM D3359) for delamination prediction
- Accelerated weathering and immersion testing for long-term defect emergence

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: high
- **ui_pattern**: matrix
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk, optimizeBlend
- **multi_component_complexity**: multi
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Wicks, Z.W.; Jones, F.N.; Pappas, S.P.; Wicks, D.A. | **title**: Organic Coatings: Science and Technology, 3rd Edition | **journal**: Wiley-Interscience | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1002/047011422X
- **author**: Bierwagen, G.P. | **title**: Surface defects and surface flows in coatings | **journal**: Progress in Organic Coatings | **year**: 1991 | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **author**: Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook, 2nd Edition | **journal**: CRC Press | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1201/9781420006834
- **author**: Orchard, S.E. | **title**: On surface levelling in viscous liquids and gels | **journal**: Applied Scientific Research, Section A | **year**: 1962 | **doi**: 10.1007/BF00382198
- **author**: Scriven, L.E.; Sternling, C.V. | **title**: The Marangoni Effects | **journal**: Nature | **year**: 1960 | **doi**: 10.1038/187186a0
- **regulatory_relevance**: HSP-based defect prediction supports VOC-compliant reformulation efforts under EPA, EU Directive 2004/42/EC, and similar regulations. By optimizing solvent blend HSP to maintain film quality with reduced VOC content, formulators can meet environmental targets without sacrificing appearance quality.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Automotive OEM and refinish coatings
- Aerospace coatings
- Architectural and decorative paints
- Industrial metal coatings
- Wood furniture coatings
- Electronics conformal coatings
- Packaging coatings and printing
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice)
- COSMOtherm
- BASF Formulation Optimizer
- Proprietary tools from coating additive suppliers (BYK, Tego/Evonik, Elementis)
- COMSOL Multiphysics (for Marangoni flow simulation)

**Uncertain fields**: literature.key_references[1].doi, literature.first_reported_year

#### Ink-Substrate Adhesion
**Japanese**: インク-基材間接着性 | **Maturity**: established

> HSP distance between ink formulation and substrate surface HSP predicts adhesion quality and delamination risk. By calculating the Ra (HSP distance) between the ink binder system and the substrate surface energy parameters, formulators can predict whether an ink will wet, adhere to, and remain bonded to a given substrate. Low Ra values indicate thermodynamic compatibility and strong adhesion, while high Ra values suggest poor wetting and risk of delamination under mechanical or environmental stress.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The adhesion between an ink film and a substrate is governed by thermodynamic compatibility at the interface. When the HSP of the ink binder closely match the surface HSP of the substrate, intermolecular interactions across the interface are maximized, leading to strong adhesion. The HSP distance Ra quantifies this mismatch: a smaller Ra means the ink binder and substrate share similar dispersion, polar, and hydrogen bonding character, promoting molecular-level contact and interdiffusion at the interface. Surface treatment (corona, plasma, flame) modifies substrate surface HSP to improve compatibility with target inks.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_ink - dD_sub)^2 + (dP_ink - dP_sub)^2 + (dH_ink - dH_sub)^2)
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: Ra < 5 MPa^0.5 for good adhesion; RED < 1.0 when using ink binder solubility sphere
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- Ink binder/resin HSP (dD, dP, dH)
- Substrate surface HSP (dD, dP, dH)
- Ink binder interaction radius R0 (if using RED approach)
- Surface treatment conditions (if applicable)
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for common polymeric binders
- Contact angle measurements + Owens-Wendt or van Oss method for substrate surface HSP
- Inverse gas chromatography (IGC) for substrate surface characterization
- Supplier technical data sheets for ink resin HSP
- Literature values for common substrates (PET, PE, PP, paper, glass)
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Surface energy and contact angle of substrate
- Ink viscosity and surface tension
- Substrate surface roughness
- Drying/curing temperature and time
- Film thickness
- Surface treatment level (dyne level)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- Substrate surface HSP estimation from contact angle data introduces uncertainty, especially for heterogeneous or porous surfaces
- HSP model does not capture mechanical interlocking contributions to adhesion on rough substrates
- Crosslinking and curing reactions alter final film HSP from initial formulation values
- Migration of additives (plasticizers, slip agents) to the interface can change adhesion over time
- HSP approach assumes equilibrium thermodynamics but adhesion also depends on kinetic factors like drying rate and solvent retention
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Highly crystalline substrates (e.g., untreated PP, PE) where surface HSP measurement is unreliable
- Systems dominated by mechanical interlocking rather than chemical adhesion
- Multi-layer ink systems where interlayer interactions dominate over ink-substrate interface
- Substrates with surface contamination or release agents that mask true surface HSP
- **validation_method**: Cross-cut adhesion test (ASTM D3359), peel strength measurement (ASTM D903), tape adhesion test, scotch tape pull-off test, and accelerated aging followed by adhesion re-testing

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: matrix
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: binary
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- Hansen, C.M. 'Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook', 2nd ed., CRC Press, 2007
- Abbott, S., Hansen, C.M. 'Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice', HSPiP, 5th ed., 2015
- Rabelink, M. et al. 'Predicting ink adhesion on polymer films using Hansen solubility parameters', Progress in Organic Coatings, 2018
- Ghosh, P. 'Adhesion and surface energy of polymeric films for flexible packaging', Journal of Applied Polymer Science, 2012
- **regulatory_relevance**: Not directly regulated, but adhesion performance requirements are specified in food packaging (FDA 21 CFR), pharmaceutical packaging (USP), and industrial coating standards (ISO 2409). HSP-based screening is used internally by formulators but not formally referenced in regulations.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: Flexible packaging and converting, Commercial and publication printing, Label and tag printing, Automotive coatings, Consumer electronics decoration
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice)
- DataPhysics OCA contact angle systems (for substrate HSP)
- Surface Analyst (for surface energy measurement)
- Proprietary ink formulation software from major ink manufacturers

**Uncertain fields**: first_reported_year, data_availability_score

#### Multilayer Coating Interlayer Adhesion
**Japanese**: 多層コーティング層間接着性 | **Maturity**: established

> In multilayer coating systems (primer/basecoat/clearcoat), HSP distance between adjacent layers predicts the risk of interlayer delamination. By mapping the HSP gradient across the coating stack, formulators can identify weak interfaces where large HSP mismatches may cause adhesion failure. Optimizing the HSP profile so that each pair of adjacent layers has a sufficiently small Ra ensures robust interlayer adhesion across the full system.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Each layer in a multilayer coating system has its own HSP determined by its resin chemistry, crosslinker, and additives. Interlayer adhesion depends on thermodynamic compatibility at each interface: when two adjacent layers have similar HSP, polymer chain interdiffusion across the interface is favored during the coating and curing process, creating a strong interphase. Large HSP jumps between layers create weak interfaces prone to delamination. The HSP gradient concept allows systematic analysis of the entire stack by computing Ra for each adjacent layer pair and identifying the weakest interface. Additionally, residual solvent from lower layers can partially dissolve or swell the upper layer if HSP are too close, causing defects.
- **key_equation**: Ra_ij = sqrt(4*(dD_i - dD_j)^2 + (dP_i - dP_j)^2 + (dH_i - dH_j)^2) for each adjacent layer pair (i, j)
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP (dD, dP, dH) for each layer (primer, basecoat, clearcoat, etc.)
- HSP of substrate surface
- Interaction radius R0 for each layer material (if using RED approach)
- Curing/drying temperature for each layer
- Solvent system HSP for each layer
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for common coating resins (epoxy, polyurethane, acrylic, alkyd, melamine)
- Supplier technical data for resin and crosslinker HSP
- Group contribution calculation for custom resin blends
- IGC measurements for cured film HSP characterization
- Literature values for automotive and industrial coating systems
- **data_availability_score**: 4
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Glass transition temperature (Tg) of each layer
- Crosslink density of each cured layer
- Thermal expansion coefficient mismatch between layers
- Solvent retention in each layer before overcoating
- Flash-off time between layer applications
- Film thickness of each layer
- Surface tension and wetting behavior

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- HSP of cured/crosslinked films differ from uncured resin HSP and are difficult to measure directly
- Interdiffusion kinetics depend on application window timing, not just thermodynamic compatibility
- Mechanical stress at interfaces due to differential shrinkage or thermal expansion is not captured by HSP
- Chemical bonding (covalent crosslinks across interface) can provide adhesion independent of HSP match
- Additives, pigments, and fillers modify effective layer HSP in ways difficult to predict
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Overcoating too late on a highly crosslinked layer where interdiffusion is kinetically blocked despite good HSP match
- Solvent pop or lifting when upper layer solvent strongly swells the lower layer (HSP too similar with aggressive solvents)
- Adhesion failure at primer-substrate interface on contaminated or poorly prepared surfaces
- Delamination caused by internal stress relaxation during thermal cycling, unrelated to HSP compatibility
- **validation_method**: Cross-cut adhesion test (ISO 2409 / ASTM D3359), pull-off adhesion test (ASTM D4541), mandrel bend test, impact resistance test, thermal cycling followed by adhesion re-testing, scanning electron microscopy of fracture interfaces

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: matrix
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: multi
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- Hansen, C.M. 'Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook', 2nd ed., CRC Press, 2007
- Awaja, F. et al. 'Adhesion of polymers', Progress in Polymer Science, 2009, Vol. 34, pp. 948-968
- Nichols, M.E. et al. 'Intercoat adhesion in automotive paint systems', Journal of Coatings Technology, 2002, Vol. 74, No. 924
- van Oss, C.J. 'Interfacial Forces in Aqueous Media', CRC Press, 2006
- Abbott, S., Hansen, C.M. 'Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice', HSPiP, 5th ed., 2015
- **regulatory_relevance**: Automotive OEM specifications (e.g., SAE J400, Ford/GM/Toyota internal specs) require interlayer adhesion testing. Aerospace coatings follow MIL-PRF standards. HSP-based screening is used as a formulation tool but is not explicitly referenced in regulatory standards.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Automotive OEM and refinish coatings
- Aerospace coatings
- Protective and marine coatings
- Architectural coatings
- Can and coil coatings
- Electronics conformal coatings
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice)
- COSMOtherm (for molecular-level compatibility prediction)
- Proprietary formulation software from major coatings companies (PPG, AkzoNobel, BASF, Axalta)
- Materials Studio (for molecular dynamics simulation of interface)

**Uncertain fields**: first_reported_year, typical_red_threshold

---

#### Pigment Dispersion Stability
**Japanese**: 顔料分散安定性 | **Maturity**: mature

> The HSP distance (Ra) and Relative Energy Difference (RED) between a pigment surface and the vehicle (resin/solvent system) predict dispersion stability, flocculation tendency, and color development in paint, ink, and coating formulations. Pigments with surface HSP close to the vehicle HSP are well-wetted and thermodynamically stabilized against flocculation, leading to superior color strength, gloss, and long-term stability. HSP matching guides both pigment surface treatment selection and vehicle formulation optimization.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Pigment particles are stabilized in a liquid medium when the vehicle HSP closely matches the pigment surface HSP (which may differ from the bulk pigment HSP due to surface treatments, coatings, or adsorbed dispersants). A low RED value means the vehicle effectively wets and solvates the pigment surface, creating a thermodynamic barrier to flocculation. When RED > 1, the pigment-vehicle interaction is unfavorable, leading to poor wetting, incomplete deflocculation during grinding, and flocculation on storage. The total HSP distance governs the Gibbs free energy of the pigment-vehicle interface: lower Ra means lower interfacial energy, stronger adsorption of vehicle molecules on the pigment surface, and greater resistance to aggregation. For multi-pigment systems, each pigment must be individually compatible with the vehicle to avoid differential flocculation and color shift.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_pigment-dD_vehicle)^2 + (dP_pigment-dP_vehicle)^2 + (dH_pigment-dH_vehicle)^2); RED = Ra/R0_pigment
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: RED < 1.0 for stable dispersion; RED < 0.8 for excellent long-term stability and color development
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: low

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- Pigment surface HSP (dD, dP, dH) - accounting for surface treatment
- Pigment interaction radius R0
- Vehicle/binder HSP (dD, dP, dH)
- Solvent blend HSP (volume-weighted average)
- Pigment volume concentration (PVC)
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database (extensive pigment and solvent data)
- Pigment manufacturer technical literature (BASF Colors & Effects, Clariant, Sun Chemical)
- Hansen and Abbott published pigment HSP tables
- Experimental determination via sedimentation or turbidity in solvent series
- Inverse gas chromatography for surface-specific HSP
- **data_availability_score**: 4
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Pigment particle size distribution
- Pigment surface area (BET)
- Surface treatment type and coverage
- Dispersant type and concentration
- Vehicle viscosity and rheology profile
- Zeta potential
- Pigment oil absorption number

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- Pigment surface HSP can differ significantly from bulk crystal HSP and depends heavily on surface treatments, which are often proprietary
- HSP alone does not capture steric or electrostatic stabilization contributions from polymeric dispersants
- Multi-pigment systems can exhibit complex co-flocculation behavior not predicted by individual RED values
- Pigment shape and aspect ratio (e.g., needle-like vs. spherical) affect dispersion stability but are not captured by HSP
- Drying and film formation change the vehicle HSP as solvent evaporates, potentially destabilizing initially stable dispersions
- Does not account for pigment crystal polymorphism effects on surface energy
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Surface-treated pigments where the treatment HSP rather than bulk pigment HSP controls behavior, but treatment HSP is unknown
- Controlled flocculation systems where mild flocculation is deliberately designed for rheology control
- Organic pigments with strong pi-pi stacking interactions that dominate over van der Waals forces represented in dD
- Carbon black dispersions where surface chemistry (pH, oxygen functional groups) varies greatly between grades
- **validation_method**: 
- Hegman grind gauge for dispersion fineness
- Rub-out test for flocculation detection
- Accelerated stability testing (centrifuge, elevated temperature storage)
- Color strength and tinting strength measurement (spectrophotometry)
- Viscosity monitoring over time
- Optical/electron microscopy for aggregate size assessment

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk, optimizeBlend
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook, 2nd Edition | **journal**: CRC Press | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1201/9781420006834
- **author**: Patton, T.C. | **title**: Paint Flow and Pigment Dispersion, 2nd Edition | **journal**: Wiley-Interscience | **year**: 1979 | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **author**: Wicks, Z.W.; Jones, F.N.; Pappas, S.P.; Wicks, D.A. | **title**: Organic Coatings: Science and Technology, 3rd Edition | **journal**: Wiley-Interscience | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1002/047011422X
- **author**: Flory, P.J.; Hansen, C.M. (via Skaarup, K.) | **title**: Characterization of pigment surfaces using HSP methodology | **journal**: Various publications and technical reports | **year**: 1967 | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **author**: Abbott, S.; Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice (HSPiP) software documentation | **journal**: HSPiP, 5th Edition eBook | **year**: 2013 | **doi**: N/A
- **first_reported_year**: 1967
- **regulatory_relevance**: HSP-based pigment-vehicle matching supports REACH and VOC compliance by enabling formulation of low-VOC and waterborne coatings with adequate pigment stability. Pigment dispersion quality indirectly affects food-contact compliance (FDA 21 CFR, EU 10/2011) through extractable substance levels.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Architectural and decorative paints
- Automotive OEM and refinish coatings
- Printing inks (offset, flexo, gravure, digital)
- Industrial coatings (wood, metal, plastic)
- Cosmetics and personal care (color cosmetics)
- Textile dyeing and printing
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice)
- COSMOtherm
- BASF Colors & Effects Dispersibility Guide
- Sun Chemical ColourSuite
- Proprietary dispersant selection tools (BYK, Tego/Evonik)

**Uncertain fields**: literature.key_references[1].doi, literature.key_references[3].doi

#### Residual Solvent Prediction
**Japanese**: 残留溶剤予測 | **Maturity**: established

> HSP affinity between a solvent and the dried or cured film matrix predicts residual solvent levels after the drying process. Solvents with HSP closely matching the film matrix (low Ra, RED < 1) are thermodynamically 'comfortable' within the polymer, leading to higher retention and slower release. Conversely, solvents with large HSP distance from the film matrix are driven out more efficiently during drying. This principle enables formulators to optimize solvent blends that provide good application properties but minimize retained solvent in the final product.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Residual solvent in a dried coating or film is governed by both thermodynamic affinity and diffusion kinetics. The thermodynamic driving force for solvent retention is quantified by HSP distance: solvents within the polymer's solubility sphere (RED < 1) have favorable interaction energy with the matrix and resist evaporation. The partition coefficient between the film and the vapor phase correlates with the Flory-Huggins interaction parameter chi, which is related to Ra. Solvents with low Ra remain trapped in the film longer, requiring higher temperatures or longer drying times for complete removal. This is particularly critical for food packaging, pharmaceutical coatings, and adhesive laminations where residual solvent limits are strictly regulated.
- **key_equation**: chi = V_s * (Ra)^2 / (6 * R * T); lower chi (lower Ra) = higher retention; residual solvent inversely correlates with RED of solvent-film pair
- **red_direction**: high_is_good
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: high

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP (dD, dP, dH) of each solvent in the formulation
- HSP (dD, dP, dH) of the dried/cured film matrix
- Interaction radius R0 of the film polymer
- Molar volume of each solvent
- Boiling point and vapor pressure of each solvent
- Drying temperature and time profile
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for solvent HSP (comprehensive coverage)
- HSPiP or literature for polymer film HSP
- Solvent physical property databases (boiling point, vapor pressure, molar volume)
- IGC measurements for cured/crosslinked film HSP
- Supplier data for coating resin systems
- **data_availability_score**: 4
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Solvent boiling point and evaporation rate
- Film thickness
- Glass transition temperature of the film polymer
- Crosslink density of cured films
- Drying oven temperature profile and air flow
- Diffusion coefficient of solvent in polymer
- Substrate permeability (for laminated structures)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- Drying is a kinetic process; HSP only describes thermodynamic affinity, not diffusion rate
- Film morphology changes during drying (skin formation) can trap solvents regardless of HSP
- Crosslinking during drying changes the film HSP continuously, making static HSP comparison approximate
- Multi-solvent blends create non-ideal evaporation profiles where azeotrope-like behavior occurs
- Very thin films and very thick films behave differently due to diffusion path length effects
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Solvent trapping under a skin layer formed by rapid surface drying, independent of HSP affinity
- Residual high-boiling co-solvents (e.g., NMP, cyclohexanone) retained despite moderate RED due to very low vapor pressure
- Water retention in hydrophilic films where hydrogen bonding creates strong specific interactions not fully captured by HSP
- Latent solvent release during lamination or storage when temperature rises above the drying temperature
- **validation_method**: Gas chromatography headspace analysis (GC-HS), total residual solvent by gravimetric method, GC-MS for specific solvent identification, real-time monitoring with process mass spectrometry, EN 13628 for residual solvents in packaging

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: production

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- Hansen, C.M. 'Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook', 2nd ed., CRC Press, 2007, Chapter on diffusion and solvent retention
- Abbott, S., Hansen, C.M. 'Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice', HSPiP, 5th ed., 2015
- Vrentas, J.S., Duda, J.L. 'Diffusion in polymer-solvent systems', Journal of Polymer Science: Polymer Physics, 1977, Vol. 15, pp. 403-416
- Miltz, J., Rosen-Doody, V. 'Migration of residual solvents in flexible food packaging', Packaging Technology and Science, 1993, Vol. 6, pp. 239-246
- Helmroth, I.E. et al. 'Predictive modeling of migration from packaging materials into food products', Trends in Food Science & Technology, 2002, Vol. 13, pp. 102-109
- **regulatory_relevance**: Directly relevant to food packaging regulations: EU Framework Regulation 1935/2004, EU Plastics Regulation 10/2011, FDA 21 CFR 175-178, EFSA guidelines on food contact materials. Residual solvent limits in pharmaceutical packaging per USP <467> and ICH Q3C. HSP-based screening is used by industry to guide solvent selection but is not a formal regulatory method.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: Flexible food packaging and lamination, Pharmaceutical packaging and coatings, Pressure-sensitive adhesives, Industrial protective coatings, Graphic arts and publication printing
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice)
- AKTS-SML (migration and diffusion modeling)
- COSMOtherm (for thermodynamic modeling)
- MIGRATEST EXP/LITE (migration simulation)
- GC-HS instrumentation (Shimadzu, Agilent) for validation

**Uncertain fields**: first_reported_year, typical_red_threshold

---

#### Solvent Substitution Design
**Japanese**: 溶剤代替設計 | **Maturity**: mature

> HSP distance minimization is used to find regulatory-compliant replacement solvents for banned or restricted originals. By identifying solvents or solvent blends with HSP values close to the original solvent, formulators can maintain dissolution power, coating performance, and process compatibility while meeting REACH, VOC, GHS, or other environmental and health regulations. HSP-based substitution is the primary rational design method for solvent replacement in the coatings, inks, adhesives, and cleaning industries.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The core principle is that solvents with similar HSP values will exhibit similar dissolution and solvation behavior toward a given solute or polymer. By calculating the HSP distance (Ra) between the banned solvent and candidate replacements, formulators can rank alternatives by functional equivalence. For solvent blends, the volume-weighted average HSP of the blend is matched to the target HSP of the original solvent. The approach extends to matching the RED value against the polymer's solubility sphere: any solvent or blend with RED < 1 for the target polymer is a functional replacement, but minimizing Ra to the original solvent ensures the closest match in evaporation behavior, viscosity effects, and process characteristics. Multi-objective optimization balances HSP matching with constraints on evaporation rate, flash point, toxicity, cost, and regulatory status.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_orig-dD_new)^2 + (dP_orig-dP_new)^2 + (dH_orig-dH_new)^2); for blends: dD_blend = sum(phi_i * dD_i), dP_blend = sum(phi_i * dP_i), dH_blend = sum(phi_i * dH_i)
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: Ra < 2-3 MPa^0.5 to the original solvent for close functional match; RED < 1.0 against the target polymer for adequate solvency
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: low

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP of original (banned/restricted) solvent (dD, dP, dH)
- HSP of target polymer/resin (dD, dP, dH) and R0
- HSP of candidate replacement solvents (dD, dP, dH)
- Regulatory constraint list (banned substances, VOC limits, OEL thresholds)
- Evaporation rate requirements
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database (comprehensive solvent HSP library with 1200+ solvents)
- REACH SVHC candidate list and Annex XIV authorization list
- EPA TRI and TSCA chemical inventories
- Solvent supplier technical data (Eastman, BASF, Dow, Solvay)
- Green chemistry solvent guides (CHEM21, GSK, Pfizer, ACS GCI)
- Published bio-based solvent HSP values
- **data_availability_score**: 5
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Evaporation rate (relative to butyl acetate or ether)
- Flash point and auto-ignition temperature
- Occupational exposure limits (TWA, STEL)
- VOC classification under relevant jurisdiction
- Cost per unit volume
- Boiling point and vapor pressure
- Kauri-butanol (Kb) value for cross-validation
- Viscosity and density

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- HSP matching ensures similar thermodynamic solvency but does not guarantee identical kinetic behavior (evaporation rate, diffusion rate)
- Solvent blends matched by average HSP may have different co-solvency effects than a single solvent at the same HSP point
- Non-equilibrium effects during coating application (evaporation-induced HSP drift) are not captured by static HSP matching
- Azeotrope formation in replacement solvent blends can change evaporation behavior unpredictably
- Some solvent functions (e.g., catalyst activity, reactivity with isocyanates) are chemical-specific and not captured by HSP
- Water-miscible to water-immiscible transitions cannot be predicted by HSP distance alone
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Chlorinated solvent replacement: high-dD solvents with no regulatory issues are scarce, making close HSP matching difficult
- NMP replacement: unique combination of high dP, dH, and high boiling point is hard to replicate with a single green solvent
- Toluene replacement in adhesives: xylene or high-flash-point aromatics match HSP but may face future restrictions themselves
- Multi-functional solvents (e.g., acting as both solvent and reactive diluent) where HSP matching does not address reactivity
- **validation_method**: 
- Dissolution power testing (Kauri-butanol value, aniline point)
- Viscosity reduction curves comparing original and replacement in target resin
- Application testing of reformulated coating (spray, brush, dip) for equivalent film quality
- Accelerated stability testing of reformulated product
- Evaporation rate measurement under application conditions
- Film property testing (gloss, adhesion, hardness, flexibility) of final coating

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: optimization
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk, optimizeBlend
- **multi_component_complexity**: multi
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook, 2nd Edition | **journal**: CRC Press | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1201/9781420006834
- **author**: Abbott, S.; Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice (HSPiP) software and eBook | **journal**: HSPiP, 5th Edition | **year**: 2013 | **doi**: N/A
- **author**: Jessop, P.G.; Jessop, D.A.; Fu, D.; Phan, L. | **title**: Solvatochromic parameters for solvents of interest in green chemistry | **journal**: Green Chemistry | **year**: 2012 | **doi**: 10.1039/C2GC16670D
- **author**: Byrne, F.P.; Jin, S.; Paggiola, G.; Petchey, T.H.M.; Clark, J.H.; Farmer, T.J.; Hunt, A.J.; McElroy, C.R.; Sherwood, J. | **title**: Tools and techniques for solvent selection: green solvent selection guides | **journal**: Sustainable Chemical Processes | **year**: 2016 | **doi**: 10.1186/s40508-016-0051-z
- **author**: Durkee, J.B. | **title**: Cleaning with Solvents: Science and Technology | **journal**: Elsevier | **year**: 2014 | **doi**: 10.1016/C2012-0-00749-9
- **first_reported_year**: 1971
- **regulatory_relevance**: HSP-based solvent substitution is directly relevant to REACH (Annex XIV authorization, SVHC substitution plans), EU VOC Directive 2004/42/EC, US EPA HAP regulations (40 CFR Part 63), California SCAQMD rules, and GHS classification. Some REACH substitution analysis reports explicitly reference HSP as a methodology for identifying suitable alternatives. The ECHA guidance on alternatives assessment acknowledges physicochemical matching approaches consistent with HSP methodology.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Automotive coatings and refinish
- Printing inks (packaging, publication, commercial)
- Adhesives and sealants
- Industrial cleaning and degreasing
- Pharmaceutical manufacturing (process solvents)
- Paint and coating manufacturing
- Electronics cleaning
- Textile processing
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice) - includes solvent optimizer module
- COSMOtherm (COSMO-RS based solvent screening)
- IBSS Solvent Database and Substitution Tool
- Eastman Solvent Selector
- BASF Navigator (solvent selection tool)
- ACS GCI Solvent Selection Guide

#### UV-Curable Ink Monomer Selection
**Japanese**: UV硬化インクモノマー選定 | **Maturity**: established

> In UV-curable ink formulation, HSP matching between the photoinitiator, oligomer, and reactive diluent (monomer) is critical for achieving a homogeneous liquid system before cure and optimal performance after cure. Good HSP compatibility ensures the photoinitiator dissolves fully for uniform cure depth, the monomer-oligomer blend has low viscosity for printability, and the cured film has appropriate adhesion and low extractable migration levels—particularly important for food-contact packaging inks.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: UV-curable inks are 100% solids systems where each component must be mutually soluble before curing. The photoinitiator must dissolve in the monomer/oligomer matrix (low Ra with the blend) to ensure uniform radical generation and complete cure depth. The reactive diluent monomer controls viscosity and must be compatible with the oligomer (low Ra) while also contributing to final film properties. After curing, unreacted species and photoinitiator fragments with high HSP affinity for the cured matrix tend to remain trapped (high residual migration risk), while those with poor affinity migrate out more readily. For food-safe inks, selecting components where cured matrix HSP is mismatched with potential migrants drives migration below detection limits.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_1 - dD_2)^2 + (dP_1 - dP_2)^2 + (dH_1 - dH_2)^2); RED_PI = Ra_PI-blend / R0_blend < 1 for photoinitiator solubility
- **red_direction**: threshold_specific
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP (dD, dP, dH) for each oligomer (e.g., epoxy acrylate, polyurethane acrylate, polyester acrylate)
- HSP for candidate reactive diluent monomers (e.g., TPGDA, HDDA, IBOA, TMPTA)
- HSP for photoinitiator(s) (e.g., TPO, Irgacure 184, Irgacure 819)
- HSP for cured film (estimated or measured)
- Interaction radius R0 for oligomer and cured film
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for acrylate monomers and oligomers
- Supplier data from photoinitiator manufacturers (IGM Resins, BASF)
- Group contribution methods for novel monomers
- Literature HSP for common UV-cure raw materials
- Experimental determination via solubility testing of photoinitiators in monomer series
- **data_availability_score**: 4
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Monomer viscosity and functionality
- Photoinitiator absorption spectrum and extinction coefficient
- UV dose and curing speed requirements
- Tg of cured film
- Migration test results (for food-contact compliance)
- Oxygen inhibition sensitivity
- Shrinkage during cure

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- HSP of cured (crosslinked) acrylate network differs from liquid monomer/oligomer HSP and is difficult to measure
- Photoinitiator solubility is also affected by crystallinity and melting point, not just HSP
- Migration behavior depends on crosslink density, film thickness, and contact conditions in addition to HSP affinity
- Reactive diluent selection involves trade-offs (viscosity, reactivity, shrinkage, toxicity) beyond HSP compatibility
- HSP do not capture the polymerization kinetics or degree of conversion
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Photoinitiator precipitation at low temperatures despite good room-temperature HSP match
- Phase separation during storage when blend is near the edge of the solubility sphere
- Unexpected migration of low-MW fragments from photoinitiator decomposition products not considered in HSP screening
- Cure inhibition in thick films where oxygen diffusion dominates over HSP-predicted compatibility
- **validation_method**: Photoinitiator dissolution testing at various concentrations and temperatures, viscosity measurements of monomer-oligomer blends, pendulum hardness and cure speed testing, migration testing per EN 1186 or FDA-specified methods, GC-MS headspace analysis for residual monomers

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: high
- **ui_pattern**: optimization
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk, optimizeBlend
- **multi_component_complexity**: multi
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- Schwalm, R. 'UV Coatings: Basics, Recent Developments and New Applications', Elsevier, 2006
- Abbott, S., Hansen, C.M. 'Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice', HSPiP, 5th ed., 2015
- Koleske, J.V. 'Radiation Curing of Coatings', ASTM International, 2002
- Lago, M.A. et al. 'Migration of photoinitiators from food packaging: a review', Food Additives & Contaminants, 2015, Vol. 32, pp. 779-798
- Hansen, C.M. 'Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook', 2nd ed., CRC Press, 2007
- **regulatory_relevance**: Highly relevant for food-contact packaging inks. EU Printing Inks Regulation (Swiss Ordinance RS 817.023.21), EuPIA Guidance, Nestle Guidance Note, and FDA 21 CFR indirect food additives all impose migration limits. HSP-based screening is used by formulators to pre-select low-migration components, though not formally cited in regulations.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: Food and beverage packaging printing, Label and flexible packaging, Commercial sheet-fed and web offset UV printing, Industrial UV coatings (wood, plastic, metal), 3D printing (UV-curable resins)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice)
- COSMOtherm (for migration prediction)
- AKTS-SML (specific migration modeling software)
- Proprietary formulation tools from UV ink manufacturers (Sun Chemical, Flint Group, Siegwerk)

**Uncertain fields**: first_reported_year, typical_red_threshold
