### Category: Computational

#### Group Contribution Method Updates
**Japanese**: 基団寄与法の更新 | **Maturity**: established

> Updated group contribution methods for predicting Hansen Solubility Parameters from molecular structure, extending the classical Hoftyzer-Van Krevelen (HVK) approach with DFT-derived fragment contributions for novel chemical structures. Group contribution methods estimate HSP by summing contributions from functional groups (fragments) in a molecule: each group contributes additive increments to the dispersion, polar, and hydrogen bonding cohesive energy components. The original HVK parameters cover common organic functional groups but lack entries for modern chemical motifs such as fluorinated groups, siloxanes, ionic liquid cations/anions, organometallic fragments, and heterocyclic structures found in pharmaceutical APIs and advanced materials. Updated parameter sets incorporate quantum chemical (DFT) calculations of fragment cohesive energies, COSMO-RS derived interaction parameters, and machine learning corrections to extend coverage to novel chemical spaces while maintaining backward compatibility with classical predictions.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Group contribution methods decompose the total cohesive energy of a molecule into additive contributions from its constituent functional groups. In the Hoftyzer-Van Krevelen framework: dD = sum(Fdi) / V, where Fdi is the molar attraction constant for group i and V is the molar volume; dP = sqrt(sum(Fpi^2)) / V, where Fpi is the polar component attraction constant; dH = sqrt(sum(Ehi) / V), where Ehi is the hydrogen bonding energy per group. The molar volume V is itself estimated by group contribution (Van Krevelen volume increments). DFT-derived updates replace empirical group parameters with quantum chemically computed values: the cohesive energy density of a molecular fragment is calculated from the difference between gas-phase and condensed-phase (COSMO or PCM solvation model) energies at the B3LYP/6-311+G(d,p) or similar level of theory. This enables parameterization for groups not covered by experimental data. Machine learning extensions use graph neural networks or descriptor-based models trained on experimental HSP data to learn nonlinear group interaction corrections that capture proximity effects, intramolecular hydrogen bonding, and conformational contributions missed by simple additive schemes.
- **key_equation**: dD = sum(Fdi) / V; dP = sqrt(sum(Fpi^2)) / V; dH = sqrt(sum(Ehi) / V); V = sum(Vi); DFT correction: Fdi(DFT) = (E_cohesive,d(fragment) * V_fragment)^(1/2) from COSMO-RS sigma-profile decomposition; ML correction: delta_HSP = f_NN(molecular_graph, group_features)
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: low

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- Molecular structure (SMILES, InChI, or structural formula) of the target compound
- Fragment decomposition scheme (functional group identification)
- Group contribution parameter table (Fdi, Fpi, Ehi, Vi for each group)
- For DFT-derived parameters: optimized fragment geometries and COSMO cavities
- For ML-corrected methods: trained model weights and molecular descriptors
- **data_sources**: 
- Van Krevelen & Te Nijenhuis 'Properties of Polymers', 4th ed. (classical HVK parameters for ~40 common groups)
- Stefanis & Panayiotou group contribution tables (extended set with ~130 first-order and ~80 second-order groups)
- DFT calculations using Gaussian, ORCA, or Turbomole with COSMO-RS (COSMOtherm) for fragment parameterization
- Experimental HSP databases (HSPiP) for training and validation of updated parameters
- PubChem and ChEMBL for molecular structures of novel compounds
- **data_availability_score**: 4
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Molar volume (experimental or estimated by group contribution)
- Molecular weight
- Density at reference temperature
- Number and type of rotatable bonds (conformational correction)
- Intramolecular hydrogen bond geometry (for correction of Ehi overestimation)
- Aromaticity index (ring current effects on dD)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- Additive group contribution assumes fragment contributions are independent of molecular context; proximity effects between groups are ignored in first-order schemes
- Intramolecular hydrogen bonding reduces the effective dH compared to the sum of group contributions, causing systematic overestimation
- Conformational effects (folded vs extended chain) affect molar volume and thus all HSP components, but are not captured by simple group addition
- DFT fragment calculations require careful choice of capping groups and solvation model, introducing method-dependent systematic errors
- Novel heterocyclic and organometallic fragments have limited experimental validation data
- Polymer repeat unit HSP estimation requires additional corrections for chain connectivity and end-group effects
- Second-order corrections (Stefanis-Panayiotou) improve accuracy but add complexity and are not available for all group combinations
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Molecules with strong intramolecular hydrogen bonding (e.g., salicylates, ortho-amino phenols) where dH is overestimated by 3-5 MPa^0.5
- Highly branched molecules where steric effects reduce effective molar volume and increase dD beyond group contribution predictions
- Zwitterionic molecules and ionic liquids where electrostatic interactions are poorly represented by fragment addition
- Large macrocyclic compounds where ring strain and cavity effects are not captured
- Compounds with significant mesomeric effects (extended conjugation) where electron delocalization affects dP beyond local group contributions
- **validation_method**: 
- Comparison of predicted HSP with experimental values from dissolution testing or IGC
- Leave-one-out cross-validation on experimental HSP databases
- Comparison with COSMO-RS computed HSP as independent computational reference
- Statistical analysis of prediction errors (RMSE, MAE) across chemical classes
- Blind prediction challenges on newly synthesized compounds

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: high
- **ui_pattern**: pairwise
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed
- **multi_component_complexity**: binary
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **regulatory_relevance**: Group contribution HSP estimation is accepted as a screening tool in REACH chemical safety assessments when experimental data is unavailable. ECHA guidance R.6 references QSAR and group contribution methods for property estimation. FDA accepts computational property estimation as supporting evidence in drug substance characterization (ICH Q6A). EMA reflection paper on the use of in silico methods references group contribution as an established computational chemistry approach.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Pharmaceutical (API HSP prediction for formulation development of novel drug candidates)
- Polymer science (prediction of HSP for new polymer structures and copolymers)
- Agrochemical (HSP estimation for novel pesticide active ingredients)
- Specialty chemicals (rapid HSP screening for novel surfactants, plasticizers, and additives)
- Materials science (HSP prediction for novel monomers, ionic liquids, and deep eutectic solvents)

**Uncertain fields**: typical_red_threshold, key_references, first_reported_year, commercial_tools

---

#### HSP Uncertainty Quantification
**Japanese**: HSP不確実性定量化 | **Maturity**: emerging

> This application addresses the propagation of experimental uncertainty through Hansen Solubility Parameter determination. When HSP values are derived from solubility sphere fitting, the underlying experimental data (good/bad solvent classifications, swelling ratios, dissolution rates) carry inherent measurement uncertainty. Bayesian inference and Monte Carlo simulation methods are used to propagate these uncertainties through the sphere fitting algorithm, yielding not point estimates but confidence regions (ellipsoids) in Hansen space. This enables quantitative risk assessment for any downstream HSP-based prediction, providing error bars on Ra distances, RED numbers, and compatibility classifications.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The core principle is that HSP sphere fitting is an inverse problem with inherent uncertainty. Each experimental data point (solvent classified as good or bad) constrains the sphere center (dD, dP, dH) and radius R0 but does not uniquely determine them. By treating solvent test outcomes as Bernoulli trials with probability related to RED, a likelihood function can be constructed. Bayesian inference with a prior on HSP space yields a posterior distribution over (dD, dP, dH, R0). Monte Carlo methods (MCMC or bootstrap resampling of the solvent test set) sample this posterior, generating an ensemble of spheres. The spread of sphere centers defines the HSP confidence region, while propagation through downstream calculations (Ra, RED, chi) gives uncertainty on any prediction. This replaces single-point HSP with probabilistic HSP, enabling decisions under uncertainty.
- **key_equation**: P(good|RED) = 1/(1+exp(k*(RED-1))); posterior ~ product_i P(outcome_i|dD,dP,dH,R0) * prior(dD,dP,dH,R0); Ra_uncertainty = sqrt(sum(partial_Ra/partial_HSPj * sigma_HSPj)^2)
- **red_direction**: threshold_specific
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: low

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- Solvent test results (good/bad/partial classification for each test solvent)
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) for each test solvent
- Number and diversity of test solvents used
- Experimental protocol details (temperature, concentration, observation time)
- Prior distribution or bounds on expected HSP range
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database (reference solvent HSP values)
- Laboratory solubility/swelling experiments
- Literature solubility data compilations
- Group contribution estimates (for prior construction)
- Inverse gas chromatography measurements
- **data_availability_score**: 4
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Number of test solvents (affects posterior width)
- Spatial distribution of test solvents in Hansen space
- Experimental repeatability data (replicate measurements)
- Solvent purity and grade information
- Observation criteria definitions (e.g., clear solution threshold)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- Assumes the Hansen sphere model is correct; systematic model error (non-spherical solubility regions) is not captured by parameter uncertainty alone
- Quality of uncertainty estimates depends heavily on the number and spatial diversity of test solvents; sparse or clustered data yield overconfident posteriors
- Binary good/bad classifications discard information compared to continuous response variables (e.g., dissolution rate)
- Prior selection can significantly influence posterior when data is sparse, introducing subjectivity
- Correlation between dD, dP, dH parameters is often ignored in simplified error propagation but can be significant
- Computational cost of full MCMC can be high for real-time applications
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- When test solvents are clustered in one region of Hansen space, the posterior is poorly constrained in orthogonal directions, yielding artificially tight confidence intervals in some directions
- Multimodal posteriors can arise when the data is consistent with multiple sphere positions, causing Gaussian approximations to fail
- Bootstrap resampling with small solvent sets (< 20) can underestimate true uncertainty
- Systematic errors in reference solvent HSP values propagate as bias that is not captured by the uncertainty analysis
- **validation_method**: 
- Cross-validation: withhold solvents and check prediction interval coverage
- Comparison of confidence intervals with repeat HSP determinations by independent labs
- Calibration checks: do 95% confidence regions contain the true value 95% of the time in simulated data
- Comparison with alternative HSP determination methods (IGC, group contribution) to check consistency within error bars

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: high
- **ui_pattern**: optimization
- **existing_modules_reusable**: 
- calculateRa
- calculateRed
- sphere fitting module (as likelihood function core)
- **multi_component_complexity**: binary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- Gharagheizi, F.; Angaji, M.T. 'A Bayesian approach for determination of Hansen solubility parameters', Journal of Molecular Liquids, 2006
- Hansen, C.M. 'Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook', 2nd Ed., CRC Press, 2007
- Vay, K.; Scheler, S.; Friess, W. 'Application of Hansen solubility parameters for understanding and prediction of drug distribution in microspheres', International Journal of Pharmaceutics, 2011
- Louwerse, M.J.; Maldonado, A.; Rousseau, S.; Moreau-Masselon, C.; Rber, B.; Thijssen, J.M. 'Revisiting Hansen Solubility Parameters by Including Thermodynamics', ChemPhysChem, 2017
- Jarvas, G.; Quellet, C.; Dallos, A. 'Estimation of Hansen solubility parameters using multivariate nonlinear QSPR modeling with COSMO screening charge density moments', Fluid Phase Equilibria, 2011

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Pharmaceutical (QbD-based solvent and excipient selection with confidence levels)
- Chemical R&D (robust solvent screening under data uncertainty)
- Coatings and adhesives (reliability-based formulation design)
- Academic research (method development and validation studies)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (provides basic sphere fitting statistics but limited uncertainty propagation)
- MATLAB/Python custom implementations (most common for full Bayesian analysis)
- COSMOtherm (offers alternative sigma-profile based uncertainty estimation)
- R statistical packages (for MCMC sampling and Bayesian inference)

**Uncertain fields**: first_reported_year, typical_red_threshold, regulatory_relevance

---

#### HSP for Ionic Liquids and DES
**Japanese**: イオン液体・深共晶溶媒のHSP | **Maturity**: emerging

> Extension of the Hansen Solubility Parameter framework to ionic liquids (ILs) and deep eutectic solvents (DES), which present unique challenges due to their unusual cohesive energy contributions. Ionic liquids are salts with melting points below 100 deg C, composed of organic cations and organic or inorganic anions, where Coulombic interactions dominate the cohesive energy density. Deep eutectic solvents are eutectic mixtures of hydrogen bond donors and acceptors (e.g., choline chloride + urea) with similarly complex interaction profiles. Determining HSP for these systems requires adaptation of measurement and calculation methods because their strong ionic/hydrogen bonding character pushes beyond the range of conventional organic solvents. HSP values for ILs and DES enable rational solvent selection for extraction, catalysis, electrochemistry, biomass processing, and as green solvent replacements in applications where their unique properties (negligible vapor pressure, wide liquid range, tunable polarity) are advantageous.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: For ionic liquids, the total cohesive energy density includes a dominant Coulombic contribution that does not map neatly onto the three Hansen parameters (dD, dP, dH). Several approaches have been proposed: (1) treating the Coulombic contribution as part of the polar component dP, yielding very high dP values (15-30 MPa^0.5); (2) introducing a fourth parameter dE (electrostatic/ionic) to separately represent the Coulombic interaction, giving a 4-parameter model delta_t^2 = dD^2 + dP^2 + dH^2 + dE^2; (3) using the Kamlet-Taft solvatochromic approach to calibrate HSP from alpha, beta, and pi* polarity descriptors. For DES, the hydrogen bonding network between the HBA (hydrogen bond acceptor, e.g., choline chloride) and HBD (hydrogen bond donor, e.g., urea, glycerol) creates a cooperative supramolecular structure where dH values are typically very high (20-40 MPa^0.5). The HSP of DES can be tuned by varying the HBA:HBD molar ratio, offering a design space not available with conventional solvents. Experimental determination commonly uses dissolution/miscibility testing with a panel of reference solvents and polymers, or IGC at elevated temperatures (ILs have high viscosity at room temperature). COSMO-RS computations provide an alternative route to IL/DES HSP by computing sigma-profiles from quantum mechanical calculations.
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- IL or DES composition (cation-anion pair for IL, HBA-HBD pair and molar ratio for DES)
- HSP values for the IL/DES (from experimental determination or COSMO-RS prediction)
- HSP values for the target solute or material
- Temperature (IL/DES properties are strongly T-dependent due to viscosity effects)
- **data_sources**: 
- Published IL HSP compilations (Marciniak 2010, Paduszynski & Domanska reviews)
- COSMO-RS computed HSP (COSMOtherm software)
- Experimental determination via dissolution/miscibility testing with solvent panels
- IGC measurements on IL-coated columns at elevated temperature
- Kamlet-Taft parameters for ILs correlated to HSP (Jessop, Kou)
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Kamlet-Taft parameters (alpha, beta, pi*) for polarity characterization
- Viscosity (strongly affects mass transfer and practical dissolution kinetics)
- Density and molar volume (needed for HSP calculation from CED)
- Water content (trace water drastically affects IL/DES properties)
- Thermal decomposition temperature (upper operating limit)
- Conductivity and electrochemical window (for electrochemical applications)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: qualitative
- **limitations**: 
- The three-parameter HSP framework was developed for molecular liquids and may be fundamentally inadequate for capturing the Coulombic interactions in ILs
- Large scatter in published IL HSP values across studies (variations of 5+ MPa^0.5 for the same IL) due to methodological differences
- The linear mixing rule for DES HSP breaks down due to strong non-ideal hydrogen bonding interactions between HBA and HBD
- High viscosity of ILs complicates experimental HSP determination by slowing dissolution kinetics (thermodynamic vs kinetic miscibility)
- IL nanostructure (polar/nonpolar domain segregation) creates microheterogeneous environments not captured by bulk HSP
- Impurities (water, halides) in ILs significantly affect measured HSP values
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- ILs with long alkyl chains (e.g., [C8mim]+) that exhibit surfactant-like behavior and micelle formation not predictable from HSP alone
- Protic ILs where proton transfer equilibria create complex speciation not represented by a single set of HSP values
- DES at extreme HBD:HBA ratios where the eutectic structure breaks down and properties change discontinuously
- Task-specific ILs with functional groups (e.g., amino-functionalized) that react with solutes rather than just solvating them
- Systems where IL-solute specific interactions (pi-pi, charge transfer) dominate over bulk solvation effects
- **validation_method**: 
- Dissolution testing of reference polymers and solutes in the IL/DES
- Liquid-liquid miscibility mapping with conventional organic solvents
- Partition coefficient measurement between IL and reference solvent
- Comparison of COSMO-RS predicted HSP with experimentally determined values
- Activity coefficient measurements at infinite dilution via GLC or headspace GC

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: high
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: 
- calculateRa (applicable but may need modified weighting for ionic systems)
- calculateRed
- classifyRisk
- sphere fitting (for experimental IL/DES HSP determination from dissolution data)
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Biomass processing (cellulose dissolution and lignocellulosic fractionation using ILs/DES)
- Pharmaceutical (API extraction, purification, and crystallization in ILs/DES)
- Electrochemistry (electrolyte design for batteries, supercapacitors, and electrodeposition)
- Chemical manufacturing (catalysis, extraction, and separation processes)
- Environmental (CO2 capture, heavy metal extraction, and waste treatment)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- COSMOtherm (COSMO-RS based HSP prediction for ILs and DES)
- HSPiP (database includes limited IL entries; sphere fitting applicable to experimental data)
- ILUAM database (University of Alabama IL database with physicochemical properties)
- Materials Studio (MD simulation for IL CED and HSP calculation)
- Sigma-Aldrich/Merck IL product catalog (provides Kamlet-Taft parameters for commercial ILs)

**Uncertain fields**: key_equation, typical_red_threshold, first_reported_year, key_references, regulatory_relevance, data_availability_score

---

#### Inverse HSP Estimation from Data
**Japanese**: データからの逆HSP推定 | **Maturity**: established

> Computational methods for determining Hansen Solubility Parameters of an unknown material by fitting an optimal HSP sphere to experimental good-solvent/bad-solvent classification data. The classic approach uses geometric optimization to find the sphere center (dD, dP, dH) and radius R0 that best separates good solvents (inside) from bad solvents (outside) in 3D Hansen space. Modern extensions include Bayesian inference methods that provide confidence regions (credible intervals) for the estimated HSP values, accounting for experimental uncertainty, limited data, and misclassified solvents. This is the foundational computational method underlying nearly all experimental HSP determination for polymers, nanomaterials, pigments, and biological materials.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The inverse problem of HSP estimation seeks to find the center coordinates (dD, dP, dH) and radius R0 of a sphere in Hansen space that optimally classifies a set of test solvents as good (inside sphere, RED < 1) or bad (outside sphere, RED > 1). The classic method minimizes misclassification by adjusting four parameters (dD, dP, dH, R0) using optimization algorithms. The original approach by Hansen used iterative manual fitting, later automated with simplex and gradient-based optimizers. The objective function typically combines misclassification count with a data-fit quality measure. Bayesian extensions model the probability of a solvent being 'good' as a function of RED through a logistic or probit link function, and use MCMC sampling or variational inference to obtain posterior distributions over (dD, dP, dH, R0), yielding credible regions that quantify uncertainty in the estimated parameters. This is critical because small experimental datasets (15-40 solvents) and subjective good/bad classification lead to significant parameter uncertainty that deterministic sphere fitting ignores.
- **key_equation**: Minimize: sum of misclassifications where RED_i < 1 for bad solvents or RED_i > 1 for good solvents; Ra_i = sqrt(4*(dD_s,i - dD)^2 + (dP_s,i - dP)^2 + (dH_s,i - dH)^2); RED_i = Ra_i / R0; Bayesian: P(good|RED) = sigmoid(a*(1 - RED) + b); posterior ~ product(P(data_i|dD,dP,dH,R0)) * prior(dD,dP,dH,R0)
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: RED = 1.0 is the classification boundary by definition; sphere fitting optimizes this boundary
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: low
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: low

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- List of test solvents with known HSP values (dD, dP, dH)
- Binary classification of each solvent as good (dissolves/disperses) or bad (does not)
- Optionally: quantitative dissolution/dispersion data (concentration, swelling ratio) for continuous fitting
- Minimum 15-20 solvents spanning Hansen space for reliable sphere fitting
- **data_sources**: 
- Experimental dissolution or dispersion testing across a solvent panel
- HSPiP database for test solvent HSP values (>1200 solvents)
- Published good/bad solvent datasets for common polymers and materials
- Swelling experiments for crosslinked polymers
- Turbidity or UV-Vis absorbance measurements for quantitative classification
- **data_availability_score**: 5
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Solvent molar volume (affects entropic contribution not captured by HSP distance)
- Temperature at which dissolution tests were conducted
- Quantitative solubility data (if available) for weighted fitting
- Polymer molecular weight (affects dissolution kinetics and threshold)
- Time allowed for dissolution (kinetic vs thermodynamic classification)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- Sphere fitting assumes the good-solvent region is spherical in Hansen space, which is an approximation; real solubility regions can be ellipsoidal or irregular
- Results are sensitive to the choice and number of test solvents; poor coverage of Hansen space leads to poorly constrained sphere parameters
- Binary good/bad classification discards quantitative information and introduces subjective threshold effects
- The 4:1:1 weighting factor in the Ra equation is assumed fixed but may not be optimal for all material types
- Classic deterministic fitting provides point estimates without uncertainty quantification
- Outliers (misclassified solvents due to kinetic effects or specific interactions) can strongly bias the fitted sphere
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Insufficient solvent coverage in one region of Hansen space causing the sphere to be poorly constrained in that direction
- Materials with non-spherical solubility regions (e.g., elongated along dH axis) where sphere model is inadequate
- Kinetically trapped states where apparent good/bad classification does not reflect thermodynamic equilibrium
- Multiple local minima in the optimization landscape leading to different solutions depending on initial guess
- Very small R0 values where few solvents fall inside the sphere, making the fit unreliable
- **validation_method**: 
- Leave-one-out cross-validation: fit sphere excluding each solvent and predict its classification
- Bootstrap resampling to estimate parameter uncertainty
- Independent validation with solvents not used in fitting
- Comparison with literature HSP values for well-characterized materials
- Bayesian posterior predictive checks

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: optimization
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, sphere fitting, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: multi
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Polymer science (HSP determination for new polymers and copolymers)
- Pharmaceutical (API and excipient HSP characterization)
- Coatings and inks (pigment and resin HSP determination)
- Nanomaterials (HSP determination for CNTs, graphene, quantum dots, MXenes)
- Chemical engineering (solvent selection based on material HSP)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (built-in sphere fitting optimization with DIY module)
- COSMOtherm (sigma-profile based HSP estimation as alternative to sphere fitting)
- Y-MB software (Hansen sphere fitting tool)
- MATLAB/Python custom implementations using scipy.optimize or Bayesian frameworks (PyMC, Stan)

**Uncertain fields**: key_references, first_reported_year, regulatory_relevance

---

#### ML HSP Prediction from Structure
**Japanese**: 構造からのML HSP予測 | **Maturity**: emerging

> Machine learning and QSPR (Quantitative Structure-Property Relationship) models that predict Hansen Solubility Parameters directly from molecular structure representations such as SMILES strings, molecular fingerprints, or 3D molecular descriptors. Approaches range from classical random forest and gradient boosting models using calculated molecular descriptors (e.g., topological, electronic, geometric features) to deep learning architectures including graph neural networks (GNN) operating on molecular graphs and transformer models processing SMILES tokens. These models are trained on experimental HSP databases (typically 500-10,000 compounds from HSPiP or curated literature) and can predict dD, dP, and dH for novel compounds without experimental measurement, enabling rapid virtual screening of candidate molecules for solvent selection, polymer design, and formulation optimization.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: ML models learn the implicit mapping from molecular structure features to HSP components by capturing structure-property relationships present in training data. The dispersion component dD correlates with molecular polarizability, molecular volume, and the presence of aromatic/aliphatic moieties. The polar component dP correlates with permanent dipole moment, charge distribution asymmetry, and electronegative atom content. The hydrogen bonding component dH correlates with hydrogen bond donor/acceptor counts, hydroxyl/amine/carboxyl group presence, and intramolecular hydrogen bonding capacity. Classical QSPR models use hand-crafted descriptors (e.g., RDKit descriptors, Dragon descriptors, MACCS keys) and learn linear or nonlinear mappings. GNN models learn atom- and bond-level representations through message-passing on molecular graphs, capturing local chemical environment effects. Transformer-based models treat SMILES as sequences and learn context-dependent token embeddings. Multi-task learning predicting all three HSP components simultaneously often improves accuracy by exploiting inter-component correlations. Transfer learning from large pre-trained molecular representations (e.g., MolBERT, ChemBERTa) further improves prediction on small HSP datasets.
- **key_equation**: HSP_predicted = f_ML(molecular_representation); Loss = sum_i(w_dD*(dD_pred - dD_exp)^2 + w_dP*(dP_pred - dP_exp)^2 + w_dH*(dH_pred - dH_exp)^2); Typical RMSE targets: dD < 1.0, dP < 1.5, dH < 2.0 MPa^0.5
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: low

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- Molecular structure (SMILES string, InChI, or molecular graph)
- Training dataset of experimentally determined HSP values (dD, dP, dH) for reference compounds
- Molecular descriptor calculations or learned representations
- Model architecture selection and hyperparameters
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database (~10,000 compounds with experimental/group-contribution HSP)
- Hansen's original solubility parameter compilations
- Published experimental HSP from dissolution/swelling/IGC measurements
- PubChem/ChEMBL for molecular structures
- RDKit or Mordred for molecular descriptor generation
- **data_availability_score**: 4
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Molar volume (often co-predicted or needed for validation)
- Boiling point (correlated with dD, useful as auxiliary training target)
- Molecular weight and density
- Experimental HSP uncertainty estimates for training data quality weighting
- 3D conformer generation for geometry-dependent descriptors

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- Training data is biased toward common organic solvents; predictions degrade for compound classes underrepresented in training (e.g., organometallics, salts, large biomolecules)
- dH prediction is consistently the least accurate component due to the complexity of hydrogen bonding patterns and cooperative effects not fully captured by structure alone
- Models trained on room-temperature HSP data cannot predict temperature dependence without explicit T-dependent training data
- SMILES-based models may not distinguish stereoisomers or conformational effects adequately
- Applicability domain is limited; extrapolation outside the chemical space of the training set is unreliable
- Group contribution methods (Hoftyzer-Van Krevelen, Hoy) serve as strong baselines that ML only modestly outperforms for well-represented compound classes
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Polymers and macromolecules where HSP depends on chain conformation and molecular weight, not just repeat unit structure
- Ionic compounds and salts where Coulombic interactions dominate beyond the HSP framework
- Molecules with strong intramolecular hydrogen bonding (e.g., salicylaldehyde) where structural descriptors overpredict dH
- Highly flexible molecules where multiple conformers contribute differently to HSP
- Novel heterocyclic scaffolds or functional group combinations absent from training data
- **validation_method**: 
- k-fold cross-validation (typically 5-fold or 10-fold) on the training dataset
- External test set validation with held-out compounds
- Comparison against group contribution method predictions
- Experimental validation via dissolution testing or IGC for selected predictions
- Applicability domain analysis using descriptor space coverage metrics

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: high
- **ui_pattern**: optimization
- **existing_modules_reusable**: 
- calculateRa (for downstream use of predicted HSP)
- calculateRed (for downstream screening with predicted values)
- classifyRisk (for confidence-based risk labeling)
- **multi_component_complexity**: binary
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Pharmaceutical (virtual screening of solvents for API crystallization and formulation)
- Chemical manufacturing (solvent replacement and green chemistry initiatives)
- Materials science (polymer-solvent compatibility prediction for novel materials)
- Agrochemical (formulation solvent screening for new active ingredients)
- Coatings and adhesives (rapid screening of novel resin-solvent combinations)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (includes group contribution and Y-MB method for HSP estimation)
- COSMOtherm (sigma-profile based, not strictly ML but computational prediction)
- ACD/Labs Percepta (QSPR-based physicochemical property prediction)
- Schrödinger Materials Science Suite (ML-based molecular property prediction)
- RDKit + scikit-learn / PyTorch Geometric (open-source ML pipeline for custom models)

**Uncertain fields**: typical_red_threshold, first_reported_year, key_references, regulatory_relevance

---

#### Molecular Dynamics HSP Calculation
**Japanese**: 分子動力学によるHSP計算 | **Maturity**: established

> All-atom molecular dynamics (MD) simulation is used to compute Hansen Solubility Parameters from first principles by calculating the cohesive energy density (CED) of a liquid and decomposing it into dispersion, polar, and hydrogen bonding contributions. A simulation box of molecules is equilibrated at the target temperature and pressure, and the total intermolecular interaction energy is computed by comparing the potential energy of the condensed phase to the gas phase (isolated molecule). The three HSP components are obtained by partitioning the pairwise interaction energies into van der Waals (Lennard-Jones), electrostatic (Coulombic), and hydrogen bonding terms. This approach provides HSP values for compounds where experimental data is unavailable, including novel molecules, reactive intermediates, polymers, and materials under non-ambient conditions of temperature and pressure.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The total solubility parameter delta_t is defined as the square root of the cohesive energy density: delta_t = sqrt(E_coh/V_m), where E_coh is the molar cohesive energy (energy required to separate molecules from the liquid to the gas phase) and V_m is the molar volume. In MD simulation, E_coh is calculated as the difference between the intramolecular energy of isolated gas-phase molecules and the total potential energy per molecule in the liquid state. The HSP decomposition is achieved by partitioning the intermolecular potential energy into components: E_vdW (van der Waals/Lennard-Jones interactions) maps to dD^2, E_elec (electrostatic/Coulombic interactions excluding H-bonds) maps to dP^2, and E_hbond (hydrogen bond interactions) maps to dH^2, such that delta_t^2 = dD^2 + dP^2 + dH^2. The hydrogen bonding energy can be separated from total electrostatic energy using geometric or energetic criteria for hydrogen bond identification. Force field selection (OPLS-AA, COMPASS, GAFF, CHARMM) critically affects accuracy. Simulations typically use NPT ensemble at 298 K and 1 atm with periodic boundary conditions and 200-1000 molecules for adequate statistics.
- **key_equation**: delta_t^2 = E_coh/V_m = (E_gas - E_liquid)/V_m; dD^2 = -E_vdW/V_m; dP^2 = -E_elec(non-HB)/V_m; dH^2 = -E_hbond/V_m; E_coh = E_vdW + E_elec + E_hbond
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: high

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- Molecular structure and force field parameters for the compound
- Force field selection (OPLS-AA, COMPASS, GAFF, TraPPE, etc.)
- Simulation conditions: temperature, pressure, number of molecules, box size
- Equilibration and production run time parameters
- Hydrogen bond definition criteria (geometric cutoffs for donor-acceptor distance and angle)
- **data_sources**: 
- Force field parameter databases (OPLS-AA, AMBER/GAFF, CHARMM General Force Field)
- Automated parameterization tools (LigParGen, ACPYPE, CGenFF)
- Quantum mechanical calculations for charge assignment (RESP, CM5, AM1-BCC)
- Experimental density and heat of vaporization data for force field validation
- NIST ThermoData Engine for reference thermophysical properties
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Experimental density (for force field validation)
- Heat of vaporization (for CED validation)
- Dielectric constant (for electrostatic component validation)
- Radial distribution functions (for liquid structure validation)
- Diffusion coefficient (for dynamics validation)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- Force field accuracy is the primary bottleneck; different force fields can give dH values differing by 3-5 MPa^0.5 for the same molecule
- Partitioning electrostatic energy into polar and hydrogen bonding components is ambiguous and method-dependent
- Long-range electrostatic corrections (Ewald summation vs cutoff) significantly affect dP values
- Simulation convergence requires long runs (5-20 ns production) for reliable energy statistics, especially for viscous or strongly hydrogen-bonded liquids
- Quantum effects (proton tunneling, charge transfer, polarization) are absent in classical force fields
- Polymers require special treatment due to chain connectivity and entanglement effects
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Highly polar aprotic solvents (DMSO, DMF) where force fields often underestimate dP
- Water and highly associated liquids where cooperative hydrogen bonding networks are poorly captured by pairwise additive force fields
- Fluorinated compounds where standard force fields lack accurate fluorine parameters
- Multifunctional molecules (amino acids, sugars) with complex intramolecular hydrogen bonding that reduces effective dH
- Ionic liquids where the CED decomposition does not straightforwardly apply due to Coulombic contributions
- **validation_method**: 
- Comparison of predicted dD, dP, dH against experimental HSP from HSPiP database
- Verification of simulated liquid density against experimental values (within 2-5%)
- Comparison of computed heat of vaporization with experimental data
- Convergence analysis: block averaging to estimate statistical uncertainty
- Cross-validation against group contribution estimates

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: high
- **ui_pattern**: pairwise
- **existing_modules_reusable**: 
- calculateRa (for using MD-derived HSP in downstream applications)
- calculateRed (for downstream screening)
- **multi_component_complexity**: binary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **first_reported_year**: 2004

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Pharmaceutical (HSP prediction for novel API candidates before synthesis)
- Polymer science (compatibility prediction for new polymer blends and composites)
- Energy materials (electrolyte-electrode compatibility for batteries)
- Chemical engineering (solvent design and process optimization)
- Coatings (HSP estimation for novel resin chemistries)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- Materials Studio (Accelrys/BIOVIA - includes Amorphous Cell and Forcite for CED calculation)
- GROMACS (open-source MD engine, widely used for HSP calculation with custom scripts)
- LAMMPS (open-source, flexible MD code with energy decomposition capabilities)
- Schrödinger Desmond (high-performance MD with automated CED workflow)
- Amsterdam Modeling Suite (AMS/ReaxFF for reactive systems HSP calculation)

**Uncertain fields**: typical_red_threshold, key_references, regulatory_relevance

---

#### Multi-component Solvent Optimization
**Japanese**: 多成分溶媒最適化 | **Maturity**: established

> Computational optimization of solvent blends containing four or more components to achieve a target HSP value, using gradient-free or evolutionary algorithms. The HSP of a mixture is calculated as the volume-weighted average of individual solvent HSPs, creating a continuous multidimensional optimization problem: find the composition (volume fractions) of N solvents from a candidate pool that minimizes the distance between the blend HSP and a target point in Hansen space, subject to constraints on cost, toxicity, volatility, viscosity, and regulatory compliance. For 4+ components, the combinatorial explosion and nonlinear constraints make exhaustive search impractical, requiring metaheuristic approaches such as genetic algorithms, particle swarm optimization, differential evolution, or Bayesian optimization. This enables green solvent replacement, regulatory-compliant formulation redesign, and cost optimization while maintaining dissolution or compatibility performance.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The HSP of a solvent mixture is approximated as the volume-fraction-weighted linear combination of individual component HSPs: dD_mix = sum(phi_i * dD_i), dP_mix = sum(phi_i * dP_i), dH_mix = sum(phi_i * dH_i), where phi_i is the volume fraction of solvent i. This linear mixing rule, while approximate, works well for most practical formulations. The optimization objective is to minimize Ra between the blend HSP and a target HSP (typically the material to be dissolved or the solvent being replaced), subject to sum(phi_i) = 1, phi_i >= 0, and additional practical constraints. For 4+ component systems, the feasible composition space is a high-dimensional simplex, and the constraint surface may be non-convex when practical constraints (cost, EHS rating, flash point, evaporation rate) are included. Gradient-free algorithms are preferred because: (1) the objective landscape may have multiple local minima when discrete solvent selection is combined with continuous composition optimization, (2) constraints may be discontinuous (e.g., regulatory banned solvent lists), and (3) the solvent candidate pool is discrete while compositions are continuous, creating a mixed-integer optimization problem.
- **key_equation**: dD_mix = sum(phi_i * dD_i); dP_mix = sum(phi_i * dP_i); dH_mix = sum(phi_i * dH_i); Minimize Ra_target = sqrt(4*(dD_mix - dD_target)^2 + (dP_mix - dP_target)^2 + (dH_mix - dH_target)^2); subject to: sum(phi_i) = 1, phi_i >= 0, and EHS/cost/physical property constraints
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: Ra_target < 1.0 MPa^0.5 for excellent match; Ra_target < R0_material for functional match; minimize RED = Ra/R0
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- Target HSP values (dD, dP, dH) to be matched (material HSP or solvent to be replaced)
- Candidate solvent pool with HSP values (dD, dP, dH) for each
- Volume fraction bounds for each solvent (min/max allowed)
- Number of components (N >= 4)
- Constraint specifications: cost limits, EHS ratings, flash point, evaporation rate, viscosity, regulatory restrictions
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database (>1200 solvents with full HSP and physical property data)
- REACH SVHC and candidate list for regulatory exclusions
- Solvent supplier databases for cost and availability data
- GHS safety data sheets for EHS constraint parameters
- Literature or experimental HSP values for the target material
- **data_availability_score**: 5
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Solvent cost per liter
- Environmental, health, and safety (EHS) scores
- Flash point and autoignition temperature
- Evaporation rate (relative to butyl acetate)
- Viscosity of the blend
- VOC classification and vapor pressure
- Boiling point range for the blend
- Kauri-Butanol (KB) value for solvency power validation

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- Linear mixing rule for HSP is an approximation that breaks down for highly non-ideal mixtures (strong hydrogen bonding, association, or phase separation)
- Activity coefficient effects in non-ideal mixtures cause the effective HSP to deviate from the volume-weighted average
- Optimization finds mathematically optimal compositions that may not be practically realizable (e.g., requiring precise four-decimal volume fractions)
- Physical property constraints (viscosity, evaporation rate) require additional mixing rules that may not be as reliable as HSP mixing
- The discrete-continuous nature (selecting solvents AND optimizing composition) creates NP-hard combinatorial challenges for large candidate pools
- Azeotrope formation in multicomponent blends affects evaporation behavior unpredictably
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Solvent blends with strong hydrogen bonding interactions (e.g., alcohol-amine mixtures) where nonlinear HSP mixing occurs
- Phase-separated blends where the effective HSP is undefined (immiscible solvent combinations)
- Blend compositions optimized for HSP match but practically unusable due to stability, stratification, or incompatibility issues
- Over-constrained problems with many practical constraints that eliminate all feasible solutions
- Blends near azeotrope compositions where evaporation changes the composition and effective HSP over time
- **validation_method**: 
- Experimental dissolution or compatibility testing of the optimized blend
- Refractive index or density measurement to verify actual blend composition
- GC-MS headspace analysis to verify no preferential evaporation has changed composition
- Comparison with known good-performing solvent blend as positive control
- Accelerated aging test to verify blend stability over time

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: high
- **ui_pattern**: optimization
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, optimizeBlend
- **multi_component_complexity**: multi
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **regulatory_relevance**: Multi-component solvent optimization is directly relevant to REACH solvent substitution requirements (Annex XVII restrictions, SVHC replacement). EU Ecolabel criteria for paints and coatings reference VOC limits achievable through optimized solvent blends. FDA 21 CFR 173 lists residual solvent classes that constrain pharmaceutical solvent optimization.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Coatings and paints (reformulation for VOC reduction and regulatory compliance)
- Pharmaceutical (ICH Q3C compliant solvent blend design)
- Printing inks (ink-jet and flexographic solvent optimization)
- Cleaning (industrial parts cleaning solvent replacement)
- Adhesives (contact adhesive solvent blend formulation)
- Chemical manufacturing (extraction and crystallization solvent design)

**Uncertain fields**: key_references, first_reported_year, commercial_tools

---

#### Pressure-dependent HSP Correction
**Japanese**: 圧力依存HSP補正 | **Maturity**: emerging

> Correction of Hansen Solubility Parameters for pressure effects, essential for applications involving supercritical fluid extraction (particularly supercritical CO2), high-pressure processing, and deep-sea/subsurface material compatibility. At elevated pressures, liquid density increases, intermolecular distances decrease, and cohesive energy density changes, all of which shift HSP values. For supercritical fluids like CO2, the HSP varies dramatically with pressure and temperature as the fluid transitions from gas-like to liquid-like densities. The ability to calculate pressure-dependent HSP enables optimization of supercritical extraction conditions, prediction of high-pressure polymer processing behavior, and assessment of material compatibility under extreme conditions encountered in oil and gas, deep-sea, and aerospace applications.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: HSP values are defined at standard conditions (25 deg C, 1 atm) but change with pressure through density-dependent and equation-of-state effects. The primary mechanism is that increased pressure compresses the liquid, reducing molar volume V_m and increasing cohesive energy density CED = E_coh/V_m, thereby increasing delta_t. For supercritical CO2, the solubility parameter can be approximated from the reduced density using the Giddings correlation: delta = 1.25*P_c^0.5 * (rho_r / rho_r_liq), where rho_r is the reduced density at the operating conditions and rho_r_liq is the reduced density of the saturated liquid. The three HSP components respond differently to pressure: dD increases approximately linearly with density through enhanced London dispersion interactions; dP changes modestly for non-polar fluids but significantly for polar fluids where compression brings dipoles closer; dH can either increase (enhanced hydrogen bond density) or decrease (disruption of hydrogen bond networks at very high pressures). For supercritical CO2, dD dominates (no permanent dipole), ranging from near zero at low pressures to ~15 MPa^0.5 at very high pressures (400+ bar), with small dP (~3-6) and dH (~3-6) contributed by the quadrupole moment and Lewis acid character. Co-solvents (entrainers) like ethanol or methanol added to scCO2 shift the effective mixture HSP into regions with higher dP and dH, enabling dissolution of more polar solutes.
- **key_equation**: Giddings: delta_t(P,T) = 1.25*P_c^0.5 * rho_r(P,T)/rho_r_liq; General: delta_t(P) = delta_t(P0) * (V_m(P0)/V_m(P))^0.5; scCO2 HSP: dD = f(rho), dP ~ 3-6 MPa^0.5 (quadrupole), dH ~ 3-6 MPa^0.5 (Lewis acid); Mixture with co-solvent: HSP_mix = phi_CO2*HSP_CO2(P,T) + phi_cosolv*HSP_cosolv
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: high

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP values at reference conditions (25 deg C, 1 atm) for the solute/material
- Operating pressure and temperature
- Equation of state parameters or density data for the solvent at operating conditions
- Critical properties (Pc, Tc, Vc) for supercritical fluid applications
- Co-solvent type and concentration (for modified scCO2)
- **data_sources**: 
- NIST WebBook for CO2 density at arbitrary P and T
- Peng-Robinson or Soave-Redlich-Kwong equation of state for PVT data
- HSPiP database for reference-condition HSP values
- Published scCO2 HSP correlations (Williams 2004, Gupta & Shim 2006)
- REFPROP (NIST) for accurate thermophysical properties of CO2 and co-solvents
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Fluid density at operating conditions (from EOS or NIST tables)
- Compressibility factor Z
- Viscosity at high pressure (for mass transfer kinetics)
- Diffusion coefficient at operating conditions
- Solute vapor pressure (for extraction selectivity)
- Co-solvent-solute specific interactions (not captured by HSP alone)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- The Giddings correlation for scCO2 HSP gives only the total solubility parameter; decomposition into dD, dP, dH requires additional assumptions or models
- HSP decomposition of supercritical fluids is ambiguous because the quadrupole moment of CO2 contributes to both dP and dH in ways that are not clearly separable
- Pressure corrections assume simple density scaling, which breaks down near critical points where fluctuations are large
- Co-solvent effects involve specific interactions (e.g., ethanol-CO2 clustering) that deviate from ideal volume-fraction mixing rules
- Entrainer effects are often larger than predicted by simple HSP mixing, suggesting specific solute-cosolvent interactions beyond HSP
- Very high pressures (>1000 bar) may cause phase transitions or structural changes in materials not predicted by HSP shifts alone
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Near-critical region where small P or T changes cause dramatic density fluctuations and HSP discontinuities
- Polar solutes in pure scCO2 where the low dP and dH of CO2 cannot be overcome even at very high pressures
- Retrograde solubility behavior (solubility decreasing with pressure at certain T) not predicted by monotonic HSP-pressure relationship
- Multi-solute systems where selectivity depends on kinetic effects (diffusion, film resistance) not captured by equilibrium HSP
- Polymer-scCO2 systems where CO2-induced plasticization changes the polymer HSP simultaneously
- **validation_method**: 
- Measurement of solute solubility in scCO2 at multiple P and T conditions
- Cloud point determination for polymer-scCO2 systems
- Comparison of predicted optimal extraction pressure with experimental extraction yield curves
- Correlation of scCO2 HSP with experimentally measured Hildebrand parameter via density
- Co-solvent screening validation through extraction efficiency measurements

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: optimization
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa (applied at each P,T point), calculateRed (with P,T-corrected HSP), classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **first_reported_year**: 1968

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Food and beverage (scCO2 decaffeination, hop extraction, essential oil extraction)
- Pharmaceutical (scCO2 particle formation, API purification, residual solvent removal)
- Cosmetics (natural ingredient extraction using scCO2)
- Oil and gas (high-pressure material compatibility, polymer seal selection for downhole conditions)
- Polymer processing (supercritical fluid impregnation, foaming, and particle formation)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (includes scCO2 HSP calculator with P and T input)
- Aspen Plus / Aspen HYSYS (process simulation with EOS-based solubility parameter calculation)
- REFPROP (NIST reference fluid properties for accurate CO2 PVT data)
- ProSim Ternary Diagram (phase equilibrium visualization for scCO2 systems)
- COSMOtherm (COSMO-RS predictions applicable to high-pressure conditions with pressure correction)

**Uncertain fields**: typical_red_threshold, key_references, regulatory_relevance

---

#### Surface HSP Determination Methods
**Japanese**: 表面HSP決定法 | **Maturity**: established

> Methods for determining Hansen Solubility Parameters of solid surfaces, particles, and fibers, which cannot be dissolved to apply traditional dissolution testing. The primary techniques include inverse gas chromatography (IGC), where probe molecules with known HSP are passed through a column packed with the solid material and retention volumes are used to calculate surface energy components; contact angle series, where a set of liquids with known HSP are used to measure advancing contact angles on a flat surface, and the Hansen sphere is fitted to the wetting/non-wetting classification; and atomic force microscopy (AFM) methods, where adhesion force measurements between a probe and the surface in various liquid media allow surface HSP determination. These methods are essential inputs for applications involving pigment dispersion, filler-matrix compatibility, nanoparticle surface modification, fiber-resin adhesion, and any process requiring knowledge of solid surface HSP values.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Surface HSP reflects the interaction potential of a solid surface with surrounding molecules, analogous to bulk HSP for liquids. In IGC, the net retention volume V_N of a series of probe solvents is related to the free energy of adsorption: delta_G_ads = -RT*ln(V_N). By using probes spanning Hansen space (alkanes for dD, polar probes for dP, hydrogen bonding probes for dH), the surface energy components gamma_d, gamma_p, gamma_h are determined and converted to HSP-like parameters. In contact angle methods, Young's equation relates the contact angle theta to surface energies: gamma_LV*cos(theta) = gamma_SV - gamma_SL, and the Owens-Wendt or acid-base (van Oss-Chaudhury-Good) models decompose the work of adhesion into dispersion and polar/acid-base contributions. A panel of test liquids (typically 10-30) with known HSP is classified as wetting (theta < 90 deg) or non-wetting (theta > 90 deg), and a Hansen sphere is fitted. In AFM colloidal probe methods, adhesion forces F_adh measured in different liquid environments allow extraction of surface HSP via F_adh proportional to W_adhesion = f(delta_surface, delta_liquid, delta_probe). The surface HSP determined by these methods often differs from bulk HSP due to surface reconstruction, contamination, roughness effects, and preferential orientation of functional groups at the interface.
- **key_equation**: IGC: delta_G_ads = -RT*ln(V_N*P_s/(S*pi_0*T_col); Contact angle: gamma_SL = gamma_S + gamma_L - 2*(sqrt(gamma_S_d*gamma_L_d) + sqrt(gamma_S_p*gamma_L_p) + sqrt(gamma_S_h*gamma_L_h)); Sphere fitting: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_s-dD_l)^2 + (dP_s-dP_l)^2 + (dH_s-dH_l)^2), classify wetting/non-wetting to fit sphere center and R0
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: RED < 1.0 for wetting/good interaction between liquid and surface; contact angle < 90 deg generally corresponds to RED < 1.0 in the fitted surface sphere
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- Set of probe liquids/solvents with known HSP values (minimum 15-30 for reliable sphere fitting)
- Solid sample in appropriate form (powder for IGC, flat surface for contact angle, particle for AFM)
- Contact angle or retention volume or adhesion force measurements for each probe
- Liquid surface tension values for contact angle methods
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for probe solvent HSP values
- Surface Measurement Systems (SMS) IGC-SEA instrument application notes
- ASTM D5946 (contact angle measurement standard)
- Published surface HSP compilations for common pigments, polymers, and fillers
- Van Oss acid-base surface energy component tables
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- BET surface area (for IGC normalization)
- Surface roughness (Ra, Rq) for contact angle correction (Wenzel/Cassie-Baxter)
- Surface composition from XPS or ToF-SIMS
- Particle size and morphology
- Zeta potential (for charged surface effects)
- Surface treatment/modification history

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- Surface HSP may differ from bulk HSP due to surface reconstruction, preferential orientation of functional groups, and contamination
- Contact angle measurements on rough surfaces require Wenzel or Cassie-Baxter corrections that introduce additional uncertainty
- IGC probes interact with the highest energy sites first, leading to surface heterogeneity effects and energy distribution rather than single HSP values
- The concept of assigning three scalar HSP values to an inherently anisotropic surface is a simplification
- Different methods (IGC, contact angle, AFM) often yield different HSP values for the same surface due to different interaction scales and sensitivities
- Humidity and surface contamination significantly affect results, requiring strict environmental control
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Porous surfaces where liquid penetration during contact angle measurement violates the Young equation assumptions
- Chemically heterogeneous surfaces (e.g., partially treated particles) where a single HSP sphere cannot represent the dual character
- Very hydrophobic surfaces (e.g., PTFE, silicone) where limited wetting with polar probes makes dP and dH determination unreliable
- Surfaces with time-dependent restructuring (e.g., plasma-treated polymers that undergo hydrophobic recovery)
- Swelling surfaces where probe liquid absorption changes the surface character during measurement
- **validation_method**: 
- Cross-validation using multiple measurement techniques (IGC + contact angle) on the same material
- Prediction of dispersion stability in untested solvents using determined surface HSP
- Correlation with practical performance (e.g., adhesion strength, dispersion quality)
- Round-robin testing between laboratories
- Comparison of IGC results at different surface coverages to assess heterogeneity effects

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: high
- **ui_pattern**: optimization
- **existing_modules_reusable**: 
- calculateRa
- calculateRed
- classifyRisk
- sphere fitting (core algorithm directly applicable to contact angle data classification)
- **multi_component_complexity**: binary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Coatings and inks (pigment and filler surface HSP for dispersion optimization)
- Composites (fiber-matrix interface characterization for adhesion prediction)
- Pharmaceutical (excipient surface characterization for powder processing and formulation)
- Nanomaterials (nanoparticle surface modification verification and solvent selection)
- Adhesives (substrate surface characterization for adhesive selection)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- Surface Measurement Systems IGC-SEA (automated inverse gas chromatography with HSP output)
- HSPiP (sphere fitting algorithm applicable to contact angle or dispersion test data)
- Krüss Drop Shape Analyzer (contact angle measurement with surface energy decomposition)
- Dataphysics OCA (contact angle systems with acid-base surface energy models)
- Bruker AFM systems (colloidal probe force spectroscopy for adhesion mapping)

**Uncertain fields**: first_reported_year, key_references, regulatory_relevance

---

#### Temperature-dependent HSP Correction
**Japanese**: 温度依存HSP補正 | **Maturity**: established

> Methods for estimating Hansen Solubility Parameters at arbitrary temperatures using thermal expansion coefficients and thermodynamic relationships. The Barton method and its extensions correct room-temperature HSP values for temperature effects by accounting for volume changes (thermal expansion), reduced intermolecular interactions at elevated temperatures, and changes in hydrogen bonding association equilibria. The dispersion parameter (dD) decreases with temperature due to volume expansion following a -1.25 power law with molar volume. The polar parameter (dP) decreases similarly but less steeply. The hydrogen bonding parameter (dH) shows the most complex behavior, decreasing significantly at high temperatures due to disruption of hydrogen bond networks. These corrections are essential for predicting solubility, compatibility, and processing behavior at temperatures far from the standard 25C reference, such as in melt processing, high-temperature coating application, cryogenic storage, and supercritical fluid applications.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: HSP values are defined at 25C reference conditions. At different temperatures, the molar volume changes due to thermal expansion, which directly affects the cohesive energy density and thus all three HSP components. The Barton method uses the coefficient of thermal expansion (alpha) to estimate volume change: V(T) = V(25C) * exp(alpha * (T - 25)). The dispersion parameter follows: dD(T) = dD(25) * (V(25)/V(T))^1.25, derived from London dispersion force theory where the interaction energy scales with the -6 power of intermolecular distance. The polar parameter follows: dP(T) = dP(25) * (V(25)/V(T))^0.5, reflecting the weaker volume dependence of dipole-dipole interactions. The hydrogen bonding parameter has a more complex temperature dependence because hydrogen bonds break progressively with increasing temperature: dH(T) = dH(25) * exp(-alpha_H * (T - 25)), where alpha_H is an empirical parameter related to the hydrogen bond dissociation enthalpy. For solvents with strong self-association (water, alcohols, carboxylic acids), the temperature dependence of dH is particularly steep due to cooperative hydrogen bond network disruption. Extensions include group contribution corrections for specific functional groups and Clausius-Clapeyron type corrections near phase transitions.
- **key_equation**: V(T) = V(25) * exp(alpha*(T-25)); dD(T) = dD(25) * (V(25)/V(T))^1.25; dP(T) = dP(25) * (V(25)/V(T))^0.5; dH(T) = dH(25) * exp(-1.22e-3*(T-25)) [approximate for non-associated liquids]; For associated liquids: dH(T)^2 = dH(25)^2 - (Delta_H_assoc/V) * (1 - T/T_ref)
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: Same as underlying application (RED < 1.0 for solubility); temperature correction shifts the threshold by modifying HSP values
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: high

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) at reference temperature (typically 25C)
- Molar volume at reference temperature
- Coefficient of thermal expansion (alpha) for each component
- Target temperature for correction
- Hydrogen bond association enthalpy (for strongly associated liquids)
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for 25C reference HSP values and molar volumes
- CRC Handbook of Chemistry and Physics for thermal expansion coefficients
- NIST WebBook for thermodynamic properties
- Literature values for hydrogen bond dissociation enthalpies
- Group contribution estimation when experimental alpha is unavailable
- **data_availability_score**: 4
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Boiling point and critical temperature (upper limit of liquid-phase correction)
- Heat capacity (Cp) for enthalpy calculations
- Vapor pressure curve (for evaporation considerations at elevated T)
- Glass transition temperature Tg (for polymer HSP temperature dependence)
- Association equilibrium constants for strongly hydrogen-bonding solvents

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- The power-law exponents (1.25 for dD, 0.5 for dP) are theoretical approximations derived from simplified intermolecular force models
- Hydrogen bonding temperature dependence is highly system-specific and the exponential approximation is crude for strongly associated liquids
- Near critical points and phase transitions, the continuous thermal expansion model breaks down
- For polymers, the temperature dependence differs above and below Tg due to different thermal expansion coefficients in glassy vs rubbery states
- The method assumes ideal gas thermal expansion; real liquid behavior at high pressures requires equation-of-state corrections
- No reliable correction exists for temperatures below the freezing point of the solvent
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Water and highly associated liquids where cooperative hydrogen bond networks create non-monotonic temperature dependence of dH
- Temperatures near the boiling point where vapor-liquid equilibrium effects dominate
- Supercooled or glassy states where thermal expansion coefficients are discontinuous
- Binary or multicomponent systems where mixing HSP at elevated temperatures requires composition-dependent corrections
- Ionic liquids where electrostatic interactions have different temperature dependence than captured by HSP
- **validation_method**: 
- Inverse gas chromatography (IGC) at multiple temperatures for experimental HSP determination
- Solubility measurements at multiple temperatures compared with predictions
- PVT (pressure-volume-temperature) measurements for thermal expansion verification
- DSC for phase transition identification affecting correction validity
- Comparison with COSMO-RS predicted temperature dependence

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: pairwise
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: binary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Polymer processing (melt compounding, extrusion, and injection molding at 150-350C)
- Coatings (high-temperature bake coatings and powder coating applications)
- Pharmaceutical (body-temperature dissolution and controlled release prediction)
- Oil and gas (downhole chemical compatibility at elevated temperatures)
- Food processing (hot-fill packaging compatibility assessment)
- Cryogenics (low-temperature seal and gasket material compatibility)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (built-in temperature correction using Barton method with user-adjustable parameters)
- COSMOtherm (COSMO-RS based temperature-dependent sigma profiles for rigorous T correction)
- Aspen Plus (equation-of-state based solubility parameter estimation at arbitrary T and P)
- Materials Studio (molecular dynamics for temperature-dependent cohesive energy density)

**Uncertain fields**: key_references, first_reported_year, regulatory_relevance

---

### Category: Electronics/Advanced

#### CNT/Graphene Dispersion Solvent
**Japanese**: CNT/グラフェン分散溶媒 | **Maturity**: established

> HSP sphere determination for carbon nanomaterials (carbon nanotubes, graphene, graphene oxide, reduced graphene oxide) enables rational solvent selection for stable dispersions. The HSP of the nanomaterial is determined by dispersing it in a panel of solvents and fitting a Hansen solubility sphere to the good/bad solvent classification. Solvents within the HSP sphere (RED < 1) produce stable dispersions, while those outside yield aggregation and sedimentation. This approach has become the standard method for solvent screening in carbon nanomaterial processing for composites, conductive inks, thin films, and energy storage applications. The method accounts for the balance of van der Waals attraction between graphitic surfaces and solvation by matching solvent-nanomaterial interaction energies.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Carbon nanomaterials possess characteristic HSP values reflecting their surface chemistry: pristine CNTs and graphene are dominated by dispersion forces (dD ~ 17.8 MPa^0.5) with modest polar and hydrogen bonding components (dP ~ 7.0, dH ~ 7.6), while graphene oxide has significantly higher dP and dH due to oxygen-containing functional groups. The HSP sphere is determined experimentally by testing dispersion stability in 30-40 solvents covering Hansen space, then fitting the sphere center (material HSP) and radius R0 using optimization algorithms. A solvent produces a stable dispersion when its HSP falls within the sphere (RED < 1), meaning the solvent-nanomaterial interaction energy is sufficient to overcome the strong van der Waals attraction between graphitic surfaces (pi-pi stacking). The enthalpy of mixing scales with (delta_solvent - delta_nanomaterial)^2, and stable dispersions require this to be minimized. Surface functionalization shifts the sphere center predictably in Hansen space, enabling design of functionalization strategies for target solvents or polymer matrices.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_solv - dD_CNM)^2 + (dP_solv - dP_CNM)^2 + (dH_solv - dH_CNM)^2); RED = Ra/R0; Dispersion concentration correlates with exp(-Ra^2/k)
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: low

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) and R0 of the carbon nanomaterial (from sphere fitting)
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) of candidate solvents
- Desired dispersion concentration and stability duration
- Nanomaterial type and surface functionalization state
- **data_sources**: 
- Experimental dispersion testing in solvent panels (primary method)
- HSPiP database for solvent HSP values
- Published CNT/graphene HSP values (Bergin 2009, Hernandez 2010, Hansen 2007)
- Computational estimation via surface energy approaches
- Literature compilations of graphene oxide and rGO HSP values
- **data_availability_score**: 2
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Solvent boiling point and vapor pressure (for processing)
- Solvent viscosity (affects dispersion kinetics and sedimentation)
- Surface tension of the solvent (Hildebrand approach complement)
- Nanomaterial aspect ratio, number of layers, and defect density
- Ultrasonication energy input and protocol
- Surfactant/dispersant type and concentration if applicable

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- HSP sphere for nanomaterials shows significant variability between studies due to differences in material quality, defect density, and functionalization degree
- Dispersion stability is kinetic, not purely thermodynamic; HSP predicts thermodynamic affinity but not long-term colloidal stability against reaggregation
- The 4:1:1 weighting factor (4 for dD) in the Ra equation was derived for molecular solutions and may not be optimal for nanomaterial surfaces
- R0 values for nanomaterials are sample-dependent and vary with preparation method, tube diameter, layer number, and oxidation degree
- High surface area and strong pi-pi interactions create energy barriers not fully captured by mean-field HSP approach
- Solvent-nanomaterial charge transfer interactions (e.g., with NMP, DMF) contribute beyond what HSP represents
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- NMP and DMF give better dispersions than HSP distance alone predicts, likely due to electron donor-acceptor interactions with the graphitic surface
- Water-based dispersions with surfactants where the effective HSP is that of the surfactant-coated nanomaterial, not the bare surface
- Solvent mixtures where synergistic cosolvency effects move the effective HSP inside the sphere unexpectedly
- Heavily oxidized graphene oxide (GO) where ionic/electrostatic interactions dominate over HSP-type solvation
- **validation_method**: 
- UV-Vis spectroscopy of supernatant after centrifugation (Beer-Lambert quantification)
- Dynamic light scattering (DLS) for particle size and stability monitoring
- Zeta potential measurement for colloidal stability assessment
- TEM/SEM imaging of dispersed vs aggregated material
- Sedimentation kinetics monitoring over days-weeks

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk, sphere fitting
- **multi_component_complexity**: binary
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**:

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Electronics (conductive inks, transparent conductive films, printed electronics)
- Composites (CNT/graphene-reinforced polymer nanocomposites)
- Energy storage (electrode slurry formulation for batteries and supercapacitors)
- Coatings (anticorrosion, antistatic, thermal management coatings)
- Biomedical (graphene-based biosensors and drug delivery carriers)
- **commercial_tools**:

**Uncertain fields**: typical_red_threshold, first_reported_year, regulatory_relevance
