#### Dielectric Polymer Film Quality
**Japanese**: 誘電体高分子膜品質 | **Maturity**: established

> The quality of thin dielectric polymer films used in capacitors, gate insulators (OFETs), and interlayer dielectrics is strongly influenced by the HSP distance between the polymer and its processing solvent. When the solvent HSP closely matches the polymer HSP (RED < 1), the polymer dissolves completely with full chain extension, producing uniform, dense films upon casting. Good HSP matching promotes slow, controlled crystallization or amorphous film formation with minimal voids, pinholes, and residual stress. In contrast, poor HSP matching (RED > 1) leads to polymer aggregation in solution, resulting in rough, porous films with reduced breakdown voltage and increased leakage current. HSP-guided solvent selection thus directly impacts dielectric constant uniformity, breakdown strength, and long-term reliability of polymer dielectric films in electronic devices.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The dielectric film formation process begins with polymer dissolution, where the polymer chain conformation in solution is governed by the Flory-Huggins interaction parameter chi, which correlates with Ra^2. In a good solvent (low Ra, RED < 1), polymer chains are extended and the solution is homogeneous at the molecular level. Upon film casting (spin-coating, slot-die, or blade coating), solvent evaporation concentrates the solution. With a good solvent, the transition from dilute solution to solid film proceeds through a uniform concentration pathway, yielding dense, smooth films. With a marginal or poor solvent (RED near or above 1), chains are collapsed and may aggregate before complete solvent removal, creating density fluctuations, voids, and rough surfaces. For semicrystalline dielectric polymers (PVDF, P(VDF-TrFE)), the solvent HSP also influences crystalline polymorph selection: solvents with high dP and dH (DMF, DMSO) favor the electroactive beta-phase, while low-polarity solvents favor the non-polar alpha-phase. The drying rate relative to crystallization kinetics determines whether the film is amorphous, semicrystalline, or contains mixed phases — all affecting dielectric properties. Residual solvent trapped in films also reduces breakdown voltage; HSP predicts the difficulty of solvent removal (stronger polymer-solvent interaction = more residual solvent).
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_poly - dD_solv)^2 + (dP_poly - dP_solv)^2 + (dH_poly - dH_solv)^2); RED = Ra/R0; chi ~ V_m * Ra^2 / (6*R*T)
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: HSP values (dD, dP, dH) of dielectric polymer, HSP values (dD, dP, dH) of processing solvent(s), R0 of the dielectric polymer solubility sphere, Polymer molecular weight and concentration, Target film thickness
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for common dielectric polymers (PMMA, PS, PVDF, P(VDF-TrFE), polyimide, PVP, CYTOP)
- HSPiP solvent database
- Published literature on dielectric polymer HSP characterization
- Polymer supplier datasheets
- Group contribution estimation for novel dielectric polymers
- **data_availability_score**: 4
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Solvent boiling point and evaporation rate
- Solution viscosity at coating concentration
- Spin speed or coating parameters
- Annealing temperature and time
- Substrate surface energy and wettability
- Polymer Tg and crystallization temperature
- Film thickness uniformity specification

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- Film quality depends on drying kinetics (evaporation rate, airflow) which are process-dependent and not captured by equilibrium HSP
- Breakdown voltage is affected by film defects (particles, pinholes) introduced during processing, not solely by HSP matching
- Semicrystalline polymer morphology depends on crystallization kinetics which involves factors beyond HSP (nucleation density, cooling rate)
- Multi-layer dielectric stacks involve interlayer compatibility not fully described by pairwise HSP
- High-k dielectric polymers with strong dipolar interactions may require extended HSP models
- Residual solvent effects depend on annealing conditions and film thickness in addition to polymer-solvent HSP affinity
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Fluoropolymer dielectrics (CYTOP, Teflon AF) with limited solubility in few specialty solvents where HSP sphere fitting has insufficient data points
- Polyimide films formed via imidization of poly(amic acid) where chemical transformation during curing changes HSP
- Crosslinked dielectric polymers (e.g., SU-8 after UV cure) where post-curing HSP differs from pre-cure solution state
- Ultrathin films (<50 nm) where substrate effects and confinement alter polymer chain organization independently of solvent HSP
- **validation_method**: 
- Dielectric breakdown voltage measurement (ASTM D149)
- Capacitance-voltage (C-V) and current-voltage (I-V) measurements
- AFM surface roughness measurement (RMS roughness)
- Spectroscopic ellipsometry for film thickness and refractive index uniformity
- XRD for crystalline phase identification in semicrystalline dielectrics
- TGA for residual solvent content quantification

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: low
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: binary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **regulatory_relevance**: No direct regulatory framework referencing HSP for dielectric films. IPC standards govern dielectric material testing for PCBs. UL 94 flammability and IEC 60243 breakdown voltage standards apply. REACH and RoHS drive elimination of hazardous solvents (NMP, DMF) from dielectric film processing, where HSP enables identification of safer solvent alternatives.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Organic/printed electronics (OFET gate dielectrics)
- Flexible display manufacturing (interlayer dielectrics)
- Capacitor manufacturing (high-k polymer dielectrics for MLCC alternatives)
- Semiconductor packaging (low-k interlayer dielectrics)
- Energy storage (polymer film capacitors for power electronics)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (polymer-solvent compatibility for film casting)
- COSMOtherm (solvent screening for specialty dielectric polymers)
- Brewer Science spin-coating process optimization tools
- COMSOL Multiphysics (thin film drying simulation, complementary to HSP selection)
- Materials Studio (molecular simulation of polymer dielectric properties)

**Uncertain fields**: first_reported_year, typical_red_threshold, key_references

#### MXene Dispersion Optimization
**Japanese**: MXene分散最適化 | **Maturity**: emerging

> HSP-based solvent selection and optimization for delamination and stable dispersion of MXene (Ti3C2Tx) nanosheets. MXenes are 2D transition metal carbides/nitrides with hydrophilic surface terminations (-OH, -O, -F) that create a unique surface chemistry distinct from graphene. The HSP sphere for MXene is determined experimentally by testing dispersion stability across a solvent panel, identifying solvents within the Hansen sphere (RED < 1) that produce stable colloidal dispersions of delaminated single- or few-layer flakes. The hydrophilic surface groups shift MXene HSP toward higher polar (dP) and hydrogen bonding (dH) components compared to pristine graphene, enabling dispersion in polar protic solvents including water. This approach guides solvent selection for applications in energy storage electrodes, electromagnetic interference shielding films, sensors, and conductive inks.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: MXene (Ti3C2Tx) nanosheets possess surface terminations (-OH, -O, -F, -Cl) introduced during selective etching of the A-layer from MAX phase precursors. These terminations create a strongly hydrophilic surface with HSP values significantly different from non-functionalized 2D materials: reported values cluster around dD ~ 16.2-18.0 MPa^0.5, dP ~ 9.0-14.0 MPa^0.5, dH ~ 14.0-22.0 MPa^0.5, depending on etching conditions and termination composition. The high dH component reflects extensive hydrogen bonding capacity of the -OH and -F terminations. Stable dispersions form when the solvent HSP lies within the MXene Hansen sphere (RED < 1), meaning the solvent-MXene interaction energy overcomes the van der Waals attraction between stacked sheets. The surface termination ratio (Tx = OH:O:F) directly influences the sphere center position in Hansen space, so different etching protocols (HF, LiF/HCl, fluorine-free) yield MXenes with different optimal solvent sets. Water and polar aprotic solvents like DMSO and NMP are typically good dispersants due to the high polar and hydrogen bonding character.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_solv - dD_MXene)^2 + (dP_solv - dP_MXene)^2 + (dH_solv - dH_MXene)^2); RED = Ra/R0; Dispersion stability correlates with RED < 1
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) and R0 of the MXene material (from sphere fitting on solvent panel data)
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) of candidate solvents
- MXene composition (Ti3C2Tx, Ti2CTx, etc.) and surface termination ratio (OH:O:F)
- Etching protocol details (HF concentration, LiF/HCl, or fluorine-free method)
- Target dispersion concentration and stability requirements
- **data_sources**: 
- Experimental dispersion testing across solvent panels (primary method, typically 20-40 solvents)
- HSPiP database for solvent HSP values
- Published MXene HSP values (Maleski 2019, Sobolciak 2019)
- XPS characterization of surface termination ratios to correlate with HSP shifts
- Contact angle measurements for surface energy estimation
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Surface termination composition from XPS (OH:O:F:Cl ratio)
- Zeta potential of dispersed MXene sheets
- Solvent boiling point and viscosity (processing considerations)
- MXene flake lateral size and thickness (from AFM/SEM)
- Oxidation state and shelf life (MXene degrades in water/oxygen exposure)
- pH of the dispersion medium

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- MXene HSP values vary significantly with surface termination composition, which depends on synthesis protocol and is difficult to control precisely
- Oxidative degradation of MXene in aqueous and oxygenated environments changes surface chemistry over time, shifting the effective HSP
- The high surface charge density of delaminated MXene introduces strong electrostatic interactions not captured by the HSP framework
- Intercalated species (Li+, TMA+, etc.) from delamination process alter effective surface chemistry and may expand the good-solvent region beyond HSP predictions
- Limited number of published MXene HSP studies means sphere parameters carry large uncertainty
- The 4:1:1 weighting in the Ra equation may not be optimal for these highly polar 2D materials
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Water dispersions stabilized primarily by electrostatic repulsion from surface charges rather than thermodynamic HSP compatibility
- Solvents that cause MXene oxidation (e.g., water at elevated temperature) appear as good solvents initially but degrade the material
- Mixed termination MXenes where the surface is heterogeneous and a single HSP sphere is a poor representation
- Concentrated dispersions where liquid crystalline ordering creates rheological behavior not predicted by HSP alone
- **validation_method**: 
- UV-Vis-NIR spectroscopy of supernatant after centrifugation (MXene has characteristic absorption at ~770 nm for Ti3C2Tx)
- Dynamic light scattering (DLS) for hydrodynamic size and stability
- Zeta potential measurement
- XRD to confirm delamination (disappearance of (002) peak of multilayer MXene)
- AFM for flake thickness confirmation (single layer ~1.5 nm for Ti3C2Tx)
- Sedimentation monitoring over days to weeks

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk, sphere fitting
- **multi_component_complexity**: binary
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **regulatory_relevance**: No specific regulatory framework for HSP-based MXene dispersion assessment. General nanomaterial regulations (REACH, TSCA) require characterization of dispersion behavior but do not prescribe HSP methodology. MXene safety assessment is still in early stages.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Energy storage (MXene electrode slurries for supercapacitors and batteries)
- Electronics (conductive MXene inks for printed electronics and flexible devices)
- EMI shielding (MXene films and coatings for electromagnetic interference shielding)
- Sensors (MXene-based chemical and biosensors)
- Composites (MXene-polymer nanocomposites for mechanical and electrical enhancement)

**Uncertain fields**: typical_red_threshold, data_availability_score, first_reported_year, key_references, commercial_tools

#### Organic Semiconductor Thin Film
**Japanese**: 有機半導体薄膜 | **Maturity**: established

> The HSP relationship between organic semiconductor molecules and processing solvents critically affects thin film morphology, crystallinity, domain size, and ultimately charge transport performance in organic field-effect transistors (OFETs), organic light-emitting diodes (OLEDs), and organic photovoltaics (OPVs). Solvent selection based on HSP matching controls the dissolution state, nucleation kinetics during film formation, and phase separation in donor-acceptor bulk heterojunction blends. Solvents with HSP close to the semiconductor (low RED) produce good dissolution but may yield amorphous films, while solvents at intermediate RED values can promote controlled crystallization. For OPV bulk heterojunction systems, differential HSP matching between solvent and donor vs acceptor components controls the phase-separated morphology essential for charge generation and transport.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Organic semiconductors (conjugated polymers like P3HT, PCBM, small molecules like pentacene derivatives) have characteristic HSP values dominated by strong dispersion interactions from extended pi-conjugation (dD ~ 18-20 MPa^0.5), with varying polar and hydrogen bonding components depending on functional groups. The solvent HSP determines: (1) dissolution quality, where RED < 1 ensures complete dissolution for solution processing; (2) film formation kinetics, where solvent evaporation rate and residual solvent-semiconductor interaction control nucleation and crystal growth; (3) in BHJ OPV blends, the relative RED of the solvent to donor and acceptor determines the sequential phase separation during drying, producing the nanoscale morphology critical for device performance. Solvent additives (e.g., DIO, CN) with specific HSP values can selectively dissolve one component, enabling morphology control. The Flory-Huggins chi parameter estimated from HSP predicts polymer-fullerene miscibility and the thermodynamic driving force for phase separation.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_solv - dD_OSC)^2 + (dP_solv - dP_OSC)^2 + (dH_solv - dH_OSC)^2); RED = Ra/R0; chi_donor-acceptor = Vs*(Ra_D-A)^2/(6*R*T)
- **red_direction**: threshold_specific
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) of the organic semiconductor(s)
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) of candidate processing solvents
- HSP values of solvent additives (if BHJ morphology control needed)
- R0 of the organic semiconductor from solubility sphere fitting
- Desired film thickness and deposition method
- **data_sources**: 
- Experimental solubility testing in solvent panels with sphere fitting
- Published HSP for common organic semiconductors (P3HT, PCBM, PEDOT:PSS)
- HSPiP database for processing solvent HSP values
- Group contribution estimation for novel semiconductor molecules
- Contact angle and surface energy measurements for thin film characterization
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Solvent boiling point (controls drying kinetics and crystallization time)
- Solvent vapor pressure and evaporation rate
- Solution concentration and viscosity
- Substrate surface energy and treatment
- Deposition method parameters (spin speed, slot-die gap, temperature)
- Molecular weight and regioregularity of conjugated polymers

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- Film morphology depends on kinetic factors (evaporation rate, substrate interaction) not captured by equilibrium HSP
- Conjugated molecules with strong pi-pi stacking have directional interactions poorly represented by isotropic HSP
- HSP of organic semiconductors are difficult to determine accurately due to limited solubility in many solvents and tendency to aggregate
- BHJ morphology optimization involves complex three-component (or more) phase behavior beyond simple pairwise HSP distances
- Post-deposition annealing (thermal or solvent vapor) further modifies morphology independent of initial solvent HSP
- Device performance correlates with morphology but the HSP-to-morphology-to-performance chain involves many intervening variables
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Solvents with identical RED but different boiling points produce drastically different film morphologies due to kinetic effects
- Halogenated solvent additives (DIO, CN) whose effectiveness depends on selective solvation not fully captured by bulk HSP
- P3HT fiber/whisker formation from marginal solvents where aging and pre-aggregation dominate over thermodynamic HSP prediction
- Non-fullerene acceptor (NFA) systems where complex molecular geometries create HSP estimation challenges
- **validation_method**: 
- Grazing incidence X-ray diffraction (GIXD) for crystallinity and orientation
- Atomic force microscopy (AFM) for surface morphology and domain size
- Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) for bulk morphology
- UV-Vis absorption spectroscopy for aggregation state
- Space-charge limited current (SCLC) for charge mobility measurement
- Device characterization (J-V curves for OPV, transfer curves for OFET)

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: high
- **ui_pattern**: optimization
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk, optimizeBlend
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- Liao, H.C.; Ho, C.C.; Chang, C.Y.; Jao, M.H.; Darling, S.B.; Su, W.F. 'Additives for Morphology Control in High-Efficiency Organic Solar Cells', Materials Today, 2013, 16, 326-336
- Duong, D.T.; Walker, B.; Lin, J.; Kim, C.; Love, J.; Purushothaman, B.; Anthony, J.E.; Nguyen, T.Q. 'Molecular Solubility and Hansen Solubility Parameters for the Analysis of Phase Separation in Bulk Heterojunctions', J. Polym. Sci. Part B: Polym. Phys., 2012, 50, 1405-1413. DOI: 10.1002/polb.23153
- Walker, B.; Tamayo, A.; Duong, D.T.; Dang, X.D.; Kim, C.; Granstrom, J.; Nguyen, T.Q. 'A Systematic Approach to Solvent Selection Based on Cohesive Energy Densities in a Molecular Bulk Heterojunction System', Adv. Energy Mater., 2011, 1, 221-229
- Machui, F.; Langner, S.; Zhu, X.; Abbott, S.; Brabec, C.J. 'Determination of the P3HT:PCBM Solubility Parameters via a Binary Solvent Gradient Method', Solar Energy Materials and Solar Cells, 2012, 100, 138-146
- Ye, L.; Hu, H.; Ghasemi, M.; Wang, T.; Collins, B.A.; Kim, J.H.; Jiang, K.; Carpenter, J.H.; Li, H.; Li, Z.; McAfee, T.; Zhao, J.; Chen, X.; Lai, J.L.Y.; Ma, T.; Bredas, J.L.; Yan, H.; Ade, H. 'Quantitative Relations between Interaction Parameter, Miscibility and Function in Organic Solar Cells', Nature Materials, 2018, 17, 253-260

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Organic photovoltaics (BHJ active layer morphology optimization)
- OLED displays (emissive layer and charge transport layer deposition)
- Organic/printed electronics (OFET channel layer processing)
- Flexible electronics (roll-to-roll processing solvent selection)
- Wearable sensors (organic semiconductor-based sensing layers)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (solvent screening and sphere fitting)
- Scienomics MAPS (molecular simulation for organic electronics)
- Schrodinger Materials Science Suite (molecular HSP prediction)
- COSMOtherm (sigma-profile based solubility for conjugated molecules)
- Ossila (organic electronics materials supplier with processing guides)

**Uncertain fields**: first_reported_year, typical_red_threshold, data_availability_score, regulatory_relevance

#### PSA Tape Peel Strength
**Japanese**: 感圧接着テープ剥離強度 | **Maturity**: established

> Pressure-sensitive adhesives (PSAs) rely on a balance of tack, peel resistance, and shear holding power, all of which correlate with the HSP distance between the adhesive polymer and the adherend surface. When the HSP of the PSA (typically acrylate, rubber, or silicone-based) closely matches that of the substrate, interfacial wetting and molecular interdiffusion are maximized, yielding high peel force. The Ra or RED value between PSA and adherend serves as a screening metric: low RED indicates thermodynamic affinity, promoting intimate contact and bond formation at the interface. This approach enables rapid selection of PSA chemistries for specific substrates (metals, plastics, glass) in electronics assembly, automotive trim, and medical device taping. Beyond simple compatibility, HSP distance also helps predict failure mode transitions between adhesive (clean peel) and cohesive (residue-leaving) failure, since strong interfacial adhesion shifts the locus of failure into the bulk adhesive.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The thermodynamic work of adhesion between a PSA and a substrate correlates with their HSP proximity. When the HSP of the PSA polymer (characterized by dD, dP, dH) closely matches the effective HSP of the adherend surface, the Flory-Huggins interaction parameter chi is minimized, promoting molecular wetting and chain interdiffusion across the interface. For polymeric substrates, direct HSP matching applies; for metal or glass surfaces, effective surface HSP values (derived from contact angle or inverse gas chromatography) are used. The peel force is not solely determined by thermodynamic adhesion but also by viscoelastic energy dissipation in the adhesive bulk during debonding. However, HSP matching controls the interfacial contribution: when RED is low, the adhesive wets and bonds strongly to the surface, and the debonding energy is dominated by bulk viscoelastic dissipation (high peel force). When RED is high, poor wetting leads to weak interfacial bonds and low peel force regardless of bulk properties. HSP analysis also guides tackifier selection: tackifiers must be compatible with the PSA polymer (low RED) to plasticize and increase tack, while remaining outside the solubility sphere of the substrate to avoid migration.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_PSA - dD_sub)^2 + (dP_PSA - dP_sub)^2 + (dH_PSA - dH_sub)^2); RED = Ra/R0; W_adhesion ~ 2*gamma_PSA*gamma_sub*[f(Ra)]
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) of PSA base polymer
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) of adherend surface (or effective surface HSP)
- R0 of the PSA polymer solubility sphere
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) of tackifier resin (if used)
- Application temperature
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for common PSA polymers (acrylates, SIS, SBS, silicones)
- Contact angle measurements on adherend surfaces (converted to surface energy components and effective HSP)
- Inverse gas chromatography (IGC) for surface HSP of solid substrates
- Tackifier supplier datasheets with HSP values
- Literature values for common substrates (PE, PP, PET, stainless steel, glass)
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Viscoelastic properties of PSA (G', G'' at bonding and debonding rates)
- Surface roughness and surface energy of adherend
- Adhesive coating thickness
- Peel rate and peel angle
- Temperature and humidity during testing
- Tackifier softening point and Mw

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- Peel force depends heavily on viscoelastic dissipation which is not captured by thermodynamic HSP distance alone
- Surface HSP of inorganic substrates (metals, glass, ceramics) are approximations derived indirectly from contact angle or IGC data
- PSA formulations are multi-component (base polymer, tackifier, plasticizer, crosslinker) making effective HSP estimation complex
- Rate- and temperature-dependent adhesion behavior requires rheological modeling beyond HSP
- Surface contamination, oxide layers, and surface treatments drastically alter effective surface HSP
- Crosslinked PSA networks cannot be characterized by simple dissolution-based HSP sphere fitting
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- High-energy metal surfaces where chemisorption (not HSP-based physisorption) dominates adhesion
- Low-surface-energy substrates (PTFE, silicone) where even HSP-matched PSAs cannot achieve sufficient wetting without surface treatment
- Aging and environmental degradation where interfacial chemistry changes over time independently of initial HSP matching
- Adhesive failure on flexible substrates where mechanical strain energy release rate dominates over thermodynamic adhesion
- **validation_method**: 
- 180-degree peel test (ASTM D3330/PSTC-101)
- 90-degree peel test for rigid substrates
- Loop tack test (ASTM D6195)
- Probe tack measurement (texture analyzer)
- Shear adhesion failure temperature (SAFT) test
- Contact angle measurement on adherend for surface energy correlation

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: matrix
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk, optimizeBlend
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **regulatory_relevance**: No direct regulatory framework for HSP in PSA design. UL and ASTM standards govern PSA tape performance testing. For medical and food-contact PSA tapes, FDA and EU regulations address biocompatibility and migration limits but do not reference HSP-based design. REACH drives substitution of hazardous solvents in PSA manufacture where HSP guides waterborne and hot-melt alternatives.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Electronics assembly (die-attach tapes, thermal interface tapes, EMI shielding tapes)
- Automotive (trim attachment, wire harnessing, surface protection films)
- Medical devices (surgical tapes, wound dressings, transdermal patches)
- Packaging (carton sealing, label stock)
- Construction (glazing tapes, insulation bonding)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (PSA-substrate compatibility screening and tackifier selection)
- Tesa/3M/Nitto internal formulation tools (proprietary HSP-based design)
- COSMOtherm (adhesive-substrate interaction prediction)
- Rheology software (Anton Paar RheoCompass) for complementary viscoelastic characterization

**Uncertain fields**: first_reported_year, typical_red_threshold, data_availability_score, key_references

---

#### Photoresist Developer Compatibility
**Japanese**: フォトレジスト現像液適合性 | **Maturity**: established

> The HSP distance between photoresist polymers and developer solvents determines the dissolution contrast that underlies pattern resolution in photolithography. In positive-tone resists, exposed (deprotected) regions must dissolve readily in the developer while unexposed regions remain intact; this differential solubility maps directly to HSP distance differences. The exposed resist has modified HSP (increased dP and dH due to deprotection of carboxylic acid groups from t-BOC or acetal protecting groups), bringing it inside the developer's solubility sphere, while the unexposed resist remains outside. For negative-tone resists, crosslinking shifts HSP and increases R0, making the crosslinked resist insoluble. HSP-based analysis enables rational developer formulation, resist chemistry design, and process window optimization for advanced lithography nodes.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The photoresist-developer system exploits a photo-induced HSP shift. Before exposure, the resist polymer (e.g., poly(4-t-butoxycarbonyloxystyrene) or methacrylate copolymers with acid-labile groups) has specific HSP values with relatively low dP and dH due to nonpolar protecting groups. Upon UV/DUV/EUV exposure and post-exposure bake, photoacid-catalyzed deprotection generates polar groups (carboxylic acids, hydroxyl groups), dramatically increasing dP and dH of the exposed polymer. The developer (aqueous TMAH 2.38% for positive resists, or organic solvents for negative-tone development) is chosen so that the exposed polymer HSP falls within the developer solubility sphere (RED < 1) while the unexposed polymer remains outside (RED > 1). The dissolution contrast (ratio of dissolution rates) correlates with the difference in RED values between exposed and unexposed states. For negative-tone development (NTD) with organic developers, the logic is inverted: organic solvents dissolve the unexposed (nonpolar) resist while the exposed (polar) regions remain. HSP analysis guides developer selection and concentration optimization for maximum contrast.
- **key_equation**: Ra_exposed = sqrt(4*(dD_dev - dD_exp)^2 + (dP_dev - dP_exp)^2 + (dH_dev - dH_exp)^2); Ra_unexposed = sqrt(4*(dD_dev - dD_unexp)^2 + (dP_dev - dP_unexp)^2 + (dH_dev - dH_unexp)^2); Contrast ~ log(Ra_unexposed/Ra_exposed)
- **red_direction**: threshold_specific
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: low

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) of unexposed resist polymer
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) of exposed (deprotected) resist polymer
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) of developer solvent or solution
- R0 for the resist polymer system
- Degree of deprotection (exposure dose dependent)
- **data_sources**: 
- Experimental dissolution rate measurements in solvent panels
- Group contribution estimation for resist polymer HSP before/after deprotection
- HSPiP database for developer solvent HSP values
- Resist supplier datasheets (limited HSP information)
- Published literature on chemically amplified resist HSP characterization
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Exposure dose and post-exposure bake temperature/time
- Developer concentration and temperature
- Resist film thickness
- Dissolution rate as function of depth (surface inhibition effects)
- Resist molecular weight and polydispersity
- Photoacid generator (PAG) loading and acid diffusion length

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- HSP of the exposed resist varies continuously with deprotection degree, which depends on local exposure dose and PEB conditions
- Aqueous TMAH developer involves ionic dissolution mechanism (carboxylate formation) partially beyond HSP framework
- Dissolution in thin films involves surface-rate-limited and diffusion-limited regimes not captured by bulk HSP compatibility
- Swelling of resist during development affects pattern fidelity but requires kinetic modeling beyond HSP
- Sub-10nm resolution lithography involves additional factors (acid diffusion, line-edge roughness) independent of HSP
- Multi-component resist formulations (base polymer, PAG, quencher, additives) complicate effective HSP estimation
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Aqueous TMAH development of carboxylic acid resists where pH-dependent ionization dominates over HSP-predicted solvation
- Negative-tone development with organic solvents where resist swelling before dissolution causes pattern collapse
- EUV resists with metal-oxide nanoparticle components where inorganic interactions are outside HSP scope
- Ultra-thin resist films (<30 nm) where substrate interaction and confinement effects alter dissolution behavior
- **validation_method**: 
- Quartz crystal microbalance (QCM) for real-time dissolution rate monitoring
- SEM cross-section imaging of developed patterns
- Critical dimension (CD) measurement and dose-to-size analysis
- Dissolution rate vs depth profiling (puddle develop or spray develop)
- Contrast curve measurement (remaining thickness vs log exposure dose)

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: high
- **ui_pattern**: pairwise
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- Ito, H. 'Chemical Amplification Resists: Inception, Implementation in Device Manufacture, and New Developments', J. Polym. Sci. Part A: Polym. Chem., 2003, 41, 3863-3870. DOI: 10.1002/pola.10722
- Hansen, C.M. 'Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook', 2nd Ed., CRC Press, 2007
- Tsiartas, P.C.; Flanagin, L.W.; Henderson, C.L.; Hinsberg, W.D.; Sanchez, I.C.; Bonnecaze, R.T.; Willson, C.G. 'The Mechanism of Phenolic Polymer Dissolution: A New Perspective', Macromolecules, 1997, 30, 4656-4664
- Yamada, S.; Mack, C.A.; Kang, D. 'Applying Hansen Solubility Parameters to Predict Photoresist-Solvent Interactions for EUV Lithography', Proc. SPIE, 2018, 10586, 105860O
- Mack, C.A. 'Fundamental Principles of Optical Lithography: The Science of Microfabrication', Wiley, 2007

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Semiconductor manufacturing (IC lithography at all technology nodes)
- MEMS/NEMS fabrication (resist patterning for microstructures)
- Photovoltaics (patterning for interdigitated back-contact cells)
- Flat panel display manufacturing (TFT backplane lithography)
- Advanced packaging (redistribution layer and bump patterning)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (resist-developer compatibility screening)
- PROLITH (Mack, lithography simulation incorporating dissolution kinetics)
- Schrodinger Materials Science (molecular-level resist property prediction)
- Sumitomo/TOK/JSR (resist suppliers with proprietary compatibility data)
- COSMOtherm (solubility prediction for resist-developer pairs)

**Uncertain fields**: first_reported_year, typical_red_threshold, data_availability_score, regulatory_relevance

#### Printed Electronics Ink-Substrate Wetting
**Japanese**: プリンテッドエレクトロニクス・インク-基板濡れ性 | **Maturity**: established

> In printed electronics, functional inks containing conductive (silver nanoparticles, PEDOT:PSS), semiconducting (organic small molecules, conjugated polymers), or dielectric materials must wet the substrate uniformly to produce continuous, defect-free films. HSP matching between the ink solvent system and the substrate surface governs the contact angle, spreading behavior, and ultimately the morphology of the printed feature. Poor HSP matching leads to de-wetting, coffee-ring effects, and non-uniform film thickness, all of which degrade device performance. By combining HSP distance with surface energy analysis, formulators can rationally select solvent blends that optimize both wetting on the target substrate and drying dynamics (using high-boiling co-solvents with matched HSP to suppress Marangoni-driven coffee rings). This approach enables design of inkjet, gravure, slot-die, and screen-printing inks for organic electronics, printed sensors, and flexible circuits.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Wetting of a substrate by a printed electronics ink is governed by the balance between ink surface tension and substrate surface energy, both of which correlate with HSP parameters. When the ink solvent HSP closely matches the substrate HSP, the interfacial energy is minimized, reducing contact angle and promoting spreading. The spreading coefficient S = gamma_substrate - gamma_ink - gamma_interfacial is positive when HSP matching drives gamma_interfacial toward zero. For multi-solvent ink systems, the composite HSP of the solvent blend must simultaneously satisfy: (1) dissolution/dispersion of the functional material (RED < 1 for the solute), (2) wetting of the substrate (low Ra between solvent and substrate), and (3) controlled drying dynamics (co-solvent with higher boiling point but similar HSP to maintain uniform composition during evaporation, suppressing Marangoni flows that cause coffee rings). The coffee-ring effect is mitigated when a binary solvent system creates an inward Marangoni flow: the low-boiling component evaporates first from the edge, locally enriching the high-boiling component which has lower surface tension, driving flow inward. HSP guides selection of such complementary solvent pairs that maintain solute compatibility throughout drying.
- **key_equation**: Ra_wetting = sqrt(4*(dD_ink - dD_sub)^2 + (dP_ink - dP_sub)^2 + (dH_ink - dH_sub)^2); Ra_solute = sqrt(4*(dD_ink - dD_solute)^2 + (dP_ink - dP_solute)^2 + (dH_ink - dH_solute)^2); Spreading coefficient S ~ f(gamma_sub, gamma_ink, Ra_wetting)
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) of ink solvent(s)
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) of functional material (conductor/semiconductor/dielectric)
- HSP values or surface energy of substrate (PET, PEN, PI, glass, paper)
- R0 for the functional material
- Boiling points and vapor pressures of solvent components
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for solvents and common polymers
- Published HSP for conjugated polymers and organic semiconductors
- Contact angle measurements on substrates for surface energy estimation
- Ink supplier technical datasheets
- Literature on printed electronics ink formulation
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Ink viscosity and shear-thinning behavior
- Surface tension of ink (static and dynamic)
- Substrate surface energy (dispersive and polar components)
- Print head nozzle diameter and jetting parameters (for inkjet)
- Substrate surface treatment (plasma, UV-ozone, SAM coating)
- Drying temperature and atmosphere
- Ink solid loading and particle size distribution

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- Wetting is a surface phenomenon while HSP describes bulk thermodynamic interactions — surface energy models (Owens-Wendt, van Oss) may be more directly applicable
- Dynamic wetting during high-speed printing involves inertial and viscous effects beyond thermodynamic HSP matching
- Coffee-ring suppression depends on Marangoni flow which requires detailed evaporation modeling not captured by HSP alone
- Nanoparticle inks (Ag, Cu) involve colloidal stability governed by steric/electrostatic stabilization in addition to HSP
- Substrate surface treatment (plasma, corona, SAM) changes surface energy dynamically and may not be well-described by static HSP values
- Ink-substrate interaction changes as solvent evaporates and composition shifts during printing
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Highly polar substrates (glass, ITO) where aqueous ink wetting is controlled by electrostatic and acid-base interactions beyond HSP
- Porous substrates (paper, textile) where capillary penetration rather than surface wetting governs ink behavior
- UV-curable inks where reactive monomer crosslinking during printing alters HSP in real time
- Nanoparticle aggregation at the contact line during drying despite favorable bulk HSP matching
- **validation_method**: 
- Contact angle goniometry (static and dynamic) for wetting characterization
- Optical profilometry or confocal microscopy for printed line/dot morphology
- Electrical resistance measurement of printed conductors as quality metric
- High-speed video of droplet spreading and drying on substrate
- AFM/SEM of dried film morphology

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: optimization
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk, optimizeBlend
- **multi_component_complexity**: multi
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **regulatory_relevance**: No direct regulatory acceptance of HSP for printed electronics ink design. IPC standards (IPC-2291, IPC-6901) govern printed electronics performance testing. REACH and VOC regulations drive substitution of hazardous solvents (NMP, chlorinated solvents) in ink formulations, where HSP-guided solvent replacement is industrially practiced. RoHS compliance affects conductive ink composition.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Flexible and printed electronics (OFET, OLED, OPV manufacturing)
- Printed sensors and biosensors
- RFID tag manufacturing
- Display backplane fabrication
- Printed circuit board prototyping and additive manufacturing
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (solvent blend optimization for ink formulation)
- Dimatix Materials Printer DMP-2850 (inkjet printing with ink optimization software)
- COSMOtherm (molecular solvent selection for organic semiconductors)
- Materials Studio (polymer-solvent interaction modeling)
- Kruss DSA (surface tension and contact angle measurement for wetting validation)

**Uncertain fields**: first_reported_year, typical_red_threshold, data_availability_score, key_references

---

#### Quantum Dot Ligand Exchange Solvent
**Japanese**: 量子ドットリガンド交換溶媒 | **Maturity**: emerging

> Colloidal quantum dots (QDs) are stabilized by surface ligands whose HSP must match the processing solvent to maintain colloidal stability. During ligand exchange — replacing long-chain native ligands (e.g., oleic acid) with short-chain or inorganic ligands for improved charge transport in devices — the solvent must simultaneously dissolve the incoming ligand, solvate the QD with its new surface chemistry, and ideally be a poor solvent for the outgoing ligand to drive the exchange. HSP matching between the QD-ligand shell and the exchange solvent governs aggregation, precipitation, and the completeness of ligand exchange, directly affecting QD film quality in LEDs, solar cells, and photodetectors. This framework enables systematic solvent selection for each step of QD processing rather than relying on empirical trial-and-error.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: QDs are effectively colloidal particles whose dispersibility depends on the HSP match between the ligand shell (the outermost organic or inorganic layer) and the solvent. The native synthesis ligands (oleic acid, oleylamine, trioctylphosphine oxide) have characteristic HSP, and solvents with low Ra to these ligands maintain stable dispersions. During ligand exchange, the QD surface transitions from one ligand type to another (e.g., oleic acid to mercaptopropionic acid, or from organic to halide/inorganic ligands). The exchange solvent must: (1) have RED < 1 relative to the incoming ligand to keep it dissolved and available for exchange, (2) have RED < 1 relative to the QD with partially exchanged ligand shell to prevent aggregation during the process, and (3) ideally have RED > 1 relative to the outgoing ligand-QD combination to thermodynamically drive precipitation/phase separation of the native ligand. Two-phase ligand exchange protocols explicitly exploit HSP differences: QDs transfer from a nonpolar phase (matching native ligands) to a polar phase (matching new ligands) at the interface. HSP distance also predicts the colloidal stability of the final QD ink for deposition.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_solv - dD_ligand)^2 + (dP_solv - dP_ligand)^2 + (dH_solv - dH_ligand)^2); RED_incoming = Ra_incoming/R0_incoming; RED_outgoing = Ra_outgoing/R0_outgoing
- **red_direction**: threshold_specific
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) of native (outgoing) ligand
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) of target (incoming) ligand
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) of exchange solvent(s)
- R0 for QD-ligand colloidal stability sphere
- QD core composition and size (affects surface energy)
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for common solvents and small organic molecules
- Group contribution estimation for ligand molecules (oleic acid, thiols, amines, carboxylates)
- Experimental dispersion stability testing (centrifugation, DLS) in solvent panels
- Published QD-solvent compatibility studies in nanocrystal literature
- IGC or contact angle measurements for QD surface characterization
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- QD core size and size distribution
- Ligand grafting density on QD surface
- Solvent boiling point and vapor pressure (for film deposition)
- Solvent viscosity (affects processing and film quality)
- Dielectric constant of solvent (affects electrostatic stabilization for charged ligands)
- Ligand binding energy to QD surface (thermodynamic driving force for exchange)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- QD surface is not a bulk material — effective HSP of a ligand-capped nanoparticle surface is size-dependent and curvature-dependent
- Ligand exchange involves kinetic barriers (activation energy for ligand desorption/adsorption) not captured by thermodynamic HSP
- Inorganic ligands (halides, metal chalcogenide complexes) are poorly described by organic HSP parameters
- Mixed ligand shells during incomplete exchange have ill-defined composite HSP
- Electrostatic stabilization (for charged ligand-capped QDs in polar solvents) operates outside HSP framework
- Quantum confinement and surface trap states affected by ligand exchange are not predicted by HSP
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Inorganic ligand (e.g., S2-, SCN-) exchange in formamide/DMSO where ionic interactions dominate over van der Waals HSP matching
- Large QDs (>10 nm) where gravitational settling competes with colloidal stability regardless of HSP match
- Ligand exchange at elevated temperatures where rapid kinetics cause uncontrolled aggregation despite favorable HSP
- Multi-shell QDs (e.g., CdSe/CdS/ZnS) where surface composition differs from simple ligand model
- **validation_method**: 
- Dynamic light scattering (DLS) for colloidal stability and aggregation monitoring
- UV-Vis and photoluminescence spectroscopy (QD optical properties as proxy for surface quality)
- FTIR/NMR to confirm ligand exchange completeness
- Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) for ligand content quantification
- Thin film characterization (AFM, SEM) for QD film morphology after deposition

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: high
- **ui_pattern**: optimization
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk, optimizeBlend
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **regulatory_relevance**: No established regulatory framework for HSP in QD processing. RoHS restricts cadmium-based QDs driving adoption of InP and perovskite QDs. REACH governs solvent use in QD manufacturing. OSHA/exposure limits apply to exchange solvents (formamide, DMF, hydrazine). HSP-guided solvent substitution supports compliance with green chemistry initiatives.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Display technology (QD-LED/QLED TVs, QD color conversion films)
- Photovoltaics (QD solar cells, luminescent solar concentrators)
- Photodetectors and image sensors
- Biomedical imaging (QD fluorescent labels)
- Quantum computing and photonics research
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (solvent screening for nanoparticle dispersions)
- COSMOtherm (molecular-level solvent-ligand interaction prediction)
- Nanocyl/Nanoco/QD Vision proprietary formulation tools
- Materials Studio (atomistic simulation of QD-ligand-solvent systems)

**Uncertain fields**: first_reported_year, typical_red_threshold, data_availability_score, key_references

---

#### Underfill/Encapsulant Compatibility
**Japanese**: アンダーフィル/封止材適合性 | **Maturity**: established

> In semiconductor packaging, underfill and encapsulant materials must adhere strongly to multiple surfaces — silicon die, solder bumps, copper pads, substrate laminate, and molding compound — while resisting moisture ingress and thermal cycling stress. HSP matching between the encapsulant (typically epoxy-based, silicone, or polyurethane) and these diverse surfaces predicts interfacial adhesion strength: low HSP distance corresponds to strong thermodynamic affinity and wetting, promoting crack-free interfaces under thermal and mechanical stress. Poor HSP matching leads to delamination, moisture-driven corrosion, and package failure. HSP analysis enables systematic selection of underfill chemistry and filler surface treatments to maximize adhesion to all critical interfaces in flip-chip, BGA, CSP, and advanced 2.5D/3D packaging architectures.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Underfill and encapsulant performance depends on adhesion at multiple material interfaces within the semiconductor package. The thermodynamic work of adhesion at each interface correlates with the HSP distance between the encapsulant matrix and the surface. For polymeric substrates (PCB laminate, solder resist), direct HSP comparison applies. For inorganic surfaces (silicon, SiO2, Cu, solder alloys), effective surface HSP derived from contact angle measurements or IGC data are used. The encapsulant must have RED < 1 relative to the most critical surfaces to ensure adequate wetting and adhesion. For epoxy underfills, the HSP changes during cure (from liquid monomer/hardener mixture to crosslinked network), and both the uncured state (governing wetting and capillary flow) and cured state (governing long-term adhesion) HSP must be considered. Silane coupling agents are selected based on HSP matching: one end of the silane matches the inorganic surface and the other matches the epoxy matrix, bridging the HSP gap. Moisture ingress prediction also uses HSP: water has extreme dH, and encapsulants with high dH (closer to water HSP) absorb more moisture, degrading adhesion at interfaces through hydrolysis. Minimizing encapsulant dH while maintaining adhesion to surfaces is a key design trade-off.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_enc - dD_surf)^2 + (dP_enc - dP_surf)^2 + (dH_enc - dH_surf)^2); RED = Ra/R0; Moisture uptake ~ f(dH_encapsulant, dH_water); W_adhesion ~ gamma_enc + gamma_surf - gamma_interfacial
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) of underfill/encapsulant resin (uncured and cured)
- HSP values or effective surface HSP of die surface (Si, SiN, SiO2)
- HSP values or effective surface HSP of substrate (BT, FR-4, polyimide flex)
- HSP values of solder resist and copper pad surfaces
- HSP values of silane coupling agents (if used)
- R0 for the encapsulant system
- **data_sources**: 
- Encapsulant supplier datasheets (some provide HSP or solubility parameter data)
- HSPiP database for epoxy resins, hardeners, and solvents
- Contact angle and IGC measurements on semiconductor packaging surfaces
- Published literature on epoxy-surface adhesion and HSP correlation
- Group contribution estimation for novel encapsulant chemistries
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- CTE (coefficient of thermal expansion) of encapsulant and surrounding materials
- Glass transition temperature (Tg) of cured encapsulant
- Moisture diffusion coefficient and saturation level
- Filler type, size, and loading (silica, alumina, BN)
- Viscosity and capillary flow characteristics (for underfill)
- Surface roughness of die and substrate
- Cure shrinkage and residual stress

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- Real package interfaces involve chemical bonding (silanes, oxide layers) in addition to physical HSP-based adhesion
- HSP of cured thermoset encapsulants cannot be measured by dissolution — must be estimated from composition or swelling tests
- Multi-material interfaces in a package each have different optimal HSP, requiring compromise formulation
- Thermal cycling reliability involves CTE mismatch and fatigue mechanics beyond adhesion/HSP
- Filler-matrix interface adds another HSP interaction layer not captured in bulk encapsulant HSP
- Surface contamination from flux residues, oxidation, and handling alters effective surface HSP
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Moisture-induced delamination at die-attach interface where absorbed water (dH = 42.3) disrupts hydrogen bonds regardless of initial HSP matching
- Lead-free solder reflow temperatures (260 C+) where encapsulant properties change and moisture generates steam pressure (popcorn failure)
- Copper oxidation changing surface HSP over time, degrading initially good encapsulant-Cu adhesion
- Low-k dielectric surfaces on advanced dies with different surface chemistry from bulk silicon
- **validation_method**: 
- Die shear test (JEDEC JESD22-B116)
- Button shear adhesion test on individual surfaces
- C-SAM (C-mode scanning acoustic microscopy) for delamination detection
- Moisture sensitivity level (MSL) testing (IPC/JEDEC J-STD-020)
- Temperature cycling test (JEDEC JESD22-A104)
- Contact angle measurement on package surfaces for wetting assessment

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: high
- **ui_pattern**: matrix
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: multi
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **regulatory_relevance**: No direct regulatory requirement for HSP analysis in semiconductor packaging. JEDEC reliability standards (JESD22 series) govern package qualification testing. IPC-SM-785 covers solder joint reliability. RoHS and REACH restrict hazardous substances in packaging materials. Halogen-free requirements drive reformulation of encapsulants where HSP guides alternative hardener and flame retardant selection.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Semiconductor packaging (flip-chip, BGA, CSP, WLP, 2.5D/3D IC)
- Automotive electronics (high-reliability under-hood packaging)
- Aerospace and defense electronics (harsh environment packaging)
- Consumer electronics (smartphone, tablet, wearable SiP modules)
- LED packaging (phosphor encapsulation and lens bonding)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (encapsulant-surface compatibility screening)
- Henkel/Namics/Zymet underfill selection guides (some incorporate HSP concepts)
- ANSYS Mechanical (complementary thermo-mechanical stress simulation)
- COSMOtherm (molecular-level adhesion energy prediction)
- Materials Studio (molecular dynamics of epoxy-surface interactions)

**Uncertain fields**: first_reported_year, typical_red_threshold, data_availability_score, key_references

### Category: Energy/Environment

#### Biofuel-Material Compatibility
**Japanese**: バイオ燃料-材料適合性予測 | **Maturity**: established

> HSP distance between biodiesel, bioethanol, and their blends with petroleum fuels and fuel system materials (elastomers, plastics, coatings) is used to predict swelling, degradation, and compatibility issues. As biofuel adoption increases, existing fuel system components designed for conventional petroleum fuels face exposure to chemically different biofuel constituents (fatty acid methyl esters in biodiesel, ethanol in E10-E85 blends). HSP analysis predicts which materials will experience unacceptable swelling, softening, or extraction of plasticizers when exposed to biofuels, enabling proactive material selection and qualification for fuel-compatible components in vehicles, storage tanks, dispensing equipment, and pipeline systems.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Material compatibility with biofuels follows HSP affinity principles: when the HSP distance Ra between a biofuel and a polymer material is small (RED < 1), the fuel can penetrate and swell the polymer, potentially causing dimensional changes, mechanical property loss, and extraction of additives. For elastomeric seals and hoses, the degree of swelling correlates with Ra according to Flory-Rehner theory modified with HSP-derived chi parameters. Biodiesel (FAME) has different HSP from petroleum diesel due to ester functional groups contributing to higher delta_p and delta_h, bringing it closer to many elastomers in HSP space. Ethanol-gasoline blends show nonlinear HSP variation with blend ratio due to hydrogen bonding effects, creating a 'co-solvency' region that can dissolve materials resistant to either pure component. The RED number predicts resistance (RED > 1) or vulnerability (RED < 1) of each material to a given fuel composition.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_fuel - dD_material)^2 + (dP_fuel - dP_material)^2 + (dH_fuel - dH_material)^2); RED = Ra / R0; Volume swell (%) ~ f(1/RED); Blend HSP: delta_blend = sum(phi_i * delta_i) (with non-ideal correction for ethanol-hydrocarbon mixtures); chi = V_fuel * Ra^2 / (6 * R * T)
- **red_direction**: high_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: RED > 1.0 for material resistance; RED < 0.8 indicates significant swelling risk (>10% volume change); RED 0.8-1.2 is a caution zone requiring experimental verification
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP of biofuel or fuel blend (delta_d, delta_p, delta_h)
- HSP and interaction radius R0 of fuel system material (elastomer, plastic, coating)
- Fuel blend composition (vol% biodiesel or ethanol)
- Material crosslink density (for elastomers)
- Service temperature range
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for common elastomers and plastics
- Published HSP for FAME biodiesel components (methyl oleate, methyl palmitate, etc.)
- Calculated blend HSP from component volume fractions
- ASTM fuel specifications for reference fuel compositions
- Rubber manufacturers' technical data sheets for elastomer HSP
- **data_availability_score**: 4
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Material crosslink density and hardness
- Fuel permeation rate through material
- Temperature cycling profile
- Exposure duration (for kinetic swelling prediction)
- Presence of fuel additives and contaminants
- Material tensile strength and elongation at break
- Fuel oxidation stability (peroxide formation)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: HSP-based compatibility prediction is well-suited for equilibrium swelling prediction but does not capture time-dependent degradation mechanisms including oxidative attack by biodiesel peroxides, hydrolysis of ester-based polymers by water in biofuels, or stress cracking under mechanical load. Biodiesel composition varies with feedstock (soy, rapeseed, palm, waste cooking oil), causing HSP variation that must be accounted for. Ethanol-gasoline blends exhibit non-ideal mixing behavior due to hydrogen bonding, making simple linear HSP blending rules inaccurate at intermediate compositions. Fuel system materials often contain plasticizers, fillers, and stabilizers whose extraction changes material properties in ways not predicted by bulk polymer HSP. Temperature cycling and combined chemical-mechanical stress are not modeled.
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Ethanol-gasoline co-solvency effects where intermediate blend ratios (E20-E40) attack materials resistant to both pure components
- Oxidized biodiesel with elevated peroxide values causing chemical degradation beyond physical swelling
- Fiber-reinforced composites (e.g., fiberglass fuel tanks) where interfacial degradation differs from matrix swelling
- Metallic corrosion by biofuels (not addressable by HSP)
- Multi-layer fuel lines where different polymers respond differently to fuel permeation
- **validation_method**: ASTM D471 standard immersion testing for rubber volume change, SAE J1748 and J2260 fuel system material compatibility testing, tensile property measurement before and after fuel exposure (ASTM D412), fuel permeation testing per CARB/EPA protocols, dynamic mechanical analysis (DMA) for modulus changes

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: matrix
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk, optimizeBlend
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Haseeb, A.S.M.A.; Fazal, M.A.; Jahirul, M.I.; Masjuki, H.H. | **title**: Compatibility of automotive materials in biodiesel: A review | **journal**: Fuel | **year**: 2011 | **doi**: 10.1016/j.fuel.2010.10.042
- **author**: Flitney, R. | **title**: Which elastomers are right for biofuels? | **journal**: Sealing Technology | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1016/S1350-4789(07)70406-3
- **author**: Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook, 2nd Edition | **journal**: CRC Press | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1201/9781420006834
- **author**: Weltschev, M. | **title**: Influence of biodiesel, normal diesel and biofuel blends on the chemical resistance of polymeric materials | **journal**: Materials and Corrosion | **year**: 2019 | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **first_reported_year**: 2005
- **regulatory_relevance**: EPA Renewable Fuel Standard (RFS2) and EU Renewable Energy Directive (RED II) mandate increasing biofuel blending, driving material compatibility requirements. SAE J2260 specifies compatibility testing for fuel system elastomers. ASTM D7467 (B6-B20 biodiesel blends) and ASTM D4806 (fuel ethanol) include material compatibility provisions. CARB fuel system permeation standards require demonstration of material resistance. UL 2583 covers material compatibility for fuel dispensing equipment.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Automotive (OEM and aftermarket fuel systems)
- Fuel distribution and dispensing equipment
- Biofuel production and blending facilities
- Aerospace (sustainable aviation fuel compatibility)
- Marine engines and fuel systems
- Agricultural equipment
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice)
- O-Ring Material Selection databases (Parker, Trelleborg)
- COSMOtherm (fuel-material interaction prediction)
- FEA software with swelling models (ANSYS, Abaqus)

**Uncertain fields**: literature.key_references[3].doi, literature.first_reported_year

---

#### CO2 Absorbent Selection
**Japanese**: CO2吸収剤選定 | **Maturity**: emerging

> HSP distance between CO2 and candidate absorbent solvents predicts absorption capacity for carbon capture applications. By comparing the HSP of CO2 under process conditions with the HSP of physical absorbents (ionic liquids, deep eutectic solvents, organic solvents, amine solutions), rapid screening of absorbent candidates is enabled. This HSP-based approach complements traditional thermodynamic models (equation of state, activity coefficient models) by providing an intuitive framework for initial solvent selection in carbon capture and utilization (CCU) processes.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: CO2 absorption in physical solvents follows the 'like dissolves like' principle: solvents whose HSP closely match those of CO2 exhibit higher physical absorption capacities (Henry's law constants). CO2 has distinct HSP values (dD ~15.6, dP ~5.2, dH ~5.8 MPa^0.5 at standard conditions) with moderate dispersion, low-moderate polarity, and moderate hydrogen bonding due to its quadrupole moment and Lewis acid character. The Ra distance between CO2 HSP and absorbent HSP correlates inversely with physical CO2 solubility. For chemical absorbents (amines, carbonate solutions), HSP provides partial guidance but the dominant absorption mechanism is chemical reaction (carbamate/bicarbonate formation), which is not directly captured by HSP. However, even for chemical absorbents, the physical solubility component (governed by HSP matching) affects the overall absorption rate by determining CO2 availability at the reaction site. For ionic liquids and deep eutectic solvents as novel CO2 absorbents, HSP screening is particularly valuable because their tunable structure allows systematic HSP optimization.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_CO2-dD_absorbent)^2 + (dP_CO2-dP_absorbent)^2 + (dH_CO2-dH_absorbent)^2); Henry's constant H inversely correlates with 1/Ra^2; Physical solubility ~ exp(-V_CO2 * Ra^2 / (6RT))
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: high

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- CO2 HSP at process temperature and pressure (dD, dP, dH)
- Absorbent HSP (dD, dP, dH) for each candidate
- Process temperature and pressure
- CO2 partial pressure in feed gas
- Absorbent molar volume
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for common organic solvents
- Published CO2 HSP values (temperature and pressure dependent)
- Published ionic liquid HSP from group contribution or COSMO-RS
- Experimental Henry's law constants for validation
- COSMO-RS calculations for novel absorbent HSP estimation
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- CO2 partial pressure and total pressure
- Absorbent viscosity (affects mass transfer rate)
- Absorbent thermal stability and degradation temperature
- Absorbent vapor pressure (determines solvent losses)
- Heat of absorption (energy cost for regeneration)
- Absorbent water content (for aqueous systems)
- Chemical reactivity with CO2 (for chemical absorbents)
- Absorbent cost and availability

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- CO2 HSP are strongly temperature and pressure dependent, requiring T/P corrections that introduce uncertainty
- Chemical absorption (amine-CO2 reactions) dominates in many practical systems and is not captured by HSP distance
- HSP treats CO2 as a simple molecule, ignoring its Lewis acid character and specific interactions with basic solvents
- Ionic liquids have complex solvation mechanisms (cavity formation, specific anion-CO2 interactions) beyond simple HSP matching
- Multi-component gas mixtures (flue gas containing N2, O2, H2O, SOx, NOx) have selectivity requirements not addressed by CO2 HSP alone
- Absorption kinetics (mass transfer coefficients, interfacial area) are equally important as thermodynamic capacity but not predicted by HSP
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Chemical absorbents (MEA, DEA, MDEA) where chemical reaction enthalpy dominates and HSP distance poorly predicts capacity
- Functionalized ionic liquids (task-specific ILs with amine groups) where chemical and physical absorption coexist
- High-pressure CO2 systems (supercritical conditions) where CO2 HSP change dramatically and standard correlations fail
- Water-lean solvents where the absence of water fundamentally changes the absorption mechanism compared to aqueous systems
- **validation_method**: 
- Gravimetric or volumetric CO2 absorption measurement
- Henry's law constant determination via gas-liquid equilibrium
- Vapor-liquid equilibrium (VLE) measurements for CO2-solvent systems
- CO2 loading measurement (mol CO2/mol absorbent) at various T and P
- Absorption rate measurement in wetted-wall column or stirred cell
- Correlation analysis: Ra vs. measured Henry's constant across solvent series

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: binary
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Venkatraman, V.; Evjen, S.; Lethesh, K.C.; Raj, J.J.; Knuutila, H.K.; Fiksdahl, A. | **title**: Rapid, comprehensive screening of ionic liquids towards sustainable applications | **journal**: Sustainable Energy & Fuels | **year**: 2019 | **doi**: 10.1039/C8SE00543E
- **author**: Moganty, S.S.; Baltus, R.E. | **title**: Regular solution theory for low pressure carbon dioxide solubility in room temperature ionic liquids: ionic liquid solubility parameter from activation energy of viscosity | **journal**: Industrial & Engineering Chemistry Research | **year**: 2010 | **doi**: 10.1021/ie901837k
- **author**: Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook, 2nd Edition | **journal**: CRC Press | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1201/9781420006834
- **author**: Williams, L.L.; Rubin, J.B.; Edwards, H.W. | **title**: Calculation of Hansen solubility parameter values for a range of pressure and temperature conditions, including the supercritical fluid region | **journal**: Industrial & Engineering Chemistry Research | **year**: 2004 | **doi**: 10.1021/ie0497543
- **regulatory_relevance**: HSP-based absorbent screening supports CCS/CCU regulatory frameworks including EU ETS (Emissions Trading System) compliance, EPA Clean Air Act greenhouse gas regulations, and Paris Agreement NDC targets. REACH compliance required for novel absorbent solvents used in industrial carbon capture. IEA and IPCC guidelines for CCS technology assessment benefit from rapid solvent screening methods.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Power generation (post-combustion carbon capture)
- Oil and gas (natural gas sweetening, enhanced oil recovery)
- Chemical and petrochemical processing
- Cement and steel manufacturing (industrial CCS)
- Direct air capture (DAC) technology
- Biogas upgrading
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice)
- COSMOtherm (COSMO-RS based screening)
- Aspen Plus (process simulation with thermodynamic models)
- gPROMS (process modeling for CCS)
- ILTHERMO (NIST ionic liquid property database)

**Uncertain fields**: hsp_principle.typical_red_threshold, literature.first_reported_year, input_data.data_availability_score

#### Hydrogen Storage Material-Solvent Interaction
**Japanese**: 水素貯蔵材料-溶媒相互作用解析 | **Maturity**: emerging

> HSP analysis is applied to evaluate interactions between porous materials (metal-organic frameworks, porous organic polymers, covalent organic frameworks) and liquid organic hydrogen carrier (LOHC) molecules for hydrogen storage system design. By matching the HSP of LOHC candidates (e.g., N-ethylcarbazole, dibenzyltoluene, formic acid derivatives) with porous host materials, optimal material-carrier combinations can be identified for reversible hydrogen uptake and release. HSP distance predicts the wettability, adsorption affinity, and swelling behavior of porous polymers in contact with LOHC liquids, while for MOFs it aids in understanding guest molecule accommodation and diffusion within pore networks. This approach accelerates materials screening for LOHC-based hydrogen storage systems targeting mobile and stationary applications.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The interaction between LOHC molecules and porous host materials follows HSP affinity principles. For porous polymer supports, small Ra between the polymer matrix and the LOHC indicates good wetting and infiltration of the carrier liquid into the pore network, enabling efficient hydrogen loading/unloading cycles. For MOFs and COFs, HSP analysis provides a first-order approximation of guest-host affinity by comparing the organic linker HSP with the LOHC HSP. The framework's internal surface chemistry (hydrophobic vs. hydrophilic pores) can be characterized by HSP, and matching these with LOHC HSP predicts preferential adsorption. In catalytic dehydrogenation/hydrogenation systems, the HSP matching between LOHC, catalyst support material, and reaction solvent influences mass transfer and catalytic efficiency. The delta_p and delta_h components are particularly important for LOHCs containing heteroatoms (N, O) that participate in hydrogen bonding with framework sites.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_LOHC - dD_material)^2 + (dP_LOHC - dP_material)^2 + (dH_LOHC - dH_material)^2); RED = Ra / R0; Adsorption affinity ~ 1/Ra^2; Pore filling fraction ~ f(RED, pore volume, LOHC molar volume)
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: high

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP of LOHC molecules (hydrogenated and dehydrogenated forms)
- HSP of porous material (framework linker or polymer matrix)
- Pore size distribution and surface area of host material
- LOHC molar volume and molecular dimensions
- Operating temperature range (hydrogenation/dehydrogenation)
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for common organic molecules
- Group contribution methods for LOHC candidate HSP estimation
- Published MOF/COF linker HSP values
- Computational HSP from molecular dynamics or DFT calculations
- Experimental contact angle and wetting measurements for HSP determination
- **data_availability_score**: 2
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- BET surface area and pore volume of host material
- Hydrogen storage capacity (wt% H2) of LOHC
- Dehydrogenation/hydrogenation enthalpy
- Catalyst type and loading
- Thermal stability of LOHC and host material
- Viscosity of LOHC at operating temperature
- Cycling stability (number of charge-discharge cycles)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: qualitative
- **limitations**: HSP analysis for hydrogen storage systems is at an early stage with limited experimental validation. MOFs and COFs have complex internal surface chemistries that cannot be fully captured by three HSP parameters; pore geometry, open metal sites, and defect sites create specific interactions beyond HSP scope. The HSP of MOF frameworks are not well-established and vary significantly with topology and functional groups. LOHC hydrogenation/dehydrogenation involves chemical bond breaking and formation that is catalytically controlled, not predicted by thermodynamic HSP affinity. Temperature effects are large (reactions occur at 150-350 C) and HSP values at these elevated temperatures are poorly known. The approach works best as a pre-screening tool for material-LOHC compatibility rather than for predicting hydrogen storage performance directly.
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- MOFs with open metal sites where specific coordination interactions dominate over general HSP affinity
- LOHC decomposition or polymerization at dehydrogenation temperatures altering effective HSP
- Pore size exclusion effects where molecular dimensions matter more than HSP affinity
- Catalyst poisoning or deactivation not captured by thermodynamic HSP analysis
- Phase changes of LOHC (solidification of dehydrogenated form) not predicted by HSP
- **validation_method**: Gravimetric or volumetric hydrogen uptake measurements, LOHC sorption isotherms in porous materials, contact angle measurements for wetting assessment, in-situ XRD or neutron diffraction for guest molecule location, cycling stability tests over multiple hydrogenation-dehydrogenation cycles, TGA/DSC for thermal stability

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: high
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: binary
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Preuster, P.; Papp, C.; Wasserscheid, P. | **title**: Liquid Organic Hydrogen Carriers (LOHCs): Toward a Hydrogen-free Hydrogen Economy | **journal**: Accounts of Chemical Research | **year**: 2017 | **doi**: 10.1021/acs.accounts.6b00474
- **author**: Teichmann, D.; Arlt, W.; Wasserscheid, P.; Freymann, R. | **title**: A future energy supply based on Liquid Organic Hydrogen Carriers (LOHC) | **journal**: Energy & Environmental Science | **year**: 2011 | **doi**: 10.1039/c1ee01454d
- **author**: Abbott, S.; Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice (HSPiP) | **journal**: HSPiP Software Documentation | **year**: 2013 | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **author**: Zhang, L.; Jiang, K.; Yang, L.; Li, L.; Hu, E.; Yang, L.; Shao, K. | **title**: Hansen solubility parameter analysis of MOF-based mixed matrix membranes | **journal**: Journal of Membrane Science | **year**: 2020 | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **regulatory_relevance**: Hydrogen storage systems are subject to safety regulations including UN GTR No. 13 for hydrogen vehicles and NFPA 2 Hydrogen Technologies Code. HSP analysis itself is not regulated, but material compatibility assessments it supports are required under pressure equipment directives (EU PED 2014/68/EU). LOHC toxicity and environmental impact assessment under REACH may benefit from HSP-based solvent characterization.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Hydrogen energy and fuel cell technology
- Automotive (hydrogen fuel cell vehicles)
- Chemical hydrogen storage
- Stationary energy storage
- Industrial gas supply and distribution
- Academic materials research
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice)
- Materials Studio (molecular simulation of MOFs)
- COSMOtherm (thermodynamic property prediction)
- RASPA (molecular simulation of adsorption in porous materials)

**Uncertain fields**: hsp_principle.typical_red_threshold, literature.key_references[2].doi, literature.key_references[3].doi, literature.first_reported_year, input_data.data_availability_score
