#### Li-ion Battery Electrolyte Design
**Japanese**: リチウムイオン電池電解液設計 | **Maturity**: emerging

> Electrolyte salt-solvent HSP compatibility predicts solvation shell structure, ion transport properties, and solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI) formation characteristics in lithium-ion batteries. HSP matching between lithium salts and organic carbonate solvents guides solvent selection and blending to optimize ionic conductivity, electrochemical stability window, and cycle life. The approach extends to next-generation electrolytes including solid-state, ionic liquid, and deep eutectic solvent systems.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: In lithium-ion battery electrolytes, the dissolution and solvation of lithium salts (LiPF6, LiBF4, LiTFSI) in organic solvents (EC, DMC, DEC, EMC) depend on thermodynamic compatibility quantifiable through HSP. The HSP distance between the salt and solvent predicts solvation strength: solvents with HSP close to the salt HSP (particularly in dP and dH components) form strong solvation shells around Li+ ions, promoting salt dissociation and ionic conductivity. However, too-strong solvation (very low Ra) can impede desolvation at the electrode interface, increasing charge-transfer resistance. The HSP framework also predicts solvent-solvent miscibility in blended electrolytes (e.g., EC/DMC), solvent-polymer interactions in gel polymer electrolytes, and solvent-SEI compatibility that governs passivation layer quality. Solvent HSP, particularly the dP and dH components, correlate with the solvent's ability to coordinate Li+ and influence the SEI composition through preferential decomposition of high-dP solvents like EC at the anode surface.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_salt-dD_solvent)^2 + (dP_salt-dP_solvent)^2 + (dH_salt-dH_solvent)^2); Ionic conductivity sigma correlates with salt-solvent compatibility; Donor number and dH are correlated for solvation strength
- **red_direction**: threshold_specific
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: high

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- Lithium salt HSP (dD, dP, dH) or ion-pair HSP estimates
- Solvent HSP (dD, dP, dH) for each electrolyte component
- Solvent blend composition (volume or mole fractions)
- Operating temperature range
- Salt concentration
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for common organic carbonate solvents
- Published HSP for battery solvents (EC, DMC, DEC, EMC, PC, FEC)
- Group contribution methods for fluorinated solvent HSP
- Computational chemistry (DFT, COSMO-RS) for salt and additive HSP estimation
- Experimental solubility screening for novel salt-solvent combinations
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Solvent dielectric constant and Gutmann donor/acceptor numbers
- Solvent electrochemical stability window (oxidation/reduction potentials)
- Solvent viscosity (affects ion mobility)
- Salt concentration and degree of dissociation
- Li+ transference number
- Electrode surface chemistry and SEI composition
- Operating voltage range and C-rate requirements

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: qualitative
- **limitations**: 
- HSP treats salt solvation as a bulk thermodynamic property, but Li+ solvation is dominated by specific coordination chemistry (first solvation shell structure) not fully captured by HSP
- Electrochemical decomposition of solvents at electrode surfaces is kinetically controlled and depends on electrode potential, not just HSP
- SEI formation involves complex multi-step reactions where HSP provides only indirect guidance through solvent reactivity trends
- Ion-ion interactions at typical battery concentrations (1M+) cause significant deviation from dilute-solution HSP predictions
- HSP does not capture the anion contribution to solvation structure and conductivity (contact ion pairs vs. solvent-separated ion pairs)
- Solid-state and polymer electrolytes involve additional factors (crystallinity, segmental motion, grain boundaries) beyond HSP
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Highly concentrated electrolytes (>3M) where ion-ion interactions dominate and the bulk solvent HSP concept breaks down
- Electrolyte additives (FEC, VC) that function through electrochemical decomposition kinetics rather than thermodynamic compatibility
- Ionic liquid electrolytes where the IL itself has tunable HSP but ion transport mechanisms differ fundamentally from molecular solvents
- Solid-state electrolytes where grain boundary resistance and mechanical properties dominate over HSP-like compatibility
- **validation_method**: 
- Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) for ionic conductivity
- Cyclic voltammetry for electrochemical stability window
- Li+ transference number measurement (Bruce-Vincent method)
- XPS and cryo-TEM for SEI composition and morphology analysis
- Galvanostatic cycling for capacity retention and Coulombic efficiency
- Raman spectroscopy for solvation shell structure characterization

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: high
- **ui_pattern**: optimization
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, optimizeBlend
- **multi_component_complexity**: multi
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Xu, K. | **title**: Nonaqueous liquid electrolytes for lithium-based rechargeable batteries | **journal**: Chemical Reviews | **year**: 2004 | **doi**: 10.1021/cr030203g
- **author**: Xu, K. | **title**: Electrolytes and interphases in Li-ion batteries and beyond | **journal**: Chemical Reviews | **year**: 2014 | **doi**: 10.1021/cr500003w
- **author**: Venkatraman, V.; Evjen, S.; Lethesh, K.C.; Raj, J.J.; Knuutila, H.K.; Fiksdahl, A. | **title**: Screening ionic liquids for CO2 capture using Hansen solubility parameters | **journal**: International Journal of Molecular Sciences | **year**: 2019 | **doi**: 10.3390/ijms20215151
- **author**: Lee, S.; Park, S.S. | **title**: Dielectric constants of binary mixtures of propylene carbonate with dimethyl carbonate and ethylene carbonate used in lithium-ion battery electrolytes | **journal**: Journal of Physical Chemistry B | **year**: 2021 | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **author**: Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook, 2nd Edition | **journal**: CRC Press | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1201/9781420006834
- **regulatory_relevance**: HSP-based electrolyte design supports compliance with UN transport regulations for lithium batteries (UN 38.3 testing requirements) by optimizing electrolyte stability. EU Battery Regulation (2023/1542) requirements for battery passport and sustainability declarations benefit from rational electrolyte design reducing hazardous solvent use. REACH registration of electrolyte solvents and salts requires understanding of mixture properties.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Lithium-ion battery manufacturing (automotive, consumer electronics, grid storage)
- Electrolyte material suppliers
- Battery R&D laboratories
- Electric vehicle OEMs
- Next-generation battery development (solid-state, Li-S, Li-air)
- **commercial_tools**: HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice), COSMOtherm (COSMO-RS based property prediction), Materials Design MedeA (molecular simulation), Scienomics MAPS platform, Battery Design Studio (CD-adapco)

**Uncertain fields**: hsp_principle.typical_red_threshold, accuracy.accuracy_level, literature.key_references[3].doi, literature.first_reported_year

#### Membrane Separation Selectivity
**Japanese**: 膜分離選択性予測 | **Maturity**: established

> HSP distance differences between a target permeate, an impurity (or rejected species), and the membrane material are used to predict separation selectivity factors in liquid-phase membrane processes. By calculating Ra values for target-membrane and impurity-membrane pairs, the relative affinity of each species for the membrane matrix can be quantified. A membrane material with low Ra to the target permeate and high Ra to the impurity will preferentially dissolve and transport the target, yielding high separation factors. This approach is applied in pervaporation, organic solvent nanofiltration (OSN), and liquid membrane processes for applications including solvent dehydration, organic-organic separation, and wastewater treatment.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Membrane separation selectivity in solution-diffusion membranes depends on differential solubility and diffusivity of components in the membrane material. HSP distance Ra between each permeating species and the membrane polymer quantifies thermodynamic affinity: smaller Ra means higher solubility in the membrane phase. The selectivity factor alpha is approximated by the ratio of solubility coefficients, which correlate inversely with Ra values. For pervaporation and OSN, the solubility selectivity component often dominates, making HSP-based screening particularly effective. The difference Delta_Ra = Ra(impurity-membrane) - Ra(target-membrane) serves as a selectivity predictor: larger positive Delta_Ra indicates better separation. For swelling-controlled transport, RED values help identify membranes that resist excessive swelling by the feed mixture while maintaining adequate permeability for the target.
- **key_equation**: Ra_i = sqrt(4*(dD_i - dD_membrane)^2 + (dP_i - dP_membrane)^2 + (dH_i - dH_membrane)^2); Selectivity predictor: Delta_Ra = Ra_impurity - Ra_target; Separation factor alpha ~ exp((Ra_impurity^2 - Ra_target^2) * V_m / (R * T))
- **red_direction**: threshold_specific
- **typical_red_threshold**: RED(target-membrane) < 1.0 for adequate permeability; RED(impurity-membrane) > 1.2 for good rejection; Delta_Ra > 3 MPa^0.5 for meaningful selectivity
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: high

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP of target permeate (delta_d, delta_p, delta_h)
- HSP of impurity/rejected species (delta_d, delta_p, delta_h)
- HSP and interaction radius R0 of membrane polymer
- Molar volumes of permeating species
- Operating temperature
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for common solvents and polymers
- Published pervaporation membrane HSP data
- Group contribution methods for novel polymers (Van Krevelen, Hoftyzer)
- Experimental swelling tests to determine membrane HSP and R0
- Literature compilations of organic solvent HSP
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Membrane thickness and morphology
- Polymer degree of crosslinking
- Feed composition and activity coefficients
- Molecular size and shape of permeants (for diffusivity selectivity)
- Membrane swelling degree
- Operating pressure (for pressure-driven processes)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: HSP-based selectivity prediction primarily captures solubility selectivity and does not account for diffusivity selectivity, which depends on molecular size, shape, and membrane free volume distribution. For rigid glassy polymer membranes, size-sieving can dominate selectivity, reducing HSP predictive accuracy. Membrane swelling by feed mixtures changes the polymer matrix HSP in situ, creating a coupling effect not captured by static HSP values. Crosslinked membranes have modified HSP compared to their linear precursors, and crosslink density effects are not directly modeled. Multi-component feed interactions (coupling effects, competitive sorption) can cause deviations from binary HSP predictions. Concentration polarization and boundary layer effects at the membrane surface are not addressed by thermodynamic HSP analysis.
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Size-sieving dominated separations in tight nanofiltration membranes where diffusivity selectivity overrides solubility selectivity
- Highly swollen membranes where in-situ HSP deviate significantly from dry membrane values
- Azeotropic or close-boiling mixtures where both components have similar HSP to the membrane
- Mixed feed effects with strong non-ideal solution behavior in the membrane phase
- Ceramic or inorganic membranes where surface adsorption rather than bulk dissolution controls selectivity
- **validation_method**: Pervaporation or nanofiltration experiments measuring flux and separation factor for target/impurity mixtures, sorption-desorption experiments for individual component uptake, swelling degree measurements in pure and mixed solvents

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: matrix
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Mulder, M.H.V.; Smolders, C.A. | **title**: On the mechanism of separation of ethanol/water mixtures by pervaporation | **journal**: Journal of Membrane Science | **year**: 1986 | **doi**: 10.1016/S0376-7388(00)80300-5
- **author**: Jonquières, A.; Clément, R.; Lochon, P.; Néel, J.; Dresch, M.; Chrétien, B. | **title**: Industrial state-of-the-art of pervaporation and vapour permeation in the western countries | **journal**: Journal of Membrane Science | **year**: 2002 | **doi**: 10.1016/S0376-7388(01)00588-5
- **author**: Mathias, P.M.; Cheng, Y.; Coleman, S. | **title**: Solubility parameters and polymer membrane selection for pervaporation | **journal**: Journal of Membrane Science | **year**: 2010 | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **author**: Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook, 2nd Edition | **journal**: CRC Press | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1201/9781420006834
- **first_reported_year**: 1986
- **regulatory_relevance**: No direct regulatory mandate for HSP-based membrane selection, but pervaporation membranes for solvent dehydration in pharmaceutical manufacturing must comply with GMP requirements. HSP screening supports solvent selection under REACH and ICH Q3C guidelines for residual solvent control. EU and EPA water treatment regulations drive membrane development where HSP screening aids material selection.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Water treatment and desalination
- Pharmaceutical solvent recovery
- Petrochemical separation
- Biofuel purification (bioethanol dehydration)
- Food and beverage processing
- Organic solvent nanofiltration
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice)
- COSMOtherm (COSMO-RS based membrane screening)
- Aspen Plus (process simulation with membrane modules)
- MATLAB/Python custom scripts for multi-component HSP optimization

**Uncertain fields**: literature.key_references[2].doi, hsp_principle.typical_red_threshold

#### PCM Encapsulation for Thermal Storage
**Japanese**: 蓄熱用PCMカプセル化 | **Maturity**: emerging

> HSP matching between phase-change materials (PCMs) and shell/matrix polymers is used to predict encapsulation compatibility and long-term cycling stability in thermal energy storage systems. PCMs (paraffins, fatty acids, salt hydrates) undergo solid-liquid transitions to store/release latent heat. Microencapsulation in polymer shells (melamine-formaldehyde, PMMA, polyurea) protects the PCM from leakage. HSP compatibility between the molten PCM core and the shell polymer determines interfacial adhesion, shell integrity under thermal cycling, and resistance to PCM permeation through the shell wall. A moderate HSP distance ensures the shell does not dissolve in or swell excessively with the PCM, while sufficient affinity maintains adhesion. This approach enables rational shell polymer selection and predicts long-term durability of encapsulated PCM systems.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The principle relies on controlling the HSP distance between the molten PCM core and the polymeric shell material. If the Ra distance is too small (RED << 1), the shell polymer may swell or dissolve in the PCM, leading to shell failure. If Ra is too large (RED >> 1), poor interfacial adhesion results in delamination and cracking during thermal cycling. An optimal intermediate RED range exists where the shell maintains structural integrity while providing sufficient adhesion to the PCM. For paraffin-based PCMs (dominated by dD), shell polymers with moderate polar and hydrogen bonding components create an appropriate mismatch that prevents dissolution while crosslinked networks resist swelling. The HSP approach also guides selection of surfactants and emulsifiers used during the encapsulation process (e.g., in situ polymerization, coacervation) by matching surfactant HSP to both core and shell phases.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_PCM - dD_shell)^2 + (dP_PCM - dP_shell)^2 + (dH_PCM - dH_shell)^2); RED = Ra/R0_shell; Swelling ratio correlates with 1/Ra^2
- **red_direction**: threshold_specific
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) of the PCM in liquid state
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) of the shell polymer
- Interaction radius R0 of the shell polymer
- PCM melting temperature and operating temperature range
- Shell polymer crosslink density (if crosslinked)
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for common paraffins and polymers
- Group contribution methods for PCM HSP estimation (Van Krevelen)
- Literature values for paraffin wax HSP (Archer 1991, Hansen 2007)
- Experimental solubility/swelling tests for shell polymer sphere fitting
- Supplier datasheets for commercial PCM products
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- PCM melting point and latent heat of fusion
- Shell polymer Tg and crosslink density
- Thermal expansion coefficient mismatch between core and shell
- Shell thickness and mechanical strength
- Volume change of PCM upon phase transition
- Interfacial tension between PCM and shell

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- HSP values for PCMs change between solid and liquid states; liquid-state values at operating temperature are needed but less commonly tabulated
- Crosslinked shell polymers have effectively larger R0 (more solvent-resistant), which modifies RED interpretation
- Volume expansion of PCM during melting creates mechanical stress not captured by HSP compatibility alone
- Salt hydrate PCMs have very different HSP profiles and may involve ionic interactions beyond HSP framework
- Long-term cycling degradation involves chemical aging, not just thermodynamic compatibility
- Microencapsulation process kinetics (polymerization rate, emulsion stability) also determine shell quality
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Paraffin PCMs with low-Tg shell polymers where RED < 0.5 leads to progressive shell softening and leakage over cycling
- Salt hydrate PCMs where ionic species interact with shell material through mechanisms not captured by HSP
- Shape-stabilized PCMs in porous matrices where capillary effects dominate over HSP-predicted compatibility
- High-temperature PCMs (>100C) where polymer shell degradation occurs before HSP-predicted incompatibility
- **validation_method**: 
- DSC cycling tests (1000+ melt-freeze cycles) monitoring latent heat retention
- SEM/TEM imaging of capsule morphology before and after cycling
- Leakage testing by mass loss measurement on filter paper
- Swelling ratio measurement of shell polymer in molten PCM
- TGA for thermal stability assessment of encapsulated PCM

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: binary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- Salaun, F.; Devaux, E.; Bourbigot, S.; Rumeau, P. 'Influence of the Solvent on the Microencapsulation of an Hydrated Salt', Carbohydrate Polymers, 2010, 79, 964-974
- Hansen, C.M. 'Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook', 2nd Ed., CRC Press, 2007
- Su, W.; Darkwa, J.; Kokogiannakis, G. 'Review of Solid-Liquid Phase Change Materials and Their Encapsulation Technologies', Renewable and Sustainable Energy Reviews, 2015, 48, 373-391
- Zhang, H.; Wang, X. 'Fabrication and Performances of Microencapsulated Phase Change Materials Based on n-Octadecane Core and Resorcinol-Modified Melamine-Formaldehyde Shell', Colloids and Surfaces A, 2009, 332, 129-138
- Jurkowska, M.; Szczygiel, I. 'Review on Properties of Microencapsulated Phase Change Materials Slurries', Applied Thermal Engineering, 2016, 98, 365-373

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Building and construction (thermal energy storage in walls and ceilings)
- Textiles (thermoregulating fabrics with microencapsulated PCM)
- Electronics (thermal management of batteries and power electronics)
- Solar energy (thermal storage for concentrated solar power)
- Cold chain logistics (temperature-controlled packaging)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (polymer-solvent compatibility module)
- Materials Studio (molecular dynamics for interfacial compatibility)
- COMSOL Multiphysics (coupled thermal-mechanical modeling of capsules)
- Rubitherm (commercial PCM supplier with material datasheets)
- Phase Change Energy Solutions (encapsulated PCM product design tools)

**Uncertain fields**: first_reported_year, typical_red_threshold, data_availability_score, regulatory_relevance

#### Perovskite Solar Cell Solvent Engineering
**Japanese**: ペロブスカイト太陽電池溶媒工学 | **Maturity**: emerging

> HSP analysis guides selection of processing solvents and anti-solvents for perovskite thin-film deposition, critically affecting film morphology, grain size, crystallinity, and defect density. The HSP distance between the perovskite precursor complex (e.g., PbI2-DMSO or MAI-PbI2-DMF adducts) and the processing solvent determines dissolution quality, while the HSP distance between the anti-solvent and the wet precursor film controls nucleation kinetics and crystal growth. Optimal solvent systems produce uniform, pinhole-free perovskite films with large grain sizes, directly improving power conversion efficiency (PCE) and device stability. HSP-based screening accelerates solvent system discovery, reducing the experimental search space for new perovskite compositions including mixed-halide and all-inorganic variants.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Perovskite precursors form Lewis acid-base adducts with coordinating solvents (DMF, DMSO, NMP), and the stability of these adducts correlates with HSP affinity. The processing solvent must have low Ra to the precursor complex to ensure complete dissolution and homogeneous solution. During anti-solvent dripping, a solvent with high Ra to the perovskite but miscibility with the processing solvent is introduced, rapidly reducing precursor solubility and triggering supersaturation-driven nucleation. The HSP difference between processing solvent and anti-solvent (Ra_solvent-antisolvent) influences the rate of solvent exchange and thus nucleation density: moderate Ra produces controlled nucleation with large grains, while very high Ra causes rapid precipitation with small grains. The hydrogen bonding parameter delta_h is particularly important because it governs the donor-acceptor interactions between solvents and Pb2+ centers in the precursor. Solvent blending with calculated HSP enables fine control of intermediate phase formation and crystallization pathway.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_solvent - dD_precursor)^2 + (dP_solvent - dP_precursor)^2 + (dH_solvent - dH_precursor)^2); Anti-solvent criterion: Ra(anti-solvent, precursor) >> Ra(processing solvent, precursor); Nucleation rate ~ exp(-Delta_G* / kT) where Delta_G* depends on supersaturation controlled by Ra(anti-solvent, processing solvent)
- **red_direction**: threshold_specific
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP of perovskite precursor complex (delta_d, delta_p, delta_h)
- HSP of candidate processing solvents
- HSP of candidate anti-solvents
- Boiling point and vapor pressure of solvents
- Donor number (DN) of coordinating solvents
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for common organic solvents
- Published perovskite precursor HSP from dissolution testing
- Group contribution estimation for novel solvent HSP
- Literature HSP for DMF, DMSO, GBL, NMP, and common anti-solvents
- Experimental solubility sphere determination via systematic dissolution tests
- **data_availability_score**: 2
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Gutmann donor number (DN) of solvents
- Solvent boiling point and evaporation rate
- Solvent viscosity (affects film thickness uniformity)
- Solvent-substrate surface energy matching
- Precursor concentration and stoichiometry
- Substrate temperature during deposition
- Ambient humidity during processing

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: HSP analysis captures thermodynamic solvent-precursor affinity but does not fully describe the complex crystallization kinetics of perovskite formation, including intermediate phase transitions (e.g., delta to alpha phase). Perovskite precursors are ionic/coordination compounds whose interactions extend beyond van der Waals, polar, and hydrogen bonding forces captured by HSP. The strong Lewis acid-base coordination between Pb2+ and donor solvents is only partially reflected in delta_h. HSP of perovskite precursor complexes are composition-dependent and not well-tabulated, requiring experimental determination for each new formulation. Film morphology depends on many non-HSP factors including spin speed, substrate wetting, anti-solvent drip timing, and ambient conditions. Degradation pathways (moisture, heat, light) are not predicted by HSP.
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Precursor complexes with strong specific coordination (e.g., DMSO-PbI2 adducts) where Lewis acid-base interactions dominate over general HSP affinity
- Mixed-cation perovskites (FA/MA/Cs) where multiple precursor species have different HSP
- Anti-solvent volume and timing effects that override thermodynamic HSP predictions
- High-humidity environments where water ingress during processing alters crystallization regardless of solvent HSP
- Scalable deposition methods (slot-die, blade coating) where fluid dynamics matter more than equilibrium solubility
- **validation_method**: SEM and AFM imaging of perovskite film morphology (grain size, coverage, pinhole density), XRD for crystallinity and phase purity, UV-Vis absorption and photoluminescence for optical quality, solar cell J-V characterization for PCE, stability testing under ISOS protocols

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: high
- **ui_pattern**: optimization
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, optimizeBlend, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Jeon, N.J.; Noh, J.H.; Kim, Y.C.; Yang, W.S.; Ryu, S.; Seok, S.I. | **title**: Solvent engineering for high-performance inorganic-organic hybrid perovskite solar cells | **journal**: Nature Materials | **year**: 2014 | **doi**: 10.1038/nmat4014
- **author**: Hamill, J.C.; Schwartz, J.; Loo, Y.-L. | **title**: Influence of Solvent Coordination on Hybrid Organic-Inorganic Perovskite Formation | **journal**: ACS Energy Letters | **year**: 2018 | **doi**: 10.1021/acsenergylett.7b01083
- **author**: Noel, N.K.; Habisreutinger, S.N.; Wenger, B.; Elliott, M.T.; Sherber, A.; Snaith, H.J. | **title**: A low viscosity, low boiling point, clean solvent system for the rapid crystallisation of highly specular perovskite films | **journal**: Energy & Environmental Science | **year**: 2017 | **doi**: 10.1039/C6EE02373H
- **author**: Cao, X.; Zhang, G.; Jiang, L.; Cai, Y.; Wang, Y.; He, X.; Zeng, Q.; Chen, J.; Jia, Y.; Wei, J. | **title**: Achieving environment-friendly production of CsPbBr3 films for efficient solar cells via HSP analysis | **journal**: Green Chemistry | **year**: 2021 | **doi**: 10.1039/D0GC04043F
- **first_reported_year**: 2014
- **regulatory_relevance**: Lead-containing perovskites fall under RoHS and REACH restrictions on hazardous substances. HSP-guided green solvent substitution supports compliance with EU Directive 2011/65/EU (RoHS) and worker safety regulations by replacing toxic solvents (DMF, NMP) with safer alternatives. No regulatory body currently mandates HSP analysis, but it aids in meeting VOC emission standards and workplace exposure limits.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Photovoltaic manufacturing
- Thin-film electronics
- Academic perovskite research
- Building-integrated photovoltaics (BIPV)
- Flexible electronics and wearable energy harvesting
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice)
- COSMOtherm (solvent screening and sigma profiles)
- Scienomics MAPS (molecular simulation for perovskite systems)
- GreatCell Solar / Solaronix (perovskite materials suppliers with formulation guides)

**Uncertain fields**: hsp_principle.typical_red_threshold, input_data.data_availability_score

#### Soil Contaminant Extraction
**Japanese**: 土壌汚染物質抽出最適化 | **Maturity**: established

> HSP matching between soil contaminants and extraction solvents is used to optimize environmental remediation processes. By minimizing the HSP distance Ra between the target contaminant (e.g., PAHs, PCBs, petroleum hydrocarbons, pesticides) and the extraction solvent, maximum extraction efficiency can be achieved. This approach enables rational solvent selection for soil washing, Soxhlet extraction, accelerated solvent extraction (ASE), and supercritical fluid extraction. HSP-based screening replaces trial-and-error solvent testing, reducing the number of experiments needed and enabling the selection of greener, less toxic extraction solvents that maintain high recovery rates for target contaminants.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The extraction of organic contaminants from soil follows the 'like dissolves like' principle quantified by HSP. A solvent with HSP close to the target contaminant (small Ra) will maximize the contaminant's solubility in the extracting phase, promoting its desorption from soil particles. The RED number (Ra/R0) predicts whether a solvent falls within the contaminant's solubility sphere. For soil-bound contaminants, the effective interaction also involves overcoming adsorption forces between the contaminant and soil organic matter (SOM). The HSP of SOM can be estimated to model contaminant-soil affinity: contaminants with small Ra to SOM are more tightly bound and harder to extract. The optimal extraction solvent must have low Ra to the contaminant and ideally high Ra to SOM to avoid excessive SOM co-extraction. Solvent blending using volume-weighted HSP averaging enables fine-tuning of extraction selectivity.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_solvent - dD_contaminant)^2 + (dP_solvent - dP_contaminant)^2 + (dH_solvent - dH_contaminant)^2); RED = Ra / R0; Extraction efficiency ~ f(1/Ra_solvent-contaminant); Blend HSP: delta_blend = sum(phi_i * delta_i)
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: RED < 1.0 for effective contaminant dissolution; RED < 0.7 preferred for high extraction recovery (>90%)
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP of target contaminant(s) (delta_d, delta_p, delta_h)
- HSP of candidate extraction solvents
- Contaminant solubility sphere radius R0
- Soil organic matter content and estimated HSP
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for solvents and common organic compounds
- Published HSP for PAHs, PCBs, BTEX, and pesticides
- Group contribution methods (Van Krevelen) for novel contaminant HSP estimation
- Literature values for soil organic matter HSP
- UNIFAC or COSMO-RS for verification of HSP-based predictions
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Soil organic carbon content (foc)
- Soil particle size distribution and clay content
- Contaminant aging and sequestration degree
- Solvent viscosity and surface tension (for soil wetting)
- Extraction temperature and contact time
- Soil moisture content
- pH of extraction medium

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: HSP-based solvent selection primarily addresses the thermodynamic affinity between contaminant and solvent but does not account for mass transfer limitations in soil matrices. Contaminant accessibility is controlled by soil structure, micropore diffusion, and aging effects that reduce bioavailability and extractability over time. HSP cannot predict kinetic aspects of extraction such as desorption rates from soil organic matter or intra-particle diffusion. Soil heterogeneity means that contaminant-soil interactions vary spatially. For ionizable contaminants (phenols, amines), pH-dependent speciation affects HSP values. Heavy metal contaminants cannot be addressed by HSP. Co-extraction of non-target soil organic matter is difficult to predict quantitatively with HSP alone.
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Highly aged contaminants with strong sequestration in soil micropores where mass transfer rather than thermodynamics limits extraction
- Ionizable contaminants (weak acids/bases) where pH-dependent speciation changes effective HSP
- Mixed contamination sites with vastly different contaminant HSP requiring compromise solvent selection
- High-clay soils where mineral surface adsorption (not organic partitioning) dominates contaminant binding
- Volatile contaminants lost during extraction due to evaporation rather than solubility limitations
- **validation_method**: Sequential extraction experiments with varying solvents, Soxhlet extraction or ASE with GC-MS or HPLC quantification of extracted contaminants, comparison of HSP-predicted solvent ranking with measured extraction recoveries, soil column leaching tests

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk, optimizeBlend
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Brandt, H.C.A.; de Groot, P.C. | **title**: A laboratory rig for studying aspects of worker exposure to bitumen fumes | **journal**: American Industrial Hygiene Association Journal | **year**: 1999 | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **author**: Batista, E.R.; Hornof, V. | **title**: Solvent selection for soil decontamination using Hansen solubility parameters | **journal**: Environmental Technology | **year**: 2005 | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **author**: Khodadoust, A.P.; Bagchi, R.; Suidan, M.T.; Brenner, R.C.; Sellers, N.G. | **title**: Removal of PAHs from highly contaminated soils found at prior manufactured gas operations | **journal**: Journal of Hazardous Materials | **year**: 2000 | **doi**: 10.1016/S0304-3894(99)00099-2
- **author**: Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook, 2nd Edition | **journal**: CRC Press | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1201/9781420006834
- **regulatory_relevance**: EPA Superfund remediation guidelines reference solvent extraction as a treatment technology. REACH regulations influence solvent choice by restricting hazardous solvents, making HSP-based green solvent substitution valuable. ISO 18287 and EPA Method 3540C/3545A for soil extraction do not mandate HSP but benefit from HSP-guided solvent optimization. Environmental remediation standards in EU (Soil Framework Directive) and Japan (Soil Contamination Countermeasures Act) drive demand for efficient extraction methods.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Environmental remediation and consulting
- Petroleum industry (contaminated site cleanup)
- Analytical chemistry laboratories
- Mining and resource extraction
- Agricultural soil testing
- Waste management and treatment
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice)
- COSMOtherm (solvent screening for extraction)
- TURBOMOLE (quantum chemical solvent design)
- EPA ProUCL (statistical analysis of contamination data)

**Uncertain fields**: literature.key_references[0].doi, literature.key_references[1].doi, literature.first_reported_year

#### Supercritical CO2 Co-solvent Selection
**Japanese**: 超臨界CO2共溶媒選択 | **Maturity**: established

> HSP-based entrainer (co-solvent/modifier) selection to extend the solvation range of supercritical CO2 (scCO2) extraction toward polar solutes. Pure scCO2 has low polarity (dP ~ 3.5, dH ~ 5.7 MPa^0.5 at typical operating conditions), which limits its ability to dissolve polar compounds. By adding a small fraction (1-10 mol%) of a polar co-solvent (e.g., ethanol, methanol, acetone), the effective HSP of the scCO2 mixture shifts toward the target solute's HSP sphere, enabling dissolution. HSP matching between the co-solvent-modified scCO2 and the target solute guides co-solvent selection and concentration optimization. This is a key green chemistry approach replacing conventional organic solvent extraction in food, pharmaceutical, and natural product industries.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The core principle is that scCO2 possesses definable but pressure- and temperature-dependent HSP values, primarily dominated by dispersion forces (dD ~ 15.6 MPa^0.5 at 200 bar, 40 C) with modest polar and hydrogen bonding components. The HSP of CO2 shifts significantly with density (and hence T and P). By adding a co-solvent, the mixture HSP can be estimated via volume-fraction-weighted linear mixing: dD_mix = phi_CO2*dD_CO2 + phi_cosolv*dD_cosolv, and similarly for dP and dH. The target is to minimize the Ra distance between the modified scCO2 mixture and the solute of interest. The RED number (Ra/R0 of the solute) predicts whether the modified scCO2 falls inside the solubility sphere of the target compound. Co-solvent selection is thus an optimization problem: find the entrainer that moves the scCO2 HSP vector most efficiently toward the solute while maintaining supercritical phase behavior and process economics.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_mix - dD_solute)^2 + (dP_mix - dP_solute)^2 + (dH_mix - dH_solute)^2); dD_mix = phi_CO2*dD_CO2(T,P) + phi_cos*dD_cos; RED = Ra/R0_solute
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: high

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) of pure CO2 at operating T and P
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) of candidate co-solvents
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) and R0 of target solute
- Operating temperature and pressure
- Co-solvent mole fraction or volume fraction
- **data_sources**: 
- Williams et al. (2004) tabulated CO2 HSP as function of T and P
- HSPiP database for co-solvent HSP values
- Experimental solubility data in scCO2 for sphere fitting
- Equation of state (Peng-Robinson) for CO2 density at T,P
- Group contribution methods for novel solute HSP estimation
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- CO2 density at operating conditions (critical for HSP estimation)
- Co-solvent vapor pressure and miscibility with CO2
- Phase equilibrium data (VLE) for CO2-cosolvent binary
- Solute melting point and sublimation pressure
- Mass transfer coefficients for extraction kinetics

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- CO2 HSP values are highly density-dependent; small errors in density estimation propagate to HSP and Ra
- Linear mixing rule for HSP of CO2-cosolvent mixture is an approximation; non-ideal mixing and clustering of co-solvent molecules around solute can occur
- At near-critical conditions, large compressibility causes steep HSP gradients with small T/P changes
- Does not account for specific chemical interactions (e.g., co-solvent-solute hydrogen bonding complexation) beyond what HSP captures
- Entrainer effect magnitude often exceeds what simple HSP mixing predicts due to local composition enhancement near the solute
- Kinetic and mass-transfer limitations in real extraction are not addressed
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Highly polar solutes (e.g., sugars, amino acids) where even maximum co-solvent addition cannot bring RED below 1.0
- Systems where co-solvent forms azeotrope-like behavior with CO2, making mixture HSP estimation unreliable
- Multi-solute extraction where selectivity depends on small HSP differences masked by co-solvent broadening of the solubility sphere
- Near-critical anomalies where clustering effects dominate over bulk HSP predictions
- **validation_method**: 
- Static or dynamic solubility measurement at controlled T and P
- Cloud point pressure determination with varying co-solvent fraction
- Gravimetric extraction yield comparison across co-solvents
- HPLC/GC analysis of extract composition and purity

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: high
- **ui_pattern**: optimization
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk, optimizeBlend
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- Williams, L.L.; Rubin, J.B.; Edwards, H.W. 'Calculation of Hansen Solubility Parameter Values for a Range of Pressure and Temperature Conditions, Including the Supercritical Fluid Region', Ind. Eng. Chem. Res., 2004, 43, 4967-4972
- Hansen, C.M. 'Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook', 2nd Ed., CRC Press, 2007, Chapter 15 (Supercritical Fluids)
- Dobbs, J.M.; Wong, J.M.; Johnston, K.P. 'Nonpolar Co-Solvents for Solubility Enhancement in Supercritical Fluid Carbon Dioxide', J. Chem. Eng. Data, 1986, 31, 303-308
- Gupta, R.B.; Shim, J.J. 'Solubility in Supercritical Carbon Dioxide', CRC Press, 2007
- Brennecke, J.F.; Eckert, C.A. 'Phase Equilibria for Supercritical Fluid Process Design', AIChE J., 1989, 35, 1409-1427

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Food processing (decaffeination, hop extraction, nutraceutical isolation)
- Pharmaceutical (API purification, natural product extraction)
- Cosmetics (essential oil and lipid extraction)
- Environmental remediation (soil decontamination with scCO2)
- Chemical manufacturing (green solvent replacement programs)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (supercritical fluid module with T/P-dependent CO2 HSP)
- Aspen Plus (equation of state modeling for scCO2 systems)
- COSMOtherm (COSMO-RS predictions for scCO2 solubility)
- SuperPro Designer (scCO2 process simulation)
- NIST Webbook (CO2 thermophysical property data)

**Uncertain fields**: first_reported_year, typical_red_threshold, regulatory_relevance

### Category: Food/Cosmetics

#### Cleaning Product Formulation
**Japanese**: 洗浄製品処方設計 | **Maturity**: established

> HSP matching between soil/stain types and surfactant/solvent cleaning systems predicts cleaning efficacy and guides formulation optimization. By characterizing the HSP of target soils (greases, oils, waxes, inks, protein-based stains) and matching them with cleaning agents, formulators can rationally select solvents, co-solvents, and surfactants that maximize soil removal. This approach is increasingly important for developing effective green cleaning products that replace hazardous solvents while maintaining performance.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Cleaning efficacy fundamentally depends on the thermodynamic affinity between the cleaning agent and the soil to be removed. When the HSP of the cleaning solvent or the hydrophobic tail environment of a surfactant micelle closely matches the HSP of the soil, dissolution or emulsification is thermodynamically favorable. The Ra distance between soil HSP and solvent HSP predicts the cleaning power: smaller Ra means the soil dissolves more readily in the cleaning medium. For surfactant-based systems, the effective HSP of the micellar interior (dominated by the hydrophobic tail structure) determines which soils can be solubilized. Mixed solvent systems and hydrotrope-assisted formulations can be optimized by adjusting the blend HSP to match diverse soil types. The approach enables a two-step strategy: first identify the soil HSP, then screen or blend solvents/surfactants to minimize Ra.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_soil-dD_solvent)^2 + (dP_soil-dP_solvent)^2 + (dH_soil-dH_solvent)^2); RED = Ra/R0_soil; Cleaning efficiency inversely proportional to RED
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: RED < 1.0 for effective soil removal; RED < 0.8 for rapid cleaning
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- Soil/stain HSP (dD, dP, dH) - varies by soil type
- Soil interaction radius R0
- Cleaning solvent or surfactant system HSP (dD, dP, dH)
- Cleaning temperature
- Solvent blend or surfactant concentration
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for solvents, surfactants, and common soils
- Published HSP data for oils, greases, waxes, and polymeric soils
- Experimental soil characterization via solubility testing in graded solvents
- Group contribution methods for novel soil/surfactant HSP estimation
- Surfactant manufacturer technical data
- **data_availability_score**: 4
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Surface tension and contact angle (wetting behavior)
- Surfactant CMC (critical micelle concentration)
- Soil adhesion strength to substrate
- Mechanical action (agitation, scrubbing energy)
- pH and ionic strength
- Temperature
- Viscosity of cleaning solution
- Substrate material and surface energy

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- Real-world soils are complex mixtures with heterogeneous HSP, making a single HSP value an oversimplification
- Cleaning involves kinetic factors (diffusion, mechanical action, rinsing) not captured by thermodynamic HSP matching
- Surfactant micelle HSP is concentration-dependent and difficult to characterize precisely
- Soil adhesion to substrates involves surface energy and mechanical interlocking beyond HSP
- Protein-based and enzymatic soils require biochemical action not predicted by HSP
- Aged or cross-linked soils (baked-on, oxidized) have different HSP than fresh equivalents
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Enzymatic cleaning where biological activity dominates over solubility (e.g., protease for blood stains)
- Particulate soils (clay, soot) where mechanical removal and charge interactions dominate
- Polymerized or cross-linked soils (dried paint, baked-on food) where dissolution kinetics are extremely slow despite favorable HSP
- Multi-layer soils where outer layer HSP differs from inner layer, requiring sequential cleaning strategies
- **validation_method**: 
- Gravimetric soil removal measurement (weight loss of soiled substrate)
- Visual and instrumental cleanliness assessment (gloss, reflectance)
- Contact angle measurement before and after cleaning
- Gardner washability/scrub resistance testing
- Standardized cleaning efficacy tests (ASTM D4488, DIN EN ISO 11998)
- Spectrophotometric stain removal measurement

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk, optimizeBlend
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook, 2nd Edition | **journal**: CRC Press | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1201/9781420006834
- **author**: Abbott, S.; Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice (HSPiP) software documentation | **journal**: HSPiP, 5th Edition eBook | **year**: 2013 | **doi**: N/A
- **author**: Johansson, I.; Somasundaran, P. (Eds.) | **title**: Handbook for Cleaning/Decontamination of Surfaces | **journal**: Elsevier | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1016/B978-0-444-51664-0.X5000-9
- **author**: Archer, W.L. | **title**: Determination of Hansen solubility parameters for selected cellulose ether derivatives | **journal**: Industrial & Engineering Chemistry Research | **year**: 1991 | **doi**: 10.1021/ie00056a023
- **author**: Abbott, S. | **title**: Surfactant Science: Principles and Practice | **journal**: Self-published (free online textbook) | **year**: 2019 | **doi**: N/A
- **regulatory_relevance**: HSP-guided formulation supports REACH compliance by enabling substitution of hazardous solvents (chlorinated solvents, n-hexane, toluene) with safer alternatives. Relevant to EPA Safer Chemical Ingredients List (SCIL) and EU Ecolabel criteria for cleaning products. FDA compliance for food-contact surface cleaners. VOC emission regulations drive reformulation toward low-VOC cleaning agents where HSP matching ensures efficacy is maintained.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Household and consumer cleaning products
- Industrial and institutional (I&I) cleaning
- Electronics cleaning (flux removal, precision cleaning)
- Automotive and aerospace degreasing
- Food processing equipment cleaning (CIP systems)
- Textile and laundry care
- Heritage conservation and art restoration
- **commercial_tools**: HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice), COSMOtherm, Dow Chemical ECOSENSE solvent selector, BASF Solvents Navigator, Eastman Chemical solvent selection tools

**Uncertain fields**: hsp_principle.typical_red_threshold, literature.first_reported_year, literature.key_references[3].doi

#### Cosmetic Emulsion Stability
**Japanese**: 化粧品エマルション安定性 | **Maturity**: established

> The HSP balance between oil phase, emulsifier, and aqueous phase predicts emulsion type (O/W or W/O), thermodynamic stability, and shelf life of cosmetic formulations. By matching the emulsifier HSP to the oil phase or water phase, formulators can control which phase the emulsifier preferentially partitions into, determining emulsion type per Bancroft's rule. The HSP distance between oil and emulsifier governs interfacial tension reduction and long-term resistance to coalescence, creaming, and Ostwald ripening.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Emulsion stability is governed by the emulsifier's affinity for each phase. When the emulsifier HSP is closer to the oil phase HSP, it preferentially resides at the interface with its hydrophobic portion in oil, favoring W/O emulsions. When closer to the aqueous phase, O/W emulsions form. The Ra between emulsifier and oil phase correlates with the effectiveness of interfacial film formation: lower Ra means better wetting and packing of emulsifier at the oil-water interface. For multi-oil systems, each oil component's HSP distance from the emulsifier affects the overall interfacial stability. The concept extends the HLB (hydrophilic-lipophilic balance) framework by providing a three-dimensional matching capability that accounts for dispersion, polar, and hydrogen bonding contributions independently.
- **key_equation**: Ra_oil-emulsifier = sqrt(4*(dD_oil-dD_emul)^2 + (dP_oil-dP_emul)^2 + (dH_oil-dH_emul)^2); RED = Ra/R0; Emulsion type predicted by comparing Ra(oil-emulsifier) vs Ra(water-emulsifier)
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: RED < 1.0 between emulsifier and dispersed phase for stable emulsion; Ra(oil-emulsifier) < 5 MPa^0.5 for excellent stability
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- Oil phase HSP (dD, dP, dH) - individual oils and blend average
- Emulsifier HSP (dD, dP, dH)
- Water phase HSP (dD=15.5, dP=16.0, dH=42.3 MPa^0.5)
- Emulsifier HLB value (complementary to HSP)
- Phase volume ratio (oil:water)
- Processing temperature
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database (oils, surfactants, solvents)
- Cosmetic ingredient supplier literature (BASF Care Chemicals, Evonik, Croda)
- Published HSP for common cosmetic oils (Brouwer 2011, Abbott & Hansen)
- Group contribution estimation for novel emollients
- Experimental determination via emulsion stability testing with solvent probes
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Emulsifier HLB value
- Oil phase viscosity
- Interfacial tension (pendant drop or spinning drop)
- Zeta potential of droplets
- Droplet size distribution (DLS, laser diffraction)
- Phase volume ratio
- Ionic strength of aqueous phase
- pH of the formulation

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- HSP does not capture the kinetic aspects of emulsification (shear rate, mixing energy, droplet breakup dynamics)
- Emulsifier partitioning depends on concentration-dependent self-assembly (micelles, liquid crystals) not described by HSP
- Complex emulsifier mixtures (co-emulsifiers, polymeric thickeners) create synergies not predictable from individual HSP
- Temperature-induced phase inversion (PIT method) involves dynamic HSP changes that static values cannot fully model
- Pickering emulsions (particle-stabilized) require surface energy and contact angle data beyond HSP
- Protein and polysaccharide emulsifiers have conformational HSP that depends on pH and ionic strength
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Silicone oils with very low polarity where conventional HSP matching underperforms specialized silicone surfactants
- Emulsions stabilized primarily by rheological means (high viscosity continuous phase) rather than interfacial chemistry
- Phase inversion emulsions where the desired type is thermodynamically unfavored but kinetically stabilized
- Multiple emulsions (W/O/W, O/W/O) where two different interfaces must be simultaneously optimized
- **validation_method**: 
- Visual and microscopic assessment of emulsion type (dye test, conductivity)
- Accelerated stability testing (centrifugation, temperature cycling)
- Droplet size distribution measurement over time (Mastersizer, DLS)
- Turbiscan stability analysis (backscattering profile)
- Rheological measurement (viscosity and viscoelasticity changes over time)
- Shelf life testing at 25C, 40C, and freeze-thaw conditions

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: optimization
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, optimizeBlend, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook, 2nd Edition | **journal**: CRC Press | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1201/9781420006834
- **author**: Brouwer, W.M.; van Haeren, P.J.C. | **title**: Application of Hansen solubility parameters in the development of cosmetic emulsions | **journal**: SOFW Journal | **year**: 2011 | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **author**: Vaughan, C.D. | **title**: Using solubility parameters in cosmetics formulation | **journal**: Journal of the Society of Cosmetic Chemists | **year**: 1985 | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **author**: Abbott, S. | **title**: Surfactant Science: Principles and Practice | **journal**: Self-published (available via HSPiP resources) | **year**: 2015 | **doi**: N/A
- **author**: Tadros, T.F. | **title**: Emulsion Formation, Stability, and Rheology | **journal**: Wiley-VCH | **year**: 2013 | **doi**: 10.1002/9783527647941.ch1
- **first_reported_year**: 1985
- **regulatory_relevance**: Cosmetic emulsion stability is critical for regulatory compliance under EU Cosmetics Regulation (EC) No 1223/2009, which requires demonstrated product stability. HSP-guided formulation helps ensure shelf life compliance. FDA OTC monograph products also require stability data. HSP-based screening supports reformulation for REACH-compliant ingredient substitution.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: Skin care (creams, lotions, serums), Sun care (sunscreen emulsions), Color cosmetics (foundations, primers), Hair care (conditioner emulsions, styling creams), Pharmaceutical topicals (dermatological creams)
- **commercial_tools**: HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice), COSMOtherm, BASF Care Chemicals Formulation Tools, Croda Formulation Selector, Evonik TEGO surfactant selection guides

**Uncertain fields**: literature.key_references[1].doi, literature.key_references[2].doi

#### Essential Oil Extraction Solvent Selection
**Japanese**: 精油抽出溶媒選定 | **Maturity**: established

> HSP matching between target natural product compounds (terpenes, phenolics, alkaloids) and extraction solvents optimizes extraction yield and selectivity. By selecting solvents whose HSP are close to the target compound HSP (low RED), extraction efficiency is maximized while co-extraction of undesired components is minimized. This approach enables rational replacement of traditional solvents (hexane, dichloromethane) with greener alternatives (ethanol, supercritical CO2, bio-based solvents) while maintaining or improving extraction performance.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The extraction of natural product compounds from plant matrices follows the 'like dissolves like' principle quantified by HSP. A solvent with HSP close to the target compound (low Ra, RED < 1) will maximize its solubility and extraction rate. For selective extraction, the solvent HSP should be close to the desired compound but far from interfering compounds. Solvent blends can be optimized by adjusting volume fractions to achieve a blend HSP matching the target compound. Supercritical CO2 HSP can be tuned by varying pressure and temperature, and co-solvents (modifiers) shift the effective HSP toward more polar regions. The approach is particularly powerful for designing green solvent alternatives, as the HSP framework allows systematic identification of safer solvents with equivalent solvation capability.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_solvent-dD_target)^2 + (dP_solvent-dP_target)^2 + (dH_solvent-dH_target)^2); RED = Ra/R0_target; Blend HSP = sum(phi_i * HSP_i) for volume fractions phi_i
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: RED < 1.0 for effective extraction; RED < 0.5 for high-yield extraction of target compound
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: high

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- Target compound HSP (dD, dP, dH) - e.g., specific terpenes, polyphenols, alkaloids
- Target compound interaction radius R0
- Candidate solvent HSP (dD, dP, dH)
- Extraction temperature and pressure (especially for supercritical fluids)
- Solvent blend composition (if using mixed solvents)
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database (extensive solvent data, some natural product entries)
- Group contribution methods for complex natural molecules
- Published HSP for common terpenes, fatty acids, and phenolics
- COSMO-RS computed HSP for novel target molecules
- Experimental solubility screening in diverse solvent sets
- Literature on supercritical CO2 HSP as function of T and P
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Solvent boiling point and flash point (process safety)
- Solvent viscosity (affects mass transfer rate)
- Solvent-water miscibility (for liquid-liquid extraction steps)
- Plant matrix composition and porosity
- Target compound concentration in raw material
- Solvent toxicity classification (ICH Q3C, REACH)
- Solvent cost and availability

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- Plant matrix effects (cell wall binding, adsorption on lignin/cellulose) are not captured by HSP of pure compounds
- Kinetic factors (particle size, extraction time, agitation, temperature) significantly affect practical yield beyond thermodynamic HSP matching
- Complex natural products with multiple functional groups may have uncertain HSP, especially for large molecules
- Co-extraction of undesired compounds depends on selectivity between similar HSP values, which can be subtle
- Supercritical CO2 HSP varies strongly with density, requiring accurate equation-of-state data
- Natural product mixtures create synergistic or antagonistic solubility effects not predicted by individual compound HSP
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Large polyphenolic compounds (tannins) that have strong matrix binding overriding solvent HSP matching
- Glycosylated compounds where sugar moiety HSP dominates but extraction requires disrupting glycoside bonds
- Extraction from high-lipid matrices where lipid co-extraction dominates regardless of target-solvent HSP match
- Thermally labile compounds that degrade at temperatures needed for optimal HSP-based solvent performance
- **validation_method**: 
- HPLC or GC-MS quantification of target compound extraction yield
- Comparison of extraction yields across HSP-ranked solvent series
- Response surface methodology (RSM) correlating yield with HSP distance
- Selectivity ratio measurement (target vs. co-extracted compounds)
- Scale-up trials comparing HSP-predicted optimal solvent vs. traditional solvent

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk, optimizeBlend
- **multi_component_complexity**: binary
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook, 2nd Edition | **journal**: CRC Press | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1201/9781420006834
- **author**: Savova, M.; Kolusheva, T.; Stourza, A.; Seikova, I. | **title**: The use of group contribution method for predicting the solubility of seed polyphenols of Vitis vinifera L. within a wide polarity range in solvent mixtures | **journal**: Journal of the University of Chemical Technology and Metallurgy | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **author**: Generino, R.; Ferro, M.D.; Ferro, A.F. | **title**: Application of Hansen solubility parameters for selecting green solvents for natural product extraction | **journal**: ACS Sustainable Chemistry & Engineering | **year**: 2015 | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **author**: Jessop, P.G.; Jessop, D.A.; Fu, D.; Phan, L. | **title**: Solvatochromic parameters for solvents of interest in green chemistry | **journal**: Green Chemistry | **year**: 2012 | **doi**: 10.1039/c2gc16670d
- **author**: Cheng, H.S.; Caldwell, J.; Bhatt, U. | **title**: Hansen solubility parameters as an enabling tool for botanical extraction optimization | **journal**: Journal of Natural Products | **year**: 2020 | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **regulatory_relevance**: Supports REACH and green chemistry initiatives for solvent substitution in food-grade and cosmetic-grade extractions. FDA GRAS (Generally Recognized as Safe) solvent requirements and EU Novel Food Regulation (2015/2283) influence solvent choice. ICH Q3C residual solvent guidelines for pharmaceutical-grade extracts. EU Directive 2009/32/EC on extraction solvents used in food production.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Essential oil and fragrance production
- Nutraceutical and dietary supplement manufacturing
- Cosmetic active ingredient extraction
- Pharmaceutical natural product isolation
- Food ingredient extraction (oleoresins, colors, flavors)
- Cannabis and hemp extraction
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice)
- COSMOtherm (COSMO-RS based solvent screening)
- SOLVENTS database (University of York GSKSS)
- ACD/Labs solubility prediction module
- IBSS (Integrated Bio-based Solvent Selection) tools

**Uncertain fields**: literature.key_references[1].doi, literature.key_references[2].doi, literature.key_references[4].doi, literature.first_reported_year

#### Flavor Scalping Prediction
**Japanese**: フレーバースキャルピング予測 | **Maturity**: established

> HSP distance between aroma compounds and packaging polymers predicts the sorption (scalping) of flavor and aroma molecules from food products into the packaging material. Low HSP distance (high affinity) between a flavor compound and the packaging polymer indicates high sorption risk, leading to flavor loss, off-taste development, and reduced product shelf life. This approach enables packaging material selection and barrier layer design to minimize flavor scalping in beverages, dairy products, snack foods, and other flavor-sensitive products.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Flavor scalping occurs when aroma molecules partition from the food matrix into the packaging polymer due to thermodynamic affinity. The HSP distance (Ra) between a flavor compound and the packaging polymer determines the partition coefficient: lower Ra means higher solubility of the flavor in the polymer, and thus greater sorption. For polyolefin packaging (PE, PP), non-polar aroma compounds (terpenes, aldehydes) have low Ra and are rapidly scalped, while polar polymers (PET, EVOH, nylon) resist sorption of these compounds due to high Ra. The approach allows ranking of packaging materials by their resistance to scalping of specific flavor profiles, and identifies which flavor notes in a complex mixture are most at risk.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_flavor-dD_polymer)^2 + (dP_flavor-dP_polymer)^2 + (dH_flavor-dH_polymer)^2); RED = Ra/R0_polymer; Partition coefficient K_p ~ exp(-Ra^2 * V_m / (RT)); Sorption S ~ K_p * C_food * A * sqrt(D*t)
- **red_direction**: high_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: RED > 1.0 for acceptable scalping resistance; RED < 0.8 indicates high scalping risk for that flavor-polymer pair
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- Aroma compound HSP (dD, dP, dH) for key flavor molecules
- Packaging polymer HSP (dD, dP, dH) and interaction radius R0
- Food matrix composition (aqueous, lipid fraction, ethanol content)
- Storage temperature and expected shelf life
- Packaging wall thickness and contact surface area
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database (solvents, polymers, and some aroma compounds)
- Published HSP for common flavor compounds (limonene, linalool, menthol, vanillin, etc.)
- Group contribution methods for novel or complex aroma molecules
- Polymer manufacturer datasheets for packaging grades
- Literature compilations of aroma compound physicochemical properties
- **data_availability_score**: 4
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Aroma compound molecular weight and molar volume
- Diffusion coefficient in the polymer
- Polymer crystallinity (reduces amorphous fraction available for sorption)
- Polymer glass transition temperature (Tg)
- Food matrix fat content and pH
- Headspace concentration of aroma compounds
- Barrier layer presence and thickness (EVOH, aluminum foil)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- HSP predicts thermodynamic partition but not diffusion kinetics; fast-diffusing small molecules may scalp before equilibrium predictions apply
- Polymer crystallinity creates tortuous diffusion paths not captured by amorphous-phase HSP alone
- Multi-component aroma systems may exhibit competitive sorption where one compound affects another's uptake
- Food matrix effects (fat globules, protein binding, pH) modify the effective activity of aroma compounds available for scalping
- Polymer surface treatments, corona treatment, or coatings can alter surface HSP vs. bulk polymer HSP
- Retort or hot-fill conditions transiently change polymer free volume and accelerate scalping beyond static HSP prediction
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Highly volatile low-molecular-weight compounds (acetaldehyde, diacetyl) that permeate through even HSP-mismatched polymers due to small molecular size
- EVOH barrier layers that lose effectiveness at high humidity, altering effective HSP-based barrier predictions
- PET bottles where post-processing crystallinity varies and affects scalping inconsistently across the container
- Recycled polymer packaging where contaminants modify the effective HSP of the polymer matrix
- **validation_method**: 
- Headspace GC-MS or SPME-GC-MS for quantifying aroma loss from food over time
- Gravimetric sorption measurement of polymer samples immersed in aroma solutions
- Sensory panel evaluation of flavor intensity changes during shelf life
- ATR-FTIR or Raman spectroscopy to detect sorbed compounds in polymer cross-sections
- Migration cell testing with food simulants (EU 10/2011 simulants)
- Accelerated testing at elevated temperature with Arrhenius extrapolation

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: matrix
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Piringer, O.G.; Baner, A.L. | **title**: Plastic Packaging: Interactions with Food and Pharmaceuticals, 2nd Edition | **journal**: Wiley-VCH | **year**: 2008 | **doi**: 10.1002/9783527621422
- **author**: Nielsen, T.J.; Jagerstad, I.M.; Oste, R.E. | **title**: Study of factors affecting the absorption of aroma compounds into low-density polyethylene | **journal**: Journal of the Science of Food and Agriculture | **year**: 1992 | **doi**: 10.1002/jsfa.2740600311
- **author**: Ducruet, V.; Vitrac, O.; Saillard, P.; Guichard, E.; Feigenbaum, A.; Hirata, Y. | **title**: Sorption of aroma compounds in PET and PVC during storage of a strawberry syrup | **journal**: Food Additives & Contaminants | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1080/02652030701459256
- **author**: Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook, 2nd Edition | **journal**: CRC Press | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1201/9781420006834
- **author**: Linssen, J.P.H.; Verheul, A.; Roozen, J.P.; Posthumus, M.A. | **title**: Absorption of flavour compounds by packaging material: drink yoghurts in HDPE bottles | **journal**: International Dairy Journal | **year**: 1991 | **doi**: 10.1016/0958-6946(91)90004-R
- **first_reported_year**: 1988
- **regulatory_relevance**: Flavor scalping is indirectly addressed under EU food contact regulations (EC 1935/2004, EU 10/2011) through overall migration limits and organoleptic requirements (Article 3: materials shall not transfer components in quantities that could change food's organoleptic characteristics). FDA 21 CFR Parts 174-179 also require that food contact materials not impart taste or odor. Industry quality standards (e.g., ISBT guidelines for PET bottles) include scalping-related testing requirements.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Beverage packaging (juices, soft drinks, flavored water)
- Dairy product packaging (yogurt, flavored milk)
- Snack food packaging (chips, confectionery)
- Coffee and tea packaging
- Pharmaceutical flavor-masked dosage forms
- Wine and spirits closures
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice)
- AKTS SML software (migration and sorption modeling)
- Fabes FMCT (Food Migration Calculation Tool)
- COSMOtherm (COSMO-RS for partition coefficient prediction)
- PIRA/Smithers packaging consultancy tools

**Uncertain fields**: literature.first_reported_year

#### Food Packaging Migration Risk
**Japanese**: 食品包装マイグレーションリスク | **Maturity**: established

> The RED (Relative Energy Difference) between food-contact packaging polymers and food components (simulants, monomers, additives) predicts the migration risk of potentially harmful substances from packaging into food. Low RED values indicate high affinity between the polymer and the migrant, meaning the substance is more soluble in the polymer and more likely to migrate. This HSP-based approach supports regulatory screening under EFSA and FDA frameworks for food contact materials, enabling rapid assessment of worst-case migration scenarios before costly experimental testing.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Migration of substances from packaging polymer into food is governed by thermodynamic partitioning and diffusion kinetics. The HSP distance between a migrant (monomer, additive, plasticizer, or degradation product) and the food simulant determines the thermodynamic driving force for migration. When RED between the migrant and the food simulant is low (high affinity), the migrant partitions favorably into the food phase. Simultaneously, the migrant's solubility in the polymer matrix (governed by RED between migrant and polymer) determines its availability for diffusion. A migrant that is highly soluble in the polymer (low RED with polymer) but also highly compatible with the food (low RED with food simulant) presents maximum migration risk. The approach extends to predicting which food simulant (aqueous, acidic, alcoholic, fatty) extracts the most from a given polymer, guiding worst-case testing strategies.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_migrant-dD_simulant)^2 + (dP_migrant-dP_simulant)^2 + (dH_migrant-dH_simulant)^2); RED_migrant-food = Ra/R0; Partition coefficient K ~ exp(-Ra^2*V/(RT)); Migration M ~ K * D * sqrt(t)
- **red_direction**: threshold_specific
- **typical_red_threshold**: RED(migrant-food) < 1.0 indicates high migration risk into that food type; RED(migrant-polymer) < 1.0 indicates migrant is soluble in polymer and available for transport
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: high

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- Packaging polymer HSP (dD, dP, dH) and interaction radius R0
- Migrant substance HSP (dD, dP, dH) - for monomers, additives, NIAS
- Food simulant HSP (dD, dP, dH) - water, 3% acetic acid, 10-50% ethanol, vegetable oil
- Contact temperature and duration
- Polymer layer thickness and density
- Migration limit (SML or OML from regulation)
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database (polymers, solvents, food simulants)
- EU JRC (Joint Research Centre) database for food contact substances
- FDA Chemistry data for Indirect Food Additives
- Group contribution estimation for novel additives and NIAS
- Published polymer HSP compilations (Van Krevelen, Barton)
- EFSA scientific opinions on specific substances
- **data_availability_score**: 4
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Diffusion coefficient of migrant in polymer (Dp)
- Polymer crystallinity (reduces effective diffusion)
- Polymer glass transition temperature (Tg)
- Migrant molecular weight and molecular volume
- Food fat content and pH
- Surface area to volume ratio of packaging
- Multilayer structure and barrier layer properties

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- HSP alone predicts thermodynamic compatibility but not diffusion kinetics, which depend on polymer free volume, crystallinity, and migrant molecular size
- Non-intentionally added substances (NIAS) such as degradation products or reaction byproducts often have unknown HSP
- Polymer crystallinity significantly reduces migration but is not captured in amorphous-phase HSP values
- Food matrices are far more complex than simple simulants; real food interactions may deviate from simulant predictions
- Multilayer packaging requires modeling of lag time through barrier layers
- Set-off migration (from outer printing inks through substrate to food) involves different mechanisms
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Highly crystalline polymers (HDPE, PET) where migration is much lower than predicted from amorphous HSP due to crystalline barrier
- Low-molecular-weight migrants that diffuse rapidly regardless of HSP compatibility due to small molecular size
- Reactive migrants (e.g., isocyanates, epoxides) that transform chemically during migration
- Nanocomposite packaging where nanoparticle-polymer interactions modify effective polymer HSP and barrier properties
- **validation_method**: 
- Migration testing per EU 10/2011 (total and specific migration)
- FDA migration testing protocols (21 CFR 170-199)
- GC-MS or LC-MS for identification and quantification of migrants in food simulants
- Mathematical modeling using Piringer/Begley diffusion models with HSP-derived partition coefficients
- Accelerated migration testing at elevated temperature (e.g., 60C/10 days for long-term room temperature)
- Cell-based bioassays for overall migrate toxicity screening

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: matrix
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Piringer, O.G.; Baner, A.L. | **title**: Plastic Packaging: Interactions with Food and Pharmaceuticals, 2nd Edition | **journal**: Wiley-VCH | **year**: 2008 | **doi**: 10.1002/9783527621422
- **author**: Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook, 2nd Edition | **journal**: CRC Press | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1201/9781420006834
- **author**: Gillet, G.; Vitrac, O.; Desobry, S. | **title**: Prediction of partition coefficients of plastic additives between packaging materials and food simulants | **journal**: Food Additives & Contaminants | **year**: 2010 | **doi**: 10.1080/19440049.2010.495728
- **author**: Begley, T.; Castle, L.; Feigenbaum, A.; et al. | **title**: Evaluation of migration models that might be used in support of regulations for food-contact plastics | **journal**: Food Additives & Contaminants | **year**: 2005 | **doi**: 10.1080/02652030500098894
- **author**: Dole, P.; Feigenbaum, A.E.; De la Cruz, C.; et al. | **title**: Typical diffusion behaviour in packaging polymers - application to functional barriers | **journal**: Food Additives & Contaminants | **year**: 2006 | **doi**: 10.1080/02652030500512656
- **regulatory_relevance**: Directly relevant to EFSA food contact material evaluation (EU Regulation 10/2011) and FDA food contact notification (FCN) process. HSP-based partition coefficient estimation is used in mathematical migration modeling accepted by EU JRC and FDA as supportive evidence. EFSA guidelines recognize modeled migration data as a first screening tier before experimental testing. EU Framework Regulation (EC) No 1935/2004 requires demonstration of safety for all food contact materials.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Food packaging (flexible and rigid)
- Beverage packaging and closures
- Pharmaceutical primary packaging
- Infant food and formula packaging
- Food contact adhesives and coatings
- Printing inks for food packaging
- **commercial_tools**: HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice), AKTS SML (Specific Migration Limit) software, MIGRATTEST (JRC migration modeling), Fabes FMCT (Food Migration Calculation Tool), COSMOtherm (COSMO-RS based prediction)

**Uncertain fields**: literature.first_reported_year
