#### Fragrance Encapsulation
**Japanese**: 香料カプセル化 | **Maturity**: established

> The HSP distance between a fragrance molecule and a wall material (polymer shell) predicts encapsulation efficiency, retention during storage, and controlled release kinetics. When the wall material HSP is sufficiently different from the fragrance HSP (high RED), the fragrance is effectively entrapped with minimal permeation through the shell. Conversely, matching HSP between fragrance and the external medium drives release. This principle enables rational selection of wall materials for microencapsulation and nanoencapsulation of fragrances in detergents, fabric softeners, personal care products, and fine perfumery.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Fragrance encapsulation relies on the thermodynamic incompatibility between the fragrance core and the wall material to retain the volatile payload. A high HSP distance (Ra) between fragrance and wall polymer means low affinity, reducing diffusion of fragrance molecules through the shell wall. The release is then triggered when the capsule encounters a medium (e.g., skin surface, fabric, water) whose HSP is closer to the fragrance, creating a thermodynamic driving force for partitioning out of the capsule. The permeability of the wall to each fragrance component scales with its solubility in the wall polymer, which is inversely related to Ra. For multi-component fragrance blends, differential RED values predict selective retention and release order of individual fragrance notes.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_fragrance - dD_wall)^2 + (dP_fragrance - dP_wall)^2 + (dH_fragrance - dH_wall)^2); RED = Ra/R0_wall; Permeability ~ exp(-k * Ra^2)
- **red_direction**: high_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: RED > 1.0 for good retention; RED > 1.5 for excellent barrier against fragrance permeation through wall
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- Fragrance component HSP (dD, dP, dH) for each key aroma molecule
- Wall material polymer HSP (dD, dP, dH) and interaction radius R0
- External medium HSP (water, skin lipid model, fabric surface)
- Wall thickness and capsule diameter
- Storage and use temperature
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database (extensive solvent and fragrance molecule data)
- Group contribution methods (Van Krevelen, Hoftyzer) for novel fragrance molecules
- Fragrance supplier technical data sheets
- Published HSP tables for common aroma chemicals (Archer 2007, Abbott & Hansen)
- Experimental determination via solubility testing in solvent series
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Fragrance vapor pressure and boiling point
- Wall material glass transition temperature (Tg)
- Wall material crystallinity and crosslink density
- Capsule size distribution
- Wall thickness uniformity
- Partition coefficient (logP) of fragrance components
- Ambient humidity and temperature conditions

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- HSP alone does not account for wall porosity, defects, or mechanical rupture during handling
- Crystalline or highly crosslinked wall materials may have barrier properties exceeding HSP predictions due to kinetic trapping
- Multi-component fragrance blends exhibit complex co-diffusion and competitive transport not captured by individual RED values
- Fragrance molecules are often small and volatile, so kinetic factors (diffusion coefficient, wall thickness) are critical alongside thermodynamic HSP matching
- Surface interactions at the wall-core interface (e.g., adsorption, hydrogen bonding) may not be fully captured by bulk HSP values
- Encapsulation processes (coacervation, spray drying, interfacial polymerization) introduce process-dependent wall structures that affect performance beyond HSP prediction
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Small, non-polar fragrance molecules (e.g., limonene, pinene) that permeate even through HSP-mismatched walls due to high diffusivity
- Wall materials that plasticize or swell upon long-term contact with fragrance, changing their effective HSP over time
- Fragrance components with strong specific interactions (e.g., vanillin hydrogen bonding) not fully represented by average dH
- Aqueous environments that hydrolyze or degrade certain wall materials (e.g., polyurea, melamine-formaldehyde) regardless of HSP
- **validation_method**: 
- Headspace GC-MS for fragrance retention and release profiling
- Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) for encapsulated fragrance content
- Accelerated aging tests at elevated temperature
- Sensory panel evaluation of fragrance intensity over time
- Optical and SEM microscopy for capsule integrity assessment
- Dynamic vapor sorption (DVS) for wall permeability measurement

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook, 2nd Edition | **journal**: CRC Press | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1201/9781420006834
- **author**: Quellet, C.; Schudel, M.; Ringgenberg, R. | **title**: Flavors & fragrance delivery systems | **journal**: Chimia | **year**: 2001 | **doi**: 10.2533/chimia.2001.12
- **author**: Madene, A.; Jacquot, M.; Scher, J.; Desobry, S. | **title**: Flavour encapsulation and controlled release - a review | **journal**: International Journal of Food Science & Technology | **year**: 2006 | **doi**: 10.1111/j.1365-2621.2005.00980.x
- **author**: Arshady, R. | **title**: Microspheres, Microcapsules & Liposomes, Vol. 1: Preparation & Chemical Applications | **journal**: Citus Books | **year**: 1999 | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **author**: Abbott, S.; Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice (HSPiP) software documentation | **journal**: HSPiP, 5th Edition eBook | **year**: 2013 | **doi**: N/A
- **regulatory_relevance**: Encapsulated fragrances in consumer products must comply with IFRA (International Fragrance Association) guidelines and EU Cosmetics Regulation (EC) No 1223/2009. HSP-based wall material selection supports compliance by predicting retention and minimizing unintended release of allergens. Microplastic concerns under EU REACH restriction proposals may affect polymer wall material choices.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: Household detergents and fabric care, Personal care and cosmetics, Fine perfumery, Air fresheners and home fragrance, Textile finishing (scented fabrics)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice)
- COSMOtherm (COSMO-RS based solubility prediction)
- Encapsys (proprietary encapsulation design tools)
- Givaudan, Firmenich, IFF proprietary formulation platforms

**Uncertain fields**: literature.key_references[3].doi, literature.first_reported_year

#### Natural Dye Extraction Optimization
**Japanese**: 天然色素抽出最適化 | **Maturity**: established

> HSP matching between target pigment molecules (anthocyanins, carotenoids, chlorophylls, betalains) and extraction solvents guides solvent selection and extraction process optimization for food colorants, textile dyes, and cosmetic pigments. By minimizing the HSP distance (Ra) between the solvent and the target pigment, extraction yield and selectivity are maximized while enabling the use of greener, food-safe solvents. This approach replaces empirical trial-and-error with rational solvent screening, particularly valuable for replacing conventional solvents with bio-based or GRAS alternatives.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The extraction of natural pigments from plant matrices follows the 'like dissolves like' principle quantified by HSP. A solvent whose HSP (dD, dP, dH) closely matches the HSP of the target pigment will maximize solubility and mass transfer driving force, leading to higher extraction yields. The HSP distance Ra between solvent and pigment governs the thermodynamic affinity. For pigment classes like anthocyanins (high dP, high dH due to glycosylated polyphenol structure) vs. carotenoids (high dD, low dP, low dH due to hydrophobic polyene chain), the optimal solvent profiles differ dramatically. Solvent blending allows fine-tuning of the effective HSP to match the target pigment precisely, enabling mixed-solvent systems (e.g., ethanol-water) that achieve better extraction than either component alone. The approach also enables selectivity: by choosing a solvent HSP that matches one pigment class but not others, selective extraction from complex plant matrices is achievable.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_solvent-dD_pigment)^2 + (dP_solvent-dP_pigment)^2 + (dH_solvent-dH_pigment)^2); RED = Ra/R0_pigment; Extraction yield correlates inversely with Ra
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: RED < 1.0 for effective extraction; RED < 0.7 for high-yield extraction
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- Target pigment HSP (dD, dP, dH) - varies by pigment class
- Pigment interaction radius R0
- Candidate solvent HSP (dD, dP, dH)
- Extraction temperature (affects HSP, especially dH)
- Solvent blend composition for mixed-solvent systems
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for common solvents
- Group contribution methods (Van Krevelen, Hoftyzer) for pigment HSP estimation
- Published pigment HSP from extraction studies (Generalized HSP for anthocyanins, carotenoids, chlorophylls)
- Experimental solubility screening in graded solvent series
- Molecular dynamics simulation for novel pigment HSP estimation
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Extraction temperature and time
- Solid-to-liquid ratio
- Plant matrix particle size
- pH (critical for anthocyanin stability and charge state)
- Solvent viscosity (affects mass transfer kinetics)
- Pigment thermal stability in chosen solvent
- Dielectric constant of solvent

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- Natural pigments exist in complex plant matrices with cell wall barriers, so thermodynamic HSP matching alone does not capture mass transfer limitations
- Many natural pigments are glycosylated or complexed with proteins/metals, making HSP estimation from base structure inaccurate
- pH-dependent structural changes (e.g., anthocyanin flavylium cation vs. quinoidal base) shift the effective HSP significantly
- Pigment degradation during extraction (thermal, oxidative) is not captured by HSP
- HSP data for many natural pigment classes remain limited and rely on group contribution estimates
- Co-extraction of non-target compounds (sugars, proteins, pectins) affects apparent extraction efficiency
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Anthocyanin extraction where pH-dependent structural transformation changes HSP during the process
- Carotenoid extraction from highly lipophilic matrices where matrix binding energy exceeds solvent affinity
- Extraction of pigment-protein complexes (e.g., phycocyanin) where protein denaturation dominates over HSP matching
- Supercritical CO2 extraction where pressure-dependent HSP shifts are poorly characterized for pigments
- **validation_method**: 
- UV-Vis spectrophotometry for total pigment quantification
- HPLC for individual pigment identification and quantification
- Comparison of extraction yields across solvent series ranked by Ra
- Response surface methodology correlating Ra with measured extraction yield
- Color measurement (CIELAB) of extracts

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk, optimizeBlend
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Generosi, J.; Hansen, C.M.; Abbott, S. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice (HSPiP) software documentation and database | **journal**: HSPiP eBook, 5th Edition | **year**: 2013 | **doi**: N/A
- **author**: Srinivas, K.; King, J.W.; Howard, L.R.; Monrad, J.K. | **title**: Solubility and solution thermodynamic properties of quercetin and quercetin dihydrate in subcritical water | **journal**: Journal of Food Engineering | **year**: 2010 | **doi**: 10.1016/j.jfoodeng.2010.04.001
- **author**: Yara-Varon, E.; Fabiano-Tixier, A.S.; Balcells, M.; Canela-Garayoa, R.; Bily, A.; Chemat, F. | **title**: Is it possible to substitute hexane with green solvents for extraction of carotenoids? A theoretical versus experimental solubility study | **journal**: RSC Advances | **year**: 2016 | **doi**: 10.1039/C6RA03016E
- **author**: Sicaire, A.G.; Vian, M.A.; Fine, F.; Carre, P.; Tostain, S.; Chemat, F. | **title**: Alternative bio-based solvents for extraction of fat and oils: solubility prediction, global yield, extraction kinetics, chemical composition and cost of manufacturing | **journal**: International Journal of Molecular Sciences | **year**: 2015 | **doi**: 10.3390/ijms16048430
- **regulatory_relevance**: HSP-guided solvent selection supports compliance with food safety regulations (FDA GRAS solvents, EU Regulation 1333/2008 on food additives, EU Directive 2009/32/EC on extraction solvents). Enables substitution of hazardous solvents (hexane, dichloromethane) with food-grade alternatives (ethanol, ethyl acetate, supercritical CO2), facilitating regulatory approval of natural colorant extracts.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Food and beverage coloring
- Cosmetics and personal care (natural pigments)
- Textile dyeing with natural dyes
- Nutraceutical and dietary supplement manufacturing
- Pharmaceutical (natural-origin APIs and excipients)
- **commercial_tools**: HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice), COSMOtherm (COSMO-RS based solvent screening), COSMO-RS TmoleX, ACD/Labs Percepta (solubility prediction module)

**Uncertain fields**: hsp_principle.typical_red_threshold, literature.first_reported_year, literature.key_references[0].doi

#### Sunscreen UV Filter Compatibility
**Japanese**: 日焼け止めUVフィルター適合性 | **Maturity**: established

> HSP distance between organic UV filters (chemical sunscreen actives) and the cosmetic vehicle (emollients, oils, emulsion phases) determines the solubility limit of the UV filter in the formulation, directly affecting achievable SPF, photostability, and product aesthetics. Poor HSP matching leads to UV filter crystallization, reduced SPF, whitening, and instability. Rational HSP-based selection of emollients and co-solvents maximizes UV filter loading and uniform distribution in the film on skin.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Organic UV filters must be dissolved in the cosmetic vehicle at concentrations high enough to achieve the target SPF. The solubility of a UV filter in an emollient or oil phase is governed by the HSP distance between them: a smaller Ra (or RED < 1) indicates the UV filter is within the solubility sphere of the vehicle, predicting good dissolution. When the RED exceeds 1, the UV filter approaches or exceeds its solubility limit, risking crystallization during storage or upon application to skin as volatile components evaporate. For combination sunscreens using multiple UV filters, each filter must be individually soluble in the vehicle. Furthermore, the HSP of the dried film on skin (after volatile components evaporate) determines long-term photostability, as UV filter migration and phase separation in the film reduce effective UV absorption. HSP matching also predicts interactions between UV filters themselves, which can affect photostability through energy transfer mechanisms.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_filter-dD_vehicle)^2 + (dP_filter-dP_vehicle)^2 + (dH_filter-dH_vehicle)^2); RED = Ra/R0_filter; Solubility limit correlates with RED: higher loading possible at lower RED
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: RED < 1.0 for adequate solubility; RED < 0.7 for high UV filter loading and long-term stability
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- UV filter HSP (dD, dP, dH) for each active ingredient
- UV filter interaction radius R0
- Emollient/oil phase HSP (dD, dP, dH)
- Target UV filter concentration (wt%)
- Storage temperature range
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for common solvents and some UV filters
- Published UV filter HSP from cosmetic science literature
- Group contribution methods for UV filter HSP estimation
- Emollient supplier technical literature (Evonik, BASF, Ashland)
- Experimental solubility screening in emollient series
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- UV filter molar absorptivity and absorption spectrum
- UV filter photostability (photodegradation rate)
- Emollient viscosity and spreading coefficient
- Melting point of UV filter (crystallization tendency)
- Water resistance requirements (for W/O or water-resistant formulations)
- SPF and UVA-PF targets
- Film thickness on skin

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- HSP predicts thermodynamic solubility but not crystallization kinetics, which depend on nucleation and supersaturation conditions
- Skin interaction (sebum, sweat, temperature) alters the effective vehicle HSP after application
- Volatile silicones and solvents evaporate after application, changing the film HSP and potentially causing UV filter precipitation
- Photodegradation products of UV filters have different HSP, potentially causing phase separation during UV exposure
- Emulsion systems add complexity: UV filter partitioning between oil and water phases is not fully captured by simple HSP matching
- Polymeric film formers used in water-resistant sunscreens modify the effective HSP environment unpredictably
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Avobenzone (butyl methoxydibenzoylmethane) crystallization in low-polarity emollients despite apparently adequate RED values, due to strong self-association
- UV filter precipitation upon volatile silicone evaporation in spray sunscreens, where the residual film HSP shifts dramatically
- Incompatibility between certain UV filter combinations (e.g., avobenzone + octinoxate) driven by photochemical rather than HSP interactions
- High-SPF formulations where UV filter concentrations exceed thermodynamic solubility limits even at optimal HSP matching
- **validation_method**: 
- Accelerated stability testing at elevated temperature (40-45C) and freeze-thaw cycling
- Polarized light microscopy for UV filter crystal detection
- In vitro SPF measurement (ISO 24443, Diffey-Robillard method)
- UV filter dissolution rate and concentration monitoring by HPLC
- Sensory evaluation (whitening, greasiness, spreadability)

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk, optimizeBlend
- **multi_component_complexity**: multi
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook, 2nd Edition | **journal**: CRC Press | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1201/9781420006834
- **author**: Binks, B.P.; Fletcher, P.D.I.; Johnson, A.J.; Marinopoulos, I.; Sherwood, J.M.; Sherwood, J. | **title**: Solubility of UV filter molecules in organic solvents and emollient esters: a Hansen solubility parameter approach | **journal**: Journal of Cosmetic Science | **year**: 2017 | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **author**: Abbott, S. | **title**: Practical Solubility - Hansen Solubility Parameters for cosmetic formulation | **journal**: HSPiP documentation and Cosmetics & Toiletries articles | **year**: 2015 | **doi**: N/A
- **author**: Chatelain, E.; Gabard, B. | **title**: Photostabilization of butyl methoxydibenzoylmethane (Avobenzone) and ethylhexyl methoxycinnamate by bis-ethylhexyloxyphenol methoxyphenyl triazine (Tinosorb S) | **journal**: Photochemistry and Photobiology | **year**: 2001 | **doi**: 10.1562/0031-8655(2001)074<0401:POBMAA>2.0.CO;2
- **regulatory_relevance**: UV filter solubility in the vehicle directly impacts regulatory compliance: FDA OTC Monograph (21 CFR 352) and EU Cosmetics Regulation (EC 1223/2009) specify maximum allowed concentrations for each UV filter. HSP-based formulation ensures filters remain dissolved at permitted levels. Stability requirements (ICH-like accelerated stability for cosmetics) demand that UV filters stay in solution throughout product shelf life. COLIPA/COSMETICS EUROPE in vitro SPF methods require uniform UV filter distribution.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Cosmetics and personal care (sunscreen formulation)
- Pharmaceutical (photoprotective dermatological products)
- Raw material/emollient suppliers
- Contract cosmetic manufacturing
- UV filter active ingredient manufacturers
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice)
- COSMOtherm
- BASF Sunscreen Simulator
- DSM Personal Care UV filter formulation tools
- Hallstar SunSolve (UV filter photostability/SPF prediction)

**Uncertain fields**: hsp_principle.typical_red_threshold, literature.key_references[1].doi, literature.first_reported_year

### Category: Pharmaceutical

#### Amorphous Solid Dispersion (ASD) Design
**Japanese**: 非晶質固体分散体（ASD）設計 | **Maturity**: established

> Amorphous solid dispersions (ASDs) improve oral bioavailability of poorly soluble drugs by molecularly dispersing the API in a polymer matrix. HSP distance between the API and carrier polymer predicts thermodynamic miscibility: small Ra values indicate favorable mixing enthalpy, which correlates with physical stability against recrystallization. The Flory-Huggins interaction parameter chi can be estimated from HSP distance, enabling rapid in-silico screening of polymer carriers before costly experimental studies.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The thermodynamic miscibility of an API-polymer pair is governed by the Gibbs free energy of mixing. The enthalpic contribution is approximated by the Flory-Huggins interaction parameter chi, which can be estimated from the HSP distance (Ra) between drug and polymer. A small Ra indicates similar intermolecular interactions (dispersion, polar, hydrogen bonding), predicting favorable mixing and resistance to amorphous phase separation or recrystallization. The approach maps both API and polymer into 3D Hansen space and uses proximity as a miscibility predictor.
- **key_equation**: chi = V_drug * (Ra)^2 / (6 * R * T), where Ra = sqrt(4*(dD1-dD2)^2 + (dP1-dP2)^2 + (dH1-dH2)^2)
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: high

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: API HSP values (dD, dP, dH), Polymer HSP values (dD, dP, dH), Molar volume of drug (V_drug), Temperature of interest, Drug loading target (weight fraction)
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for common polymers (PVP, PVPVA, HPMC, HPMCAS, Soluplus)
- Group contribution methods (Van Krevelen, Hoftyzer) for novel APIs
- Inverse gas chromatography (IGC) measurements
- Solubility parameter estimation from molecular structure (e.g., COSMOtherm)
- Literature values for FDA-approved polymers
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Glass transition temperature (Tg) of API and polymer
- Melting point and enthalpy of fusion of crystalline API
- Molecular weight of polymer
- Drug loading ratio
- Moisture sorption isotherms
- Crystallization tendency classification of API

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- HSP-based chi estimation assumes regular solution theory, neglecting specific directional interactions such as ionic or charge-transfer complexes
- Polymer conformational entropy contributions are not captured by HSP distance alone
- Temperature dependence of HSP values is often approximated or ignored, reducing accuracy at processing temperatures vs. storage temperatures
- Drug-polymer hydrogen bonding specificity (donor-acceptor pairing) is not fully resolved by the single dH parameter
- Kinetic factors (nucleation barriers, polymer chain mobility) are not predicted
- High drug loading scenarios deviate from dilute-mixing assumptions
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- APIs with strong self-association (dimers, catemers) may show poorer miscibility than HSP predicts
- Polymers with specific acid-base interactions with APIs (e.g., HPMCAS with basic drugs) may show better miscibility than Ra alone predicts
- Systems where crystallization is kinetically controlled rather than thermodynamically driven
- Salt forms or ionic APIs where electrostatic interactions dominate
- **validation_method**: 
- Differential scanning calorimetry (DSC) - single Tg indicates miscibility
- Modulated DSC for Tg detection at low drug loadings
- X-ray powder diffraction (XRPD) for crystallinity detection
- Solid-state NMR for domain size estimation
- Accelerated stability studies (40C/75%RH) for recrystallization monitoring
- Dissolution testing under non-sink conditions

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: binary
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Greenhalgh, D.J. et al. | **journal**: Journal of Pharmaceutical Sciences | **year**: 1999 | **title**: Solubility parameters as predictors of miscibility in solid dispersions | **doi**: 10.1021/js9804069
- **author**: Forster, A. et al. | **journal**: Journal of Pharmacy and Pharmacology | **year**: 2001 | **title**: Selection of excipients for melt extrusion with two poorly water-soluble drugs by solubility parameter calculation and thermal analysis | **doi**: 10.1211/0022357011775532
- **author**: Marsac, P.J. et al. | **journal**: Journal of Pharmaceutical Sciences | **year**: 2006 | **title**: Estimation of drug-polymer miscibility and solubility in amorphous solid dispersions using experimentally determined interaction parameters | **doi**: 10.1002/jps.20625
- **author**: Thakral, S. and Thakral, N.K. | **journal**: Journal of Pharmaceutical Sciences | **year**: 2013 | **title**: Prediction of drug-polymer miscibility through the use of solubility parameter based Flory-Huggins interaction parameter and the experimental validation | **doi**: 10.1002/jps.23583
- **regulatory_relevance**: FDA encourages mechanistic understanding of ASD stability; HSP-based miscibility screening is commonly included in IND/NDA filings as part of formulation rationale. ICH Q8 pharmaceutical development framework supports science-based formulation selection including solubility parameter approaches.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: Pharmaceutical (oral solid dosage), Contract research organizations (CRO), Generic pharmaceutical development, Pharmaceutical excipient manufacturers
- **commercial_tools**: HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice), COSMOtherm (Dassault Systemes / SCM), Materials Studio (Dassault Systemes BIOVIA), ADMET Predictor (Simulations Plus), MeltCalc

**Uncertain fields**: typical_red_threshold, first_reported_year

#### Biologic Formulation Buffer Selection
**Japanese**: バイオ医薬品製剤用緩衝液選定 | **Maturity**: emerging

> HSP-based co-solvent and buffer excipient assessment for protein conformation stability in biologic drug products. This application uses Hansen solubility parameters to evaluate how formulation excipients (sugars, polyols, surfactants, amino acids, buffer species) interact with the protein surface to maintain native conformation and prevent aggregation. By comparing the HSP of the solvent environment with the effective surface HSP of the protein, formulators can predict whether a given buffer composition will stabilize or destabilize the biologic, enabling rational selection of formulation components for monoclonal antibodies, enzymes, and other therapeutic proteins.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Protein stability in aqueous formulations depends on the balance of protein-solvent and protein-protein interactions. The preferential exclusion theory (Timasheff) explains how co-solutes either stabilize proteins (kosmotropes, excluded from protein surface) or destabilize them (chaotropes, preferentially bound). HSP provides a framework to quantify these interactions: excipients with HSP values very different from the protein surface HSP promote preferential exclusion and stabilization, while excipients with HSP values similar to hydrophobic patches on the protein surface may promote unfolding. The hydrogen bonding parameter (dH) is particularly important as it governs water structure around the protein. Mapping buffer excipients in Hansen space relative to the protein's effective surface HSP enables prediction of stabilizing vs. destabilizing formulation components.
- **red_direction**: threshold_specific
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: high

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- Effective protein surface HSP values (dD, dP, dH) or hydrophobicity profile
- Excipient/co-solvent HSP values (dD, dP, dH)
- Buffer species HSP values
- Formulation pH and ionic strength
- Protein concentration
- Storage temperature range
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for common co-solvents and excipients
- Computational estimation of protein surface HSP from amino acid sequence and 3D structure
- Literature values for sugars (trehalose, sucrose), polyols (sorbitol, mannitol), and surfactants (polysorbate 20/80)
- Molecular dynamics simulations for protein surface interaction parameters
- Experimental preferential interaction measurements (densimetry, vapor pressure osmometry)
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Protein melting temperature (Tm) and aggregation onset temperature (Tagg)
- Protein isoelectric point (pI) and charge at formulation pH
- Second virial coefficient (B22) for protein-protein interaction
- Viscosity at target protein concentration
- Surfactant critical micelle concentration (CMC)
- Osmolality of final formulation

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: qualitative
- **limitations**: 
- Protein surface HSP is an approximation; real protein surfaces are heterogeneous with hydrophobic, charged, and polar patches
- HSP framework was developed for small molecules and polymer systems; extension to large biomacromolecules is not rigorously validated
- Electrostatic interactions (charge-charge, charge-dipole) critical for protein stability are poorly captured by the three HSP parameters
- Conformational dynamics and flexibility of proteins are not described by static HSP values
- Specific binding interactions (e.g., metal ion coordination, disulfide scrambling) are outside the HSP framework
- Concentration-dependent colloidal stability aspects (opalescence, liquid-liquid phase separation) require additional parameters beyond HSP
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Proteins with dominant electrostatic instability (charge repulsion at low ionic strength) where HSP provides no insight
- Surfactant-protein interactions that depend on specific binding sites rather than bulk HSP matching
- Oxidation-driven degradation pathways unrelated to solubility parameters
- Freeze-thaw or agitation-induced aggregation driven by interfacial stress rather than thermodynamic solution HSP
- **validation_method**: 
- Differential scanning fluorimetry (DSF/nanoDSF) for thermal stability (Tm shifts)
- Size exclusion chromatography (SEC) for aggregate quantification
- Dynamic light scattering (DLS) for colloidal stability and aggregation onset
- Isothermal chemical denaturation (ICD) for conformational stability
- Accelerated stability studies at 25C and 40C
- Subvisible particle analysis (MFI, FlowCam)

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: high
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: multi
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **regulatory_relevance**: Biologic formulation development is governed by ICH Q5C (stability of biotechnological products) and ICH Q8 (pharmaceutical development). While HSP-based buffer selection is not yet a standard regulatory expectation, mechanistic understanding of excipient-protein interactions is valued in BLA submissions. FDA encourages science-based rationale for formulation design of biologics under the QbD framework.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Biopharmaceutical (monoclonal antibody formulation)
- Biosimilar development
- Vaccine formulation
- Contract development organizations specializing in biologics
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice)
- COSMOtherm for excipient interaction modeling
- UNCHAINED LABS (Stunner, Uncle) for high-throughput formulation screening
- Molecular Operating Environment (MOE) for protein surface analysis

**Uncertain fields**: key_equation, typical_red_threshold, first_reported_year, data_availability_score, key_references

---

#### Cocrystal Screening
**Japanese**: 共結晶スクリーニング | **Maturity**: emerging

> Cocrystals are multi-component crystalline forms where an API and a coformer are held together by non-covalent interactions, typically hydrogen bonds. HSP distance between API and coformer predicts the likelihood of cocrystal formation: components with similar HSP values (small Ra) are more likely to form stable cocrystals. This enables rapid virtual screening of coformer libraries before labor-intensive experimental crystallization trials, significantly reducing the number of experiments needed for polymorph and cocrystal exploration.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Cocrystal formation requires sufficient molecular complementarity for heteromeric interactions (API-coformer) to be energetically competitive with homomeric interactions (API-API and coformer-coformer). HSP proximity in Hansen space indicates similar intermolecular interaction profiles, which is a necessary (though not sufficient) condition for cocrystal formation. The hydrogen bonding component dH is particularly critical because most pharmaceutical cocrystals are sustained by hydrogen bonding synthons. The approach uses Ra distance as a pre-screening filter to prioritize coformer candidates.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_API - dD_coformer)^2 + (dP_API - dP_coformer)^2 + (dH_API - dH_coformer)^2)
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: low

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- API HSP values (dD, dP, dH)
- Coformer HSP values (dD, dP, dH)
- Hydrogen bonding donor/acceptor profiles of API and coformer
- Molecular structure information for synthon analysis
- **data_sources**: 
- Group contribution methods (Hoftyzer-Van Krevelen) for HSP estimation
- HSPiP database for common coformers (carboxylic acids, amides, etc.)
- COSMOtherm sigma-profile calculations
- Cambridge Structural Database (CSD) for known cocrystal structures
- Literature compilations of pharmaceutical coformer HSP values
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Hydrogen bond propensity scores
- Molecular electrostatic potential surfaces
- pKa values (for salt vs cocrystal determination, delta-pKa rule)
- Crystal packing efficiency
- Melting point of individual components
- Supramolecular synthon compatibility

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: qualitative
- **limitations**: 
- HSP distance is a thermodynamic approximation that does not capture the directional and geometric specificity of hydrogen bonding synthons critical for cocrystal formation
- Cannot distinguish between cocrystal, salt, eutectic, and solid solution outcomes
- Polymorphism of the cocrystal itself is not predicted
- Stoichiometry of cocrystal (1:1, 2:1, etc.) is not predicted by HSP alone
- Kinetic factors in crystallization (solvent, supersaturation, seeding) are not captured
- False negatives are common: some successful cocrystals have large Ra values due to complementary rather than similar interactions
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Cocrystals stabilized by halogen bonding or pi-pi stacking rather than hydrogen bonding
- Systems where crystal packing efficiency dominates over molecular interaction similarity
- Zwitterionic APIs where charge-assisted hydrogen bonds override HSP predictions
- Coformers with multiple conformational polymorphs
- **validation_method**: 
- Powder X-ray diffraction (PXRD) for new phase identification
- Differential scanning calorimetry (DSC) for melting point and thermal behavior
- Single crystal X-ray diffraction for structure determination
- Raman or IR spectroscopy for hydrogen bond detection
- Hot-stage microscopy for screening

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: low
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: binary
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Mohammad, M.A. et al. | **journal**: International Journal of Pharmaceutics | **year**: 2011 | **title**: Hansen solubility parameter as a tool to predict cocrystal formation | **doi**: 10.1016/j.ijpharm.2011.01.030
- **author**: Abramov, Y.A. et al. | **journal**: Crystal Growth & Design | **year**: 2012 | **title**: Rational coformer or solvent selection for pharmaceutical cocrystallization or desolvation | **doi**: 10.1021/cg300024v
- **author**: Perlovich, G.L. | **journal**: CrystEngComm | **year**: 2015 | **title**: Thermodynamic characteristics of cocrystal formation and melting points for rational design of pharmaceutical two-component systems | **doi**: 10.1039/C5CE00783F
- **author**: Khalaji, M. et al. | **journal**: Crystal Growth & Design | **year**: 2021 | **title**: Solubility parameter-based screening for pharmaceutical cocrystal design | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **regulatory_relevance**: FDA draft guidance on pharmaceutical cocrystals (2018) classifies cocrystals as a special drug product; HSP screening is accepted as part of the scientific rationale for coformer selection in regulatory submissions but is not mandated.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: Pharmaceutical (solid-state chemistry), Contract research organizations (CRO), Academic pharmaceutical sciences, Agrochemical formulation
- **commercial_tools**: HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice), COSMOtherm, Mercury (CCDC) for CSD analysis, Materials Studio (BIOVIA)

**Uncertain fields**: typical_red_threshold, first_reported_year, key_references[3].doi

---

#### Excipient Compatibility Screening
**Japanese**: 賦形剤適合性スクリーニング | **Maturity**: established

> Excipient compatibility screening uses HSP distance between an active pharmaceutical ingredient (API) and common tablet excipients (binders, fillers, disintegrants, lubricants, glidants) to predict physical stability of solid dosage formulations. A small HSP distance (Ra) between API and excipient indicates thermodynamic affinity, which may promote dissolution of the drug into the excipient matrix or eutectic formation, while a large Ra suggests immiscibility and physical separation. By mapping all candidate excipients in Hansen space relative to the API, formulators can rapidly identify combinations prone to incompatibility (e.g., API softening, discoloration, potency loss) and prioritize stable formulations before committing to accelerated stability studies.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The physical compatibility between an API and excipients is governed by the thermodynamic affinity of their molecular interactions. When the HSP distance (Ra) between the API and an excipient is small, the components share similar dispersion, polar, and hydrogen-bonding characteristics, which can lead to molecular-level mixing, solid solution formation, or surface wetting effects that alter drug stability or dissolution. Conversely, a large Ra indicates dissimilar interaction profiles and predicts physical separation with minimal interaction. For excipient screening, the goal is context-dependent: for binders, moderate affinity may be desirable for granule cohesion, while for lubricants like magnesium stearate, excessive affinity to the API surface can retard dissolution. The approach positions the API and all candidate excipients as points in 3D Hansen space and uses Ra or RED values to rank compatibility risk.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_API - dD_exc)^2 + (dP_API - dP_exc)^2 + (dH_API - dH_exc)^2)
- **red_direction**: threshold_specific
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- API HSP values (dD, dP, dH)
- Excipient HSP values (dD, dP, dH) for each candidate
- Temperature of storage and processing conditions
- Drug-excipient ratio in formulation
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for common pharmaceutical excipients
- Group contribution methods (Van Krevelen, Hoftyzer) for novel excipients
- Handbook of Pharmaceutical Excipients for physical property data
- Published literature values for FDA-approved excipients (e.g., MCC, lactose, PVP, HPMC, magnesium stearate)
- Experimental solubility testing for HSP determination of new APIs
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Moisture content and water activity
- Melting point and glass transition temperature of API and excipients
- Particle size distribution
- Compaction pressure
- pH of the microenvironment (for pH-sensitive interactions)
- Specific surface area of excipients

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- HSP predicts thermodynamic affinity but not kinetic rates of solid-state reactions or chemical degradation
- Chemical incompatibilities (e.g., Maillard reaction between lactose and primary amines) are not captured by HSP distance
- Surface interactions at particle-particle contacts may differ from bulk HSP predictions
- Moisture-mediated interactions add complexity not fully described by anhydrous HSP values
- Polymorphic form of the API can shift effective HSP values
- Mechanical processing (milling, compaction) can alter local interactions beyond equilibrium HSP predictions
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Chemical reactions between API and excipient functional groups (acid-base, oxidation, transesterification) not predicted by HSP
- Moisture-catalyzed degradation where water acts as a mediator rather than a solvent
- Lubricant over-blending effects that are process-dependent rather than thermodynamic
- Excipient impurities (e.g., residual peroxides in PEG, formaldehyde in PVP) driving incompatibility
- **validation_method**: 
- Binary mixture accelerated stability studies (40C/75%RH, 2-4 weeks)
- Differential scanning calorimetry (DSC) of API-excipient blends for thermal event shifts
- Isothermal stress testing (IST) with HPLC assay for degradation products
- XRPD for polymorphic changes in blends
- FTIR/Raman spectroscopy for interaction detection
- Visual inspection and colorimetric analysis

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: low
- **ui_pattern**: matrix
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: multi
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Greenhalgh, D.J. et al. | **journal**: Journal of Pharmaceutical Sciences | **year**: 1999 | **title**: Solubility parameters as predictors of miscibility in solid dispersions | **doi**: 10.1021/js9804069
- **author**: Hancock, B.C. et al. | **journal**: Pharmaceutical Research | **year**: 1997 | **title**: The use of solubility parameters in pharmaceutical dosage form design | **doi**: 10.1023/A:1012167202002
- **author**: Bharate, S.S. et al. | **journal**: Journal of Pharmaceutical Investigation | **year**: 2016 | **title**: Interactions and incompatibilities of pharmaceutical excipients with active pharmaceutical ingredients: a comprehensive review | **doi**: 10.1007/s40005-016-0234-8
- **author**: Forster, A. et al. | **journal**: Journal of Pharmacy and Pharmacology | **year**: 2001 | **title**: Selection of excipients for melt extrusion with two poorly water-soluble drugs by solubility parameter calculation and thermal analysis | **doi**: 10.1211/0022357011775532
- **regulatory_relevance**: Excipient compatibility screening is a standard component of ICH Q8 pharmaceutical development. HSP-based rationale for excipient selection is accepted in IND and NDA filings as supporting evidence for formulation design. FDA guidance on pharmaceutical development encourages science-based approaches to excipient selection, and HSP screening is recognized as a valid in-silico preselection tool.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Pharmaceutical (oral solid dosage forms)
- Generic pharmaceutical development
- Contract development and manufacturing organizations (CDMO)
- Pharmaceutical excipient manufacturers
- **commercial_tools**: HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice), COSMOtherm (Dassault Systemes / SCM), ADMET Predictor (Simulations Plus), Minitab / JMP for DOE-based compatibility studies

**Uncertain fields**: typical_red_threshold, first_reported_year, sensitivity_to_hbond_split

---

#### Inhalation Drug-Propellant Compatibility
**Japanese**: 吸入薬-噴射剤適合性評価 | **Maturity**: established

> HSP distance between a drug substance and HFA propellant (HFA-134a or HFA-227ea) predicts suspension or solution stability in pressurized metered-dose inhalers (pMDIs). When the drug HSP closely matches the propellant HSP, solution-based pMDIs with molecularly dissolved drug are feasible. When HSP distance is large, suspension formulations result, and the magnitude of the mismatch influences physical stability parameters such as flocculation rate, creaming/sedimentation, and valve clogging. This HSP-based approach enables rapid screening of drug-propellant combinations and guides co-solvent selection (e.g., ethanol) to bridge HSP gaps for improved formulation stability.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The solubility of drug particles in HFA propellants follows classical like-dissolves-like principles quantified by HSP distance. HFA propellants (HFA-134a: 1,1,1,2-tetrafluoroethane; HFA-227ea: 1,1,1,2,3,3,3-heptafluoropropane) have distinctive HSP profiles with moderate dispersion forces, significant polarity from C-F bonds, and low hydrogen bonding capacity. Most drug substances have substantially different HSP profiles, leading to suspension-type formulations. The Ra between drug and propellant determines whether a solution or suspension formulation is achievable. Co-solvents like ethanol shift the effective HSP of the propellant blend toward the drug's HSP, improving solubility. Surfactant excipients (oleic acid, lecithin) further modulate the interface. The approach calculates Ra between drug and propellant (or propellant-cosolvent blend) to predict formulation type and optimize co-solvent concentration.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_drug - dD_propellant)^2 + (dP_drug - dP_propellant)^2 + (dH_drug - dH_propellant)^2); For co-solvent blends: dD_mix = phi_1*dD_1 + phi_2*dD_2 (volume-weighted mixing)
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: high

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- Drug HSP values (dD, dP, dH)
- Propellant HSP values (HFA-134a: dD=12.1, dP=7.1, dH=5.7 MPa^0.5; HFA-227ea: dD=11.9, dP=5.4, dH=2.9 MPa^0.5) [uncertain]
- Co-solvent HSP values (ethanol: dD=15.8, dP=8.8, dH=19.4 MPa^0.5)
- Co-solvent concentration range
- Canister storage temperature and pressure
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for propellant and co-solvent HSP values
- Group contribution methods for drug HSP estimation
- Published literature on HFA propellant properties
- Experimental solubility measurements in propellant systems (high-pressure solubility cells)
- Fluorocarbon chemistry references for propellant HSP values
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Drug particle size and morphology
- Drug crystal habit and surface energy
- Propellant vapor pressure at storage temperature
- Moisture content and water solubility in propellant
- Surfactant HLB and concentration
- Valve and actuator material compatibility
- Drug solubility in propellant-cosolvent mixtures (experimental)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- HFA propellant HSP values have some uncertainty due to limited experimental determination methods under pressurized conditions
- Surface energy and crystal face-specific interactions of micronized drug particles are not fully captured by bulk HSP
- Electrostatic charging of drug particles in low-dielectric propellant medium is not predicted by HSP
- Valve and gasket interactions with the formulation add complexity beyond drug-propellant HSP matching
- Moisture ingress effects on suspension stability are not predicted by anhydrous HSP analysis
- Drug solubility in pressurized propellant at canister conditions may deviate from ambient HSP predictions
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Ostwald ripening in suspension formulations driven by particle size distribution rather than HSP
- Crystal growth or polymorphic transformation in the propellant medium
- Drug-surfactant interactions creating unexpected solution behavior not predicted by drug-propellant Ra alone
- Extractable/leachable interactions between formulation and container closure system
- **validation_method**: 
- High-pressure view cell solubility measurements
- Delivered dose uniformity (DDU) testing per USP <601>
- Aerodynamic particle size distribution (APSD) by cascade impaction
- Accelerated stability studies at 25C/60%RH and 40C/75%RH
- Visual inspection of canister contents through transparent containers
- Valve delivery consistency testing over canister life

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: optimization
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk, optimizeBlend
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **regulatory_relevance**: pMDI formulation development is governed by FDA guidance for metered dose inhaler and dry powder inhaler drug products. HSP-based rationale for propellant and co-solvent selection is accepted in ANDA and NDA CMC sections. The transition from CFC to HFA propellants drove significant HSP-based reformulation research. EMA guideline on pharmaceutical development of inhalation products also supports science-based formulation approaches.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Pharmaceutical (respiratory drug delivery)
- Generic inhaler development
- Propellant manufacturers (Honeywell, Mexichem)
- Contract manufacturing organizations for inhalation products
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice)
- COSMOtherm for solubility prediction in fluorocarbon systems
- Proveris ARIA for delivered dose and spray characterization
- Copley Scientific inhaler testing equipment

**Uncertain fields**: typical_red_threshold, first_reported_year, key_references

---

#### Liposome Membrane Permeability
**Japanese**: リポソーム膜透過性 | **Maturity**: emerging

> Liposomes are phospholipid bilayer vesicles used as drug carriers. The HSP distance between a drug molecule and the lipid bilayer predicts passive membrane permeation and encapsulation efficiency. Drugs with HSP values close to the lipid membrane partition favorably into the bilayer, while very hydrophilic or very different HSP drugs remain in the aqueous core. This approach guides rational design of liposomal formulations by predicting drug loading location (bilayer vs. aqueous core), encapsulation efficiency, and release kinetics.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The lipid bilayer of a liposome has characteristic HSP values determined by the phospholipid composition (e.g., DPPC, DSPC, cholesterol). Drug partitioning between the aqueous phase and the bilayer is governed by the difference in HSP between the drug and each phase. A drug with HSP close to the bilayer interior (low Ra to lipid) will preferentially partition into the membrane, while a drug with HSP far from the bilayer (high Ra) will remain in the aqueous compartment. The RED value relative to the bilayer predicts the degree of membrane interaction and passive permeation rate. Cholesterol content modulates bilayer HSP by increasing the dispersion component.
- **key_equation**: Ra(drug-bilayer) = sqrt(4*(dD_drug - dD_lipid)^2 + (dP_drug - dP_lipid)^2 + (dH_drug - dH_lipid)^2); Partition coefficient ~ exp(-Ra^2 * V_drug / (R * T))
- **red_direction**: threshold_specific
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- Drug HSP values (dD, dP, dH)
- Lipid bilayer HSP values (dD, dP, dH) for specific phospholipid composition
- Molar volume of drug
- Temperature (affects bilayer phase state: gel vs liquid crystalline)
- **data_sources**: 
- Group contribution methods for drug HSP
- Published lipid bilayer HSP values (limited; often estimated from component lipid HSP)
- Molecular dynamics simulations for lipid HSP profiles
- HSPiP database for common pharmaceutical excipients
- Literature values for standard lipids (DPPC, DSPC, DOPC, cholesterol)
- **data_availability_score**: 2
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Log P of drug
- Lipid composition and cholesterol molar ratio
- Bilayer phase transition temperature (Tm)
- Vesicle size and lamellarity
- Drug ionization state (pKa and formulation pH)
- Drug molecular weight

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: qualitative
- **limitations**: 
- Lipid bilayer is anisotropic with a gradient of properties from headgroup to hydrophobic core; a single set of HSP values is an oversimplification
- HSP of the bilayer changes significantly with lipid composition, cholesterol content, and temperature
- Electrostatic interactions between charged drugs and charged lipid headgroups are not captured by standard HSP
- Membrane curvature and vesicle size effects on permeation are not predicted
- Active transport mechanisms and protein-mediated processes in biological membranes are not accounted for
- Limited experimental HSP data available for lipid bilayers compared to polymers or solvents
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Ionizable drugs where pH-dependent partitioning dominates over HSP-predicted behavior
- Large molecules (MW > 1000) where size exclusion effects override solubility parameter predictions
- Drugs that form complexes with specific lipid headgroups (e.g., calcium-mediated binding)
- Phase-separated or domain-forming lipid mixtures (rafts) with heterogeneous HSP
- **validation_method**: 
- Encapsulation efficiency measurement (ultracentrifugation or dialysis)
- Drug release kinetics (dialysis bag method)
- Fluorescence quenching for bilayer localization
- Differential scanning calorimetry (DSC) for drug-lipid interaction
- Small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) for bilayer structure
- Isothermal titration calorimetry (ITC) for binding thermodynamics

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: pairwise
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Fedotov, V.D. et al. | **journal**: [uncertain] | **year**: [uncertain] | **title**: Application of Hansen solubility parameters to predict drug-lipid bilayer interactions | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **author**: Adamska, K. and Voelkel, A. | **journal**: Journal of Chromatography A | **year**: 2006 | **title**: Hansen solubility parameters for polyethylene glycols by inverse gas chromatography | **doi**: 10.1016/j.chroma.2006.07.005
- **author**: Gupta, R. et al. | **journal**: Molecular Pharmaceutics | **year**: 2020 | **title**: Solubility parameter-based prediction of drug loading in liposomal formulations | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **author**: Hansen, C.M. | **journal**: Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook, 2nd ed. | **year**: 2007 | **title**: Chapter on biological materials | **doi**: ISBN 978-0849372483
- **regulatory_relevance**: No specific regulatory guidance references HSP for liposome design. However, FDA guidance on liposome drug products (2018) encourages mechanistic understanding of drug-lipid interactions, where HSP analysis can support formulation rationale in IND submissions.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Pharmaceutical (injectable and topical liposomal products)
- Cosmetics (liposomal skincare)
- Nanomedicine research
- Gene therapy (lipid nanoparticle design)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP
- COSMOtherm
- GROMACS / NAMD (molecular dynamics for bilayer HSP estimation)
- Materials Studio

**Uncertain fields**: typical_red_threshold, first_reported_year, key_references[0].journal, key_references[0].year, key_references[0].doi, key_references[2].doi

---

#### Nanoparticle Drug Loading Optimization
**Japanese**: ナノ粒子薬物充填最適化 | **Maturity**: established

> HSP matching between a drug molecule and a nanocarrier polymer (such as PLGA, PCL, chitosan, or lipid-based systems) predicts encapsulation efficiency and drug loading capacity. When the HSP distance between API and carrier is small, favorable thermodynamic mixing drives higher drug incorporation into the nanoparticle matrix. This approach enables rational selection of carrier materials and prediction of maximum achievable drug loading without extensive trial-and-error experimentation, significantly accelerating nanoformulation development for injectable, oral, and inhalation nanoparticle drug delivery systems.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Drug encapsulation in polymeric nanoparticles is governed by the thermodynamic compatibility between the drug and the carrier matrix. The Flory-Huggins interaction parameter (chi), estimable from the HSP distance (Ra) between drug and polymer, determines the enthalpic driving force for mixing. A low Ra value indicates that the drug and polymer share similar dispersion, polar, and hydrogen bonding characteristics, resulting in favorable drug-polymer miscibility and high encapsulation efficiency. During nanoprecipitation or emulsion-solvent evaporation, the drug preferentially partitions into the polymer phase when their HSP values are closely matched. The approach maps candidate carrier polymers in Hansen space relative to the drug target and ranks them by Ra or chi to predict optimal drug-carrier combinations.
- **key_equation**: chi = V_drug * (Ra)^2 / (6 * R * T); Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_drug - dD_polymer)^2 + (dP_drug - dP_polymer)^2 + (dH_drug - dH_polymer)^2); Encapsulation efficiency correlates inversely with Ra
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- Drug HSP values (dD, dP, dH)
- Carrier polymer HSP values (dD, dP, dH)
- Molar volume of the drug
- Process solvent HSP values
- Target drug loading ratio
- Processing temperature
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for common biodegradable polymers (PLGA, PLA, PCL)
- Group contribution methods for novel drug molecules
- COSMOtherm calculations for API HSP estimation
- Published literature values for chitosan, lipid excipients, and PEG derivatives
- Experimental solubility screening for HSP sphere fitting
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Molecular weight and end-group chemistry of carrier polymer
- Polymer crystallinity and glass transition temperature
- Drug aqueous solubility and log P
- Process solvent properties (boiling point, miscibility with water)
- Surfactant type and concentration
- Nanoparticle preparation method parameters

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- HSP predicts thermodynamic affinity but actual loading depends on kinetics of nanoparticle formation (solvent removal rate, mixing intensity)
- Surface localization vs. core encapsulation cannot be distinguished by HSP alone
- Drug crystallization within nanoparticles is a kinetic phenomenon not captured by equilibrium HSP matching
- Polymer molecular weight and chain end effects alter effective HSP values
- Multi-component systems (drug + polymer + surfactant + solvent) create complex partitioning not fully described by binary HSP comparison
- Drug-drug interactions at high loading concentrations can override drug-polymer affinity predictions
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Drugs with strong self-association tendency (pi-pi stacking, H-bond dimers) that crystallize within nanoparticles despite good HSP match
- Polymer degradation products (e.g., acidic PLGA oligomers) interacting with base-sensitive drugs
- Surfactant-drug competition for polymer surface sites not predicted by bulk HSP
- Burst release due to surface-adsorbed drug despite favorable bulk HSP matching
- **validation_method**: 
- Encapsulation efficiency measurement by UV-Vis or HPLC after nanoparticle separation
- Drug loading quantification by dissolving nanoparticles and assaying drug content
- DSC and XRPD for characterizing drug physical state within nanoparticles
- In vitro drug release profiling
- TEM/SEM for nanoparticle morphology assessment
- Dynamic light scattering (DLS) for particle size and stability

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk, optimizeBlend
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **regulatory_relevance**: Nanoparticle formulations are reviewed under FDA guidance for nanotechnology-based drug products. HSP-based carrier selection rationale is accepted as part of the Quality by Design (QbD) framework in CMC submissions. EMA reflection papers on nanomedicines encourage mechanistic understanding of drug-carrier interactions.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Pharmaceutical (nanomedicine and advanced drug delivery)
- Biotechnology
- Contract research organizations (CRO) specializing in nanoformulation
- Academic translational research centers
- **commercial_tools**: HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice), COSMOtherm (Dassault Systemes / SCM), Materials Studio (Dassault Systemes BIOVIA), Scienomics MAPS platform

**Uncertain fields**: typical_red_threshold, first_reported_year, key_references

---

#### Polymorph/Solvate Risk Assessment
**Japanese**: 多形・溶媒和物リスク評価 | **Maturity**: emerging

> Solvent HSP regions that overlap with metastable polymorph zones in Hansen space indicate crystallization conditions likely to produce unwanted polymorphic forms or solvates. By mapping the HSP coordinates of crystallization solvents against known polymorph stability domains, formulators can predict which solvent systems risk producing metastable forms during manufacturing. This approach enables rational solvent selection for crystallization processes to ensure consistent production of the desired polymorphic form, reducing batch failures and regulatory complications associated with polymorphic transitions.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Different polymorphs of the same API present different molecular packing arrangements and therefore different effective surface energies and interaction profiles. The solvent-solute interaction during crystallization, governed by HSP similarity, influences nucleation kinetics and determines which polymorph preferentially nucleates. Solvents with HSP values similar to the metastable form's molecular surface characteristics tend to stabilize that form's crystal nucleus, promoting its growth. Mapping the Hansen space into zones associated with different polymorphic outcomes creates a predictive landscape: the HSP coordinates of the crystallization solvent determine which polymorph domain is accessed. Solvate formation is predicted when the solvent HSP falls within a region of strong complementary hydrogen bonding with the API crystal lattice.
- **key_equation**: Ra_polymorph_i = sqrt(4*(dD_solvent - dD_polymorph_i)^2 + (dP_solvent - dP_polymorph_i)^2 + (dH_solvent - dH_polymorph_i)^2); Polymorph outcome predicted by minimum Ra to each form's HSP zone
- **red_direction**: threshold_specific
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: high

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- API HSP values for each known polymorph (dD, dP, dH)
- Solvent HSP values (dD, dP, dH)
- Crystallization temperature and cooling rate
- Solubility data in candidate solvents for polymorph identification
- Known polymorph landscape of the API
- **data_sources**: 
- Experimental polymorph screening results (solvent-based and thermal methods)
- HSPiP database for solvent HSP values
- Cambridge Structural Database (CSD) for crystal packing analysis
- Slurry conversion experiments to establish thermodynamic stability relationships
- Group contribution estimates for API surface HSP per polymorph
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Thermodynamic stability ranking of known polymorphs (melting point, enthalpy of fusion)
- Solubility curves in each solvent for each polymorph (enantiotropic vs. monotropic)
- Supersaturation ratio at crystallization onset
- Cooling rate and agitation conditions
- Seed crystal form and quantity
- Water activity in solvent system (for hydrate risk)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: qualitative
- **limitations**: 
- Effective HSP values for individual polymorphs are difficult to determine experimentally and often rely on indirect estimation
- Nucleation kinetics (induction time, critical nucleus size) are not predicted by thermodynamic HSP matching alone
- Concomitant polymorphism (simultaneous nucleation of multiple forms) cannot be predicted
- Solvent-mediated transformation during crystallization adds kinetic complexity beyond HSP prediction
- Disappearing polymorphs phenomenon is inherently unpredictable
- Anti-solvent crystallization introduces rapid composition changes not captured by static HSP analysis
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Kinetically trapped metastable forms that persist despite thermodynamic HSP predictions favoring the stable form
- Template-induced nucleation by impurities or container surfaces overriding solvent HSP effects
- Desolvation of solvates producing unexpected polymorphs during drying
- High-pressure or mechanochemical conditions altering the polymorph landscape beyond ambient HSP predictions
- **validation_method**: 
- XRPD pattern matching for polymorph identification
- Single crystal X-ray diffraction for structural confirmation
- DSC/TGA for thermal characterization and solvate detection
- Raman microscopy for in-situ polymorph monitoring during crystallization
- Hot-stage microscopy for visual observation of polymorphic transitions
- Slurry bridging experiments for relative stability confirmation

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: high
- **ui_pattern**: optimization
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, classifyRisk, optimizeBlend
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **regulatory_relevance**: ICH Q6A requires identification and control of polymorphic forms. FDA and EMA expect risk assessment of polymorph changes during manufacturing. HSP-based solvent selection rationale for crystallization process design is accepted as part of ICH Q8/Q11 pharmaceutical development and manufacturing process understanding. Polymorph control is a critical quality attribute (CQA) requiring scientific justification.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Pharmaceutical (API manufacturing and crystallization process development)
- Generic pharmaceutical development (polymorph IP navigation)
- Contract development and manufacturing organizations (CDMO)
- Pharmaceutical process chemistry
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice)
- Mercury (CCDC) for crystal packing analysis
- CrystalPredictor/CrystalOptimizer
- COSMOtherm for solvent screening
- Technobis Crystal16/Crystalline for experimental polymorph screening

**Uncertain fields**: typical_red_threshold, first_reported_year, data_availability_score, key_references

---

#### Protein Aggregation Risk
**Japanese**: タンパク質凝集リスク | **Maturity**: emerging

> Protein therapeutics (monoclonal antibodies, enzymes, peptides) are prone to aggregation during manufacturing, storage, and administration. The HSP distance between the protein surface and the formulation buffer/excipient environment predicts conformational stability and aggregation propensity. Formulation conditions that minimize Ra between the protein surface HSP and the solvent HSP help maintain the native state and reduce aggregation. This approach enables rational excipient screening for biopharmaceutical formulation development.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Protein surfaces expose a heterogeneous distribution of amino acid residues with varying hydrophobic, polar, and hydrogen bonding character. The effective surface HSP of a protein in its native state reflects this distribution. When the formulation environment HSP deviates significantly from the protein surface HSP, the protein experiences unfavorable interactions that can promote partial unfolding and exposure of hydrophobic patches, leading to aggregation. Excipients (sugars, polyols, amino acids, surfactants) modify the effective HSP of the formulation buffer. The goal is to select excipients that bring the solution HSP closer to the protein surface HSP, thereby stabilizing the native conformation.
- **key_equation**: Ra(protein-buffer) = sqrt(4*(dD_protein - dD_buffer)^2 + (dP_protein - dP_buffer)^2 + (dH_protein - dH_buffer)^2)
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: high

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- Protein surface HSP values (dD, dP, dH) - estimated from surface amino acid composition
- Buffer/formulation HSP values (dD, dP, dH)
- Excipient HSP values (dD, dP, dH)
- Protein concentration
- Temperature and pH conditions
- **data_sources**: 
- Amino acid surface composition analysis from crystal structure or homology model
- Group contribution methods applied to exposed amino acid residues
- Published HSP values for common excipients (trehalose, sucrose, arginine, polysorbate 80)
- Molecular dynamics simulations for surface HSP estimation
- Protein Data Bank (PDB) structures
- **data_availability_score**: 2
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Protein melting temperature (Tm) and onset of aggregation temperature (Tagg)
- Second virial coefficient (B22 / kD) for colloidal stability
- Protein isoelectric point (pI) and formulation pH
- Ionic strength
- Protein concentration
- Viscosity of formulation
- Surface hydrophobicity index (from ANS fluorescence)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: qualitative
- **limitations**: 
- Protein surface HSP estimation is highly approximate; the surface is heterogeneous and dynamic
- HSP is an equilibrium thermodynamic concept that does not capture kinetic aspects of protein aggregation (nucleation, growth)
- Conformational changes upon unfolding drastically alter effective surface HSP, creating a moving target
- Specific protein-excipient interactions (preferential exclusion/binding) are not well represented by bulk HSP matching
- Electrostatic interactions (critical for protein stability) are only partially captured by the polar component dP
- Very limited validation studies compared to polymer/solvent applications of HSP
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Proteins where aggregation is driven by specific sequence motifs (e.g., amyloidogenic regions) rather than overall surface properties
- Aggregation initiated by chemical degradation (oxidation, deamidation) rather than conformational instability
- High-concentration formulations (>100 mg/mL) where protein-protein interactions are complex and concentration-dependent
- Interfacial aggregation (air-water, ice-water interfaces) driven by surface adsorption rather than bulk solution compatibility
- **validation_method**: 
- Size exclusion chromatography (SEC) for aggregation monitoring
- Dynamic light scattering (DLS) for particle size distribution
- Micro-flow imaging (MFI) for subvisible particle counting
- Differential scanning fluorimetry (DSF / nanoDSF) for Tm determination
- Accelerated stability studies at elevated temperature
- Turbidity measurements

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: high
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: multi
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Lai, M.C. and Topp, E.M. | **journal**: Journal of Pharmaceutical Sciences | **year**: 1999 | **title**: Solid-state chemical stability of proteins and peptides | **doi**: 10.1021/js980374e
- **author**: Turowski, M. et al. | **journal**: [uncertain] | **year**: [uncertain] | **title**: Hansen solubility parameters applied to protein formulation stability | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **author**: Garidel, P. et al. | **journal**: Biophysical Chemistry | **year**: 2015 | **title**: High-concentration protein formulations: How high is high? | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **author**: Hanada, M. et al. | **journal**: Journal of Pharmaceutical Sciences | **year**: 2018 | **title**: Solubility parameter-based analysis for rational selection of excipients for protein stabilization | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **regulatory_relevance**: FDA and ICH Q5C guidelines on stability testing of biotechnological products do not specifically reference HSP. However, QbD-based approaches (ICH Q8/Q9/Q10) support science-driven formulation development where HSP screening can contribute to the mechanistic understanding required for regulatory submissions.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Biopharmaceutical (monoclonal antibody formulation)
- Biosimilar development
- Vaccine formulation
- Industrial enzymes
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP
- COSMOtherm
- Molecular operating environment (MOE) for surface property calculation
- Schrodinger Suite (protein surface analysis)

**Uncertain fields**: typical_red_threshold, first_reported_year, key_references[1].journal, key_references[1].year, key_references[1].doi, key_references[2].doi, key_references[3].doi
