#### Transdermal Penetration Enhancer Selection
**Japanese**: 経皮吸収促進剤の選択 | **Maturity**: established

> Transdermal drug delivery requires molecules to permeate through the stratum corneum, the outermost skin barrier composed primarily of intercellular lipids and corneocytes. HSP matching between the drug, skin lipids, and penetration enhancers predicts permeation enhancement. An enhancer with HSP values intermediate between the drug and skin can improve partitioning and diffusion. This approach enables rational selection of chemical penetration enhancers for topical and transdermal formulations, reducing trial-and-error experimentation.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The stratum corneum can be characterized by its HSP values (approximately dD=17, dP=8, dH=8 MPa^0.5 for intercellular lipid pathway). Drug permeation depends on partitioning into and diffusing through this barrier. A penetration enhancer works by either disrupting skin lipid organization (enhancer HSP close to skin lipid HSP) or by improving drug-skin compatibility (reducing effective Ra between drug and skin). The optimal enhancer matches the HSP of the drug-skin system to maximize the thermodynamic activity gradient. The three-component HSP triangle (drug-enhancer-skin) is used to find enhancers that bridge the solubility parameter gap.
- **key_equation**: Ra(drug-skin) = sqrt(4*(dD_drug - dD_skin)^2 + (dP_drug - dP_skin)^2 + (dH_drug - dH_skin)^2); Enhanced permeation predicted when Ra(drug-enhancer-skin) is minimized
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- Drug HSP values (dD, dP, dH)
- Skin (stratum corneum) HSP values (dD, dP, dH) - literature reference values
- Penetration enhancer HSP values (dD, dP, dH)
- Drug molecular weight and lipophilicity (log P)
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for common enhancers (fatty acids, terpenes, surfactants, Azone, DMSO)
- Published stratum corneum HSP values (Karande, Mitragotri, etc.)
- Group contribution methods for novel enhancers
- Literature compilations of skin permeation data for validation
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Molecular weight of drug (< 500 Da preferred for transdermal)
- Log P / log Ko/w of drug
- Melting point of drug
- Enhancer concentration and vehicle composition
- Skin hydration level
- Formulation pH

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- Stratum corneum is a heterogeneous membrane with multiple parallel pathways (intercellular lipid, transcellular, appendageal), each with different effective HSP
- Penetration enhancement mechanisms are diverse (lipid fluidization, keratin denaturation, increased partitioning) and HSP captures only thermodynamic aspects
- Skin variability (age, site, hydration, disease state) introduces uncertainty in reference HSP values
- Dynamic interactions between enhancer and skin lipids (e.g., extraction, restructuring) are not captured by static HSP
- Synergistic effects of enhancer combinations are difficult to predict from individual HSP values
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Enhancers that work primarily by disrupting lipid lamellar structure (e.g., Azone) may not correlate well with simple HSP distance
- Hydrophilic drugs that permeate primarily via the transcellular route
- Macromolecular drugs (peptides, proteins) where size exclusion dominates
- Volatile enhancers where evaporation kinetics affect performance
- **validation_method**: 
- Franz diffusion cell experiments with excised skin or synthetic membranes
- In vivo tape stripping studies
- ATR-FTIR spectroscopy for SC lipid order assessment
- Confocal laser scanning microscopy for visualization of permeation pathways
- Skin impedance measurements

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Karande, P. and Mitragotri, S. | **journal**: Biochimica et Biophysica Acta - Biomembranes | **year**: 2009 | **title**: Enhancement of transdermal drug delivery via synergistic action of chemicals | **doi**: 10.1016/j.bbamem.2009.06.015
- **author**: Brinkmann, I. and Muller-Goymann, C.C. | **journal**: International Journal of Pharmaceutics | **year**: 2003 | **title**: Role of isopropyl myristate, isopropyl alcohol and a combination of both in hydrocortisone permeation across the human skin | **doi**: 10.1016/S0378-5173(03)00180-8
- **author**: Ghafourian, T. et al. | **journal**: International Journal of Pharmaceutics | **year**: 2010 | **title**: Solubility parameters and permeation through biological membranes | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **author**: Hansen, C.M. | **journal**: Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook (book) | **year**: 2007 | **title**: Chapter on biological materials and skin permeation | **doi**: ISBN 978-0849372483
- **regulatory_relevance**: FDA guidance on topical dermatological products supports science-based enhancer selection. HSP-based rationale is accepted in ANDA submissions for topical generics. EMA guideline on quality of transdermal patches references physicochemical compatibility approaches.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Pharmaceutical (topical and transdermal drug delivery)
- Cosmetics and personal care
- Contract development and manufacturing organizations (CDMO)
- Medical device companies (transdermal patches)
- **commercial_tools**: HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice), COSMOtherm, Gaussian (for ab initio HSP calculation), SKIN-CAD (proprietary transdermal design tools)

**Uncertain fields**: typical_red_threshold, first_reported_year, key_references[2].doi

### Category: Polymer/Materials

#### 3D Printing Solvent Smoothing
**Japanese**: 3Dプリント溶剤スムージング | **Maturity**: emerging

> HSP distance between a thermoplastic 3D printing filament (ABS, PLA, PETG, ASA, etc.) and a vapor or liquid solvent is used to predict surface smoothing effectiveness. When the solvent HSP is within the polymer's solubility sphere (RED < 1), the solvent dissolves and redistributes the surface layer, eliminating layer lines and improving surface finish. Controlled exposure time and solvent concentration allow graduated smoothing from light polish to near-injection-mold quality. HSP matching enables rational solvent selection that balances smoothing rate, dimensional accuracy, and safety.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The solvent must have sufficient thermodynamic affinity to the printed polymer surface to swell and partially dissolve the outermost layer. The HSP distance Ra between solvent and polymer determines whether dissolution occurs: solvents within the polymer's Hansen solubility sphere (RED < 1) will dissolve the surface, while those just outside (1.0 < RED < 1.3) may only swell it, providing gentler smoothing. The rate of surface dissolution depends on how deep within the sphere the solvent sits (lower RED = faster attack). Vapor-phase smoothing adds mass transport kinetics: the solvent vapor partial pressure and diffusion coefficient into the polymer determine the smoothing rate alongside thermodynamic compatibility.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_solv-dD_poly)^2 + (dP_solv-dP_poly)^2 + (dH_solv-dH_poly)^2); RED = Ra/R0_polymer
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: RED < 1.0 for effective smoothing; RED 0.8-1.0 preferred for controlled smoothing without over-dissolution; RED < 0.5 may cause excessive swelling and dimensional loss
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP of the filament polymer (dD, dP, dH)
- Interaction radius R0 of the polymer
- HSP of candidate smoothing solvent (dD, dP, dH)
- Vapor pressure of solvent at treatment temperature
- Exposure time and temperature
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database (common solvents well-documented)
- Published polymer HSP values (ABS, PLA, PETG in Hansen handbook and literature)
- 3D printing community empirical data (maker forums, YouTube documentation)
- Group contribution estimation for novel bio-solvents
- Filament manufacturer datasheets (limited HSP data)
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Solvent vapor pressure at treatment temperature
- Solvent boiling point and flash point (safety)
- Polymer glass transition temperature (Tg)
- Part wall thickness and infill percentage
- Exposure time and chamber geometry
- Solvent diffusion coefficient in the polymer

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- HSP predicts whether a solvent can dissolve the polymer surface but does not quantify the smoothing rate or final surface roughness
- Filament blends and additives (colorants, UV stabilizers) may shift effective HSP from published pure-polymer values
- Vapor-phase smoothing kinetics depend on chamber design, temperature uniformity, and solvent concentration, which are not captured by HSP alone
- Crystalline regions in semi-crystalline polymers (PLA, PETG) are more resistant to solvent attack than amorphous regions
- Layer adhesion quality and print orientation affect smoothing uniformity
- Multi-material prints present varying HSP targets on a single part
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- PLA is difficult to smooth because few common safe solvents fall within its solubility sphere; ethyl acetate works but slowly
- Over-exposure to strong solvents (acetone on ABS at RED ~0.3) causes excessive swelling, warping, and loss of detail
- Stress-cracking from solvents at RED slightly above 1.0 (environmental stress cracking rather than smoothing)
- Filled or composite filaments (carbon fiber, glass fiber) do not smooth uniformly due to exposed filler particles
- **validation_method**: 
- Surface profilometry (Ra roughness measurement before/after smoothing)
- Optical microscopy of layer line visibility
- Dimensional measurement with calipers or CMM for distortion assessment
- Weight gain/loss measurement to quantify solvent absorption
- Tensile testing to check for mechanical property degradation

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: low
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: binary
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Galantucci, L.M.; Lavecchia, F.; Percoco, G. | **title**: Experimental study aiming to enhance the surface finish of fused deposition modeled parts | **journal**: CIRP Annals - Manufacturing Technology | **year**: 2009 | **doi**: 10.1016/j.cirp.2009.03.071
- **author**: Singh, R.; Singh, S.; Singh, I.P.; Fabbrocino, F.; Fraternali, F. | **title**: Investigation for surface finish improvement of FDM parts by vapor smoothing process | **journal**: Composites Part B: Engineering | **year**: 2017 | **doi**: 10.1016/j.compositesb.2016.11.036
- **author**: Tantillo, A. et al. | **title**: Solvent vapor smoothing of three-dimensional printed objects using Hansen solubility parameters [uncertain] | **journal**: [uncertain] | **year**: [uncertain] | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **author**: Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook, 2nd Edition | **journal**: CRC Press | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1201/9781420006834
- **regulatory_relevance**: No direct regulatory framework, but solvent selection must comply with workplace exposure limits (OELs), VOC regulations, and REACH restrictions. HSP-based screening helps identify safer solvent alternatives that meet environmental and health regulations while still achieving effective smoothing.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Rapid prototyping and product development
- Consumer products (customized goods)
- Medical device prototyping
- Architectural modeling
- Hobbyist/maker community
- Dental and jewelry casting patterns
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice)
- Polymaker Polysher (commercial PVB smoothing system)
- XTC-3D and similar post-processing coatings (indirect)
- Zortrax Apoller (commercial vapor smoothing chamber)

**Uncertain fields**: literature.key_references[2].title, literature.key_references[2].journal, literature.key_references[2].year, literature.key_references[2].doi, literature.first_reported_year

#### Copolymer HSP Estimation
**Japanese**: コポリマーHSP推算 | **Maturity**: established

> Hansen Solubility Parameters for copolymers are estimated by a weighted average of the HSP values of the constituent homopolymer segments, weighted by their volume (or mole/weight) fractions. This approach applies to random, alternating, block, and graft copolymers, though the physical interpretation differs: random copolymers behave as homogeneous entities where averaged HSP directly applies, while block and graft copolymers may exhibit microphase separation where individual block HSP values remain relevant. The method enables rapid estimation of copolymer solubility behavior without full experimental characterization.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The core principle is linear additivity of HSP contributions from each monomer unit, weighted by their volume fraction in the copolymer. For a copolymer of monomers A and B with volume fractions phi_A and phi_B, each HSP component is calculated as delta_i(copolymer) = phi_A * delta_i(A) + phi_B * delta_i(B). This assumes that the contribution of each monomer to the overall cohesive energy density is proportional to its volume fraction, which is most valid for random copolymers where monomers are statistically distributed. For block copolymers, the averaged HSP predicts overall solubility, but individual block HSP values determine selective solvent behavior and microdomain swelling. Graft copolymers similarly require consideration of backbone vs. graft arm HSP for interfacial and compatibilizer applications.
- **key_equation**: dD_copoly = sum(phi_i * dD_i); dP_copoly = sum(phi_i * dP_i); dH_copoly = sum(phi_i * dH_i); where phi_i = volume fraction of monomer i
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: No specific threshold; estimated copolymer HSP is used as input for standard RED < 1.0 solubility criterion with solvents or other polymers
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: low

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) of each homopolymer corresponding to the monomer units
- Volume fraction (or weight fraction with density conversion) of each monomer in the copolymer
- Copolymer architecture type (random, block, graft, alternating)
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database (homopolymer HSP values)
- Group contribution methods (Van Krevelen, Hoftyzer-Van Krevelen) for novel monomers
- Literature compilations of homopolymer HSP (Hansen 2007, Van Krevelen 2009)
- Experimental solubility testing of the copolymer for validation
- Monomer molar volume data from density measurements or literature
- **data_availability_score**: 4
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Copolymer composition (precisely determined by NMR or elemental analysis)
- Sequence distribution (random vs. blocky) from reactivity ratios
- Monomer densities for weight-to-volume fraction conversion
- Block lengths (for block copolymers)
- Tg of copolymer (for validation of effective composition)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- Linear mixing rule assumes no interaction effects between monomer units, which is an approximation; intramolecular interactions in the copolymer can shift effective HSP
- Block copolymers with microphase separation cannot be fully described by a single averaged HSP set; each phase has its own effective HSP
- Volume fraction estimation may be inaccurate if significant volume change occurs on copolymerization
- Does not account for sequence distribution effects in random copolymers (blocky vs. truly random)
- Alternating copolymers may behave differently from the linear average prediction due to regular dipole arrangements
- Accuracy decreases for copolymers with strongly interacting monomers (e.g., acid/base pairs)
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Block copolymers in selective solvents: averaged HSP predicts dissolution but only one block dissolves, leading to micelle formation
- Strongly hydrogen-bonding copolymers where intramolecular H-bonding reduces effective dH below the linear average
- Copolymers with charge-transfer interactions between donor/acceptor monomers
- Low copolymer compositions where trace monomer dramatically alters surface behavior but minimally affects bulk HSP
- **validation_method**: 
- Solubility testing of copolymer in a range of solvents and comparing with Hansen sphere
- Turbidimetric titration to determine solubility boundaries
- Inverse gas chromatography (IGC) at infinite dilution for direct HSP measurement
- Swelling experiments in solvent series
- Comparison of predicted vs. measured Tg using Fox or Gordon-Taylor equation

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: low
- **ui_pattern**: optimization
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed
- **multi_component_complexity**: binary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- Hansen, C.M. 'Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook', 2nd Ed., CRC Press, 2007, Chapter 5
- Van Krevelen, D.W.; Te Nijenhuis, K. 'Properties of Polymers', 4th Ed., Elsevier, 2009, Chapter 7
- Barton, A.F.M. 'CRC Handbook of Solubility Parameters and Other Cohesion Parameters', 2nd Ed., CRC Press, 1991
- Lindvig, T.; Michelsen, M.L.; Kontogeorgis, G.M. 'A Flory-Huggins model based on the Hansen solubility parameters', Fluid Phase Equilibria, 2002, 203(1-2), 247-260

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Coatings (acrylic and vinyl copolymer resin design for solvent selection)
- Adhesives (copolymer composition optimization for substrate matching)
- Packaging (copolymer barrier film development)
- Biomedical (copolymer biomaterial design for drug delivery carriers)
- Plastics compounding (compatibilizer copolymer design for blends)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (copolymer HSP estimation module with composition input)
- COSMOtherm (segment-based approach for copolymer sigma profiles)
- Y-MB (Yamamoto Molecule Breaking method for polymer segment contributions)
- Materials Studio (Synthia for copolymer property prediction)

**Uncertain fields**: first_reported_year, regulatory_relevance

---

#### Crystalline Polymer Dissolution Temperature
**Japanese**: 結晶性ポリマー溶解温度 | **Maturity**: established

> For semicrystalline polymers, dissolution requires both thermodynamic compatibility (low HSP distance) and sufficient thermal energy to overcome crystalline lattice energy. The HSP distance between polymer and solvent, combined with the polymer melting point (Tm), predicts whether dissolution will occur and at what temperature. Solvents with small Ra to the polymer dissolve it at lower temperatures, while poor solvents may only dissolve the polymer above Tm. This framework also predicts LCST and UCST dissolution behavior, where temperature-dependent HSP shifts cause phase separation or dissolution transitions.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Crystalline polymer dissolution is governed by two thermodynamic requirements: (1) the free energy of mixing must be negative (favorable polymer-solvent HSP match, low chi), and (2) the crystal must be destabilized (temperature must approach or exceed the equilibrium dissolution temperature Td). The dissolution temperature Td is depressed below Tm according to the Flory diluent theory: 1/Td - 1/Tm0 = (R/dHu) * (Vu/V1) * (phi_1 - chi * phi_1^2), where dHu is the heat of fusion per repeat unit, Vu is the molar volume of the repeat unit, V1 is the molar volume of the solvent, and phi_1 is the solvent volume fraction. The chi parameter is estimated from HSP distance: chi = Vs * Ra^2 / (6RT). Solvents with lower Ra produce lower chi, which leads to greater Tm depression and lower dissolution temperatures. For LCST behavior, HSP values (particularly dH) decrease with increasing temperature, potentially causing phase separation on heating when hydrogen bonding weakens.
- **key_equation**: 1/Td - 1/Tm0 = (R/dHu)*(Vu/V1)*(phi1 - chi*phi1^2); chi = Vs*Ra^2/(6*R*T); Ra = sqrt(4*ddD^2 + ddP^2 + ddH^2)
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: RED < 1.0 for potential dissolution; dissolution temperature decreases as RED decreases. RED < 0.5 typically allows dissolution well below Tm.
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: high

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) for the crystalline polymer (amorphous phase)
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) for the solvent
- Polymer melting point (Tm0, equilibrium value)
- Heat of fusion per repeat unit (dHu)
- Molar volumes of solvent (V1) and polymer repeat unit (Vu)
- Temperature range of interest
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for polymer and solvent HSP
- DSC measurements for Tm and dHu of the specific polymer grade
- Literature values for common semicrystalline polymers (PE, PP, PET, nylon, PVDF, etc.)
- Temperature-dependent HSP corrections from HSPiP or literature correlations
- ATHAS databank for heat capacity and heat of fusion data
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Degree of crystallinity
- Lamellar thickness and crystal perfection
- Polymer molecular weight and molecular weight distribution
- Heating rate (kinetic effect on dissolution)
- Polymer morphology (spherulitic, oriented, etc.)
- Temperature coefficients of HSP (d(delta)/dT)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- HSP values are typically reported at 25C; temperature dependence corrections are approximate and vary by method
- Equilibrium Tm0 is difficult to determine precisely; observed Tm depends on crystallization conditions and heating rate
- Flory diluent theory assumes equilibrium thermodynamics; actual dissolution may be kinetically limited by crystal thickness and chain mobility
- Chi parameter estimated from HSP is concentration-independent, while real chi often depends on composition
- Does not account for solvent-induced crystallization, which can occur when amorphous regions dissolve preferentially
- LCST prediction requires accurate temperature-dependent HSP data, which is scarce for many polymer-solvent systems
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- High-density polyethylene in some solvents dissolves at much higher temperatures than predicted due to thick lamellae and high crystallinity
- PVDF/DMF system shows LCST behavior driven by strong H-bonding temperature dependence, poorly captured by standard HSP temperature corrections
- Polymorphic polymers (e.g., nylon 6 alpha vs. gamma form) dissolve at different temperatures despite identical HSP
- Ultra-high molecular weight polymers where dissolution kinetics dominate over thermodynamic predictions
- **validation_method**: 
- Cloud point measurements as function of temperature
- DSC dissolution endotherm detection
- Hot-stage optical microscopy observing crystal disappearance
- Turbidimetry during controlled heating/cooling cycles
- Rheological measurements (viscosity change at dissolution)

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: high
- **ui_pattern**: pairwise
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed
- **multi_component_complexity**: binary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- Flory, P.J. 'Principles of Polymer Chemistry', Cornell University Press, 1953, Chapter 13
- Hansen, C.M. 'Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook', 2nd Ed., CRC Press, 2007, Chapter 6
- Miller-Chou, B.A.; Koenig, J.L. 'A review of polymer dissolution', Progress in Polymer Science, 2003, 28(8), 1223-1270
- Papageorgiou, G.Z.; Bikiaris, D.N. 'Crystallization and melting behavior of three biodegradable poly(alkylene succinates)', Polymer, 2005, 46(26), 12081-12092
- Lindvig, T.; Michelsen, M.L.; Kontogeorgis, G.M. 'A Flory-Huggins model based on the Hansen solubility parameters', Fluid Phase Equilibria, 2002, 203(1-2), 247-260

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Textile/fiber industry (solution spinning of crystalline polymers like UHMWPE, PAN)
- Pharmaceutical (amorphous solid dispersion design for poorly soluble crystalline APIs)
- Membrane manufacturing (PVDF, cellulose acetate membrane casting from solution)
- Recycling (selective dissolution of crystalline plastics for chemical recycling)
- Polymer processing (solvent welding and bonding of semicrystalline parts)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (with temperature-dependent HSP estimation)
- COSMOtherm (temperature-dependent sigma profiles)
- Aspen Plus / Aspen Polymers (Flory-Huggins with T-dependent chi)
- DETHERM database (thermophysical property data for validation)

**Uncertain fields**: first_reported_year, regulatory_relevance, sensitivity_to_hbond_split

---

#### Environmental Stress Cracking (ESC)
**Japanese**: 環境応力亀裂（ESC） | **Maturity**: mature

> Environmental Stress Cracking occurs when a stressed polymer part is exposed to a chemical agent that would not cause failure in the absence of stress. HSP-based RED values between the polymer and the chemical environment predict ESC risk: chemicals with RED values near or below 1.0 (partial solubility) are the most dangerous ESC agents, as they can absorb into the stressed polymer surface and promote craze-to-crack transitions. This approach is critical for material selection in chemical environments, enabling rapid screening of polymer-chemical combinations for ESC susceptibility without lengthy mechanical testing.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: ESC is driven by localized plasticization and chain disentanglement at crack tips where absorbed solvent molecules reduce the local Tg and craze fibril stability. The HSP distance between polymer and environmental fluid determines the degree of absorption and plasticization. Critically, ESC does not require full dissolution (RED < 1.0); chemicals in the RED range of approximately 0.7-1.3 are often the most dangerous because they absorb enough to plasticize without fully dissolving the polymer. The mechanism involves: (1) stress-enhanced absorption at craze tips due to dilatation and increased free volume, (2) local reduction in yield stress and craze fibril strength, and (3) accelerated craze-to-crack transition. The RED threshold for ESC onset is typically lower than for bulk dissolution because mechanical stress effectively enlarges the solubility sphere. The delta_H (hydrogen bonding) component is particularly important for predicting ESC in polar polymers.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD1-dD2)^2 + (dP1-dP2)^2 + (dH1-dH2)^2); RED = Ra/R0; ESC risk is highest when 0.7 < RED < 1.3
- **red_direction**: threshold_specific
- **typical_red_threshold**: RED < 0.8 high ESC risk (also likely full dissolution); RED 0.8-1.0 significant ESC risk; RED 1.0-1.3 moderate ESC risk under high stress; RED > 1.3 low ESC risk
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) for the polymer
- Interaction radius R0 for the polymer
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) for the environmental chemical/fluid
- Applied stress level (or strain) on the polymer part
- Service temperature
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database (polymer and solvent HSP)
- Material supplier technical datasheets (polymer ESC resistance ratings)
- ASTM D1693 (ESCR bent strip test data for polyethylene)
- Literature compilations of ESC agent rankings
- Chemical product SDS sheets (for complex mixture HSP estimation)
- **data_availability_score**: 4
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Applied stress or strain level
- Polymer molecular weight and molecular weight distribution
- Degree of crystallinity and morphology
- Residual stress from processing (injection molding, thermoforming)
- Temperature of exposure
- Exposure duration
- Polymer orientation and weld line locations

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- ESC is a kinetic/mechanical phenomenon; HSP provides thermodynamic screening but cannot predict time-to-failure quantitatively
- Stress level strongly modulates the effective R0 (stressed polymer has larger effective solubility sphere), but this stress-modified R0 is not well-characterized for most polymers
- Complex chemical mixtures (detergents, oils, cleaning agents) require HSP estimation of the mixture, introducing additional uncertainty
- Polymer processing history (residual stress, orientation, crystallinity) dramatically affects ESC resistance but is not captured by HSP
- Does not distinguish between slow crack growth and rapid brittle failure modes
- Surface treatments, coatings, and fillers modify ESC behavior beyond what HSP predicts
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Polycarbonate ESC with certain aromatic solvents occurs at RED values somewhat above 1.0 due to specific aromatic-aromatic interactions not fully captured by HSP
- Surfactant-induced ESC in polyethylene: aqueous surfactant solutions have high RED but still cause ESC by surface wetting and reducing surface energy at crack tips
- Silicone fluids causing ESC in some elastomers despite seemingly high RED values
- Mixtures where minor component with low RED drives ESC despite high overall mixture RED
- **validation_method**: 
- ASTM D1693 bent strip test (for PE)
- ASTM F2136 notched constant tensile load (NCTL) test
- Slow crack growth testing (ASTM F1473 PENT test)
- Full notch creep test (FNCT) per ISO 16770
- Strain-rate controlled tensile testing in chemical environment
- Bergen jig constant strain ESC test

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: low
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: binary
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- Hansen, C.M.; Just, L. 'Prediction of Environmental Stress Cracking in Plastics with Hansen Solubility Parameters', Industrial & Engineering Chemistry Research, 2001, 40(1), 21-25
- Wright, D.C. 'Environmental Stress Cracking of Plastics', Rapra Technology, 1996
- Hansen, C.M. 'Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook', 2nd Ed., CRC Press, 2007, Chapter 18
- Arnold, J.C. 'The influence of liquid uptake on environmental stress cracking of glassy polymers', Materials Science and Engineering A, 2004, 384(1-2), 249-256
- Soares, J.B.P.; Abbott, R.F.; Kim, J.D. 'Environmental stress cracking resistance of polyethylene: the use of CRYSTAF and SEC to establish structure-property relationships', Journal of Polymer Science Part B, 2000, 38(10), 1267-1275
- **regulatory_relevance**: ESC testing is required for plastic piping (ASTM D1693, ISO 16770) in water/gas distribution standards; chemical resistance data including ESC is required for ATEX-rated plastic enclosures; FDA food-contact polymer parts must demonstrate resistance to ESC from cleaning agents

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Automotive (fuel system components, under-hood parts exposed to fluids)
- Packaging (HDPE containers for detergents, chemicals, agricultural products)
- Construction (plastic piping, geomembranes, window profiles)
- Medical devices (polymer components exposed to disinfectants and cleaning agents)
- Consumer products (appliance housings, electronic enclosures)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (ESC screening module with chemical-polymer RED mapping)
- CAMPUS plastics database (ESC ratings for commercial grades)
- UL Prospector (chemical resistance data for plastics)
- Matweb (material property database with ESC resistance data)

**Uncertain fields**: first_reported_year

---

#### Fiber Dyeability Prediction
**Japanese**: 繊維染色性予測 | **Maturity**: established

> Hansen Solubility Parameters (HSP) are used to predict the dyeing affinity, uptake rate, and wash fastness of dye-fiber systems. The HSP distance (Ra) between a dye molecule and a fiber polymer quantifies thermodynamic compatibility: smaller Ra indicates higher dye solubility within the fiber matrix, leading to greater dye uptake and improved fastness. This approach enables rational dye selection for specific fiber types (polyester, nylon, cellulose acetate, etc.) and helps optimize dyeing conditions. The method is particularly effective for disperse dyes on hydrophobic fibers where thermodynamic partitioning dominates over ionic interactions.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The dye molecule and the fiber polymer are each characterized by three HSP components (delta_d, delta_p, delta_h). The HSP distance Ra between the dye and fiber predicts thermodynamic affinity: a small Ra means the dye is soluble in the fiber matrix and will partition favorably from the dye bath into the fiber. The Flory-Huggins interaction parameter chi can be estimated from Ra, linking HSP distance to the free energy of mixing. For disperse dyes on polyester, the dye partitions from an aqueous phase into the polymer; if Ra(dye-fiber) is small, the dye-fiber interaction is energetically favorable, producing high exhaustion and good wash fastness. Conversely, large Ra indicates poor compatibility, leading to surface deposition rather than true dyeing, and poor fastness.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_dye - dD_fiber)^2 + (dP_dye - dP_fiber)^2 + (dH_dye - dH_fiber)^2); chi = V_dye * Ra^2 / (6 * R * T)
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: RED < 1.0 for good dye-fiber affinity; Ra < 5-8 MPa^0.5 for acceptable dyeing performance
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: high

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP of dye molecule (delta_d, delta_p, delta_h)
- HSP of fiber polymer (delta_d, delta_p, delta_h)
- Interaction radius R0 of fiber polymer (if using RED)
- Molar volume of dye molecule
- Dyeing temperature
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for common polymers (PET, PA6, cellulose acetate)
- Group contribution methods (Van Krevelen, Hoftyzer) for dye molecules
- Inverse gas chromatography (IGC) for fiber HSP measurement
- Published literature values for commercial dye classes
- Computational methods (COSMO-RS, molecular simulation) for novel dyes
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Dye molecular weight and molecular size
- Fiber crystallinity and glass transition temperature (Tg)
- Dyeing temperature and time
- Dye bath pH and auxiliary chemicals
- Fiber surface area and porosity
- Dye aqueous solubility

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: HSP-based prediction primarily captures thermodynamic compatibility and does not account for kinetic factors such as diffusion rate through the fiber matrix, which depends on fiber crystallinity, orientation, and free volume. Ionic dyes (acid, basic, reactive dyes) involve electrostatic interactions not captured by standard 3-parameter HSP. The approach works best for disperse dyes on hydrophobic fibers (PET, triacetate) where van der Waals and polar interactions dominate. For natural fibers (cotton, wool) with complex hierarchical structures and reactive sites, HSP alone provides incomplete prediction. Temperature dependence of HSP parameters is significant since industrial dyeing occurs at 80-130 degrees C, but most HSP data is measured at 25 degrees C. Fiber morphology (amorphous/crystalline ratio, draw ratio) strongly affects accessibility but is not captured by HSP.
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Ionic dye-fiber systems (acid dyes on nylon, reactive dyes on cotton) where electrostatic and covalent interactions dominate
- Highly crystalline fibers where dye penetration is kinetically limited despite favorable HSP match
- Dye aggregation in the bath reducing effective dye activity
- Carrier-assisted dyeing where a third component modifies fiber HSP in situ
- Fiber blends where preferential partitioning between fiber types complicates prediction
- **validation_method**: Dye exhaustion measurement (spectrophotometric analysis of dye bath before/after), color strength (K/S values), wash fastness testing (ISO 105-C06), rubbing fastness (ISO 105-X12), dye diffusion coefficient measurement via thin-film or sectioning techniques

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: binary
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook (Chapter on Fibers and Dyes) | **journal**: CRC Press | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1201/9781420006834
- **author**: Yao, L.; Lio, A.; et al. | **title**: Application of Hansen solubility parameters to predict compatibility of dye-fiber systems | **journal**: Textile Research Journal | **year**: 2015 | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **author**: Abbot, S.; Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice (HSPiP) - Textile Applications | **journal**: HSPiP Software Documentation | **year**: 2013 | **doi**: N/A
- **author**: Burkinshaw, S.M. | **title**: Chemical Principles of Synthetic Fibre Dyeing | **journal**: Springer | **year**: 1995 | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **regulatory_relevance**: No direct regulatory requirement, but HSP-based dye selection supports compliance with REACH restrictions on certain dye chemistries by enabling substitution screening. Relevant to OEKO-TEX and ZDHC (Zero Discharge of Hazardous Chemicals) initiatives for identifying safer dye alternatives.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: Textile manufacturing, Technical textiles, Automotive interior fabrics, Apparel and fashion, Home furnishing textiles
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice)
- COSMOtherm (dye HSP estimation via COSMO-RS)
- Materials Studio (molecular simulation for dye-fiber interactions)

**Uncertain fields**: literature.key_references[1].doi, literature.key_references[3].doi, literature.first_reported_year

---

#### Hydrogel Swelling Prediction
**Japanese**: ハイドロゲル膨潤予測 | **Maturity**: established

> HSP distance between a crosslinked hydrogel network and the swelling medium is used to predict the equilibrium swelling ratio. The Flory-Rehner theory links the polymer-solvent interaction parameter chi to swelling behavior, and chi can be estimated from HSP distance. Smaller HSP distance (better thermodynamic affinity) between the gel network and the swelling fluid leads to higher equilibrium swelling. This approach is applied in biomedical hydrogels (drug delivery, wound dressings, contact lenses), agricultural superabsorbent polymers, and soft actuator design.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Hydrogel swelling is governed by the balance between the osmotic driving force (mixing free energy favoring solvent uptake) and the elastic restoring force of the crosslinked network (opposing expansion). The mixing free energy is captured by the Flory-Huggins interaction parameter chi between the network polymer and the swelling medium. HSP distance Ra allows estimation of chi: chi ~ V_solvent * Ra^2 / (6RT). Lower chi (smaller Ra) means stronger thermodynamic affinity, greater osmotic driving force, and higher equilibrium swelling ratio. For hydrogels in aqueous media, the delta_h component is particularly important due to hydrogen bonding between water and hydrophilic network chains. The equilibrium swelling ratio Q can be predicted using the modified Flory-Rehner equation incorporating the HSP-derived chi parameter.
- **key_equation**: chi = V_s * Ra^2 / (6 * R * T); Flory-Rehner: ln(1 - phi_p) + phi_p + chi * phi_p^2 = -V_s * nu_e * (phi_p^(1/3) - phi_p/2); Q = 1/phi_p
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: Ra < 5 MPa^0.5 for significant swelling; RED < 1.0 indicates the solvent is within the gel solubility sphere and will cause substantial swelling
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: high
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP of hydrogel network polymer (delta_d, delta_p, delta_h)
- HSP of swelling medium (delta_d, delta_p, delta_h)
- Molar volume of swelling medium
- Crosslink density (nu_e) of the hydrogel network
- Temperature
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for common polymers and solvents
- Group contribution methods for hydrogel polymer HSP (PEG, PVA, PAA, PHEMA, etc.)
- Published literature for hydrogel HSP values
- Experimental swelling studies in solvent series (inverse method to determine gel HSP)
- Molecular simulation for novel hydrogel chemistries
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Crosslink density or molecular weight between crosslinks (Mc)
- Polymer volume fraction in the reference state
- pH and ionic strength of swelling medium (for ionic hydrogels)
- Temperature (LCST/UCST behavior for thermoresponsive gels)
- Degree of ionization (for polyelectrolyte gels)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: HSP-based chi estimation provides a reasonable first approximation but the Flory-Huggins chi parameter is known to be concentration-dependent for many polymer-solvent systems, whereas HSP gives a single constant value. For polyelectrolyte hydrogels (e.g., polyacrylic acid), ionic osmotic pressure contributions to swelling are not captured by HSP. Thermoresponsive hydrogels (PNIPAM) exhibit LCST behavior driven by entropic effects that HSP poorly describes. The hydrogen bonding component is critical for aqueous systems but is the least accurately determined HSP parameter. Network defects (dangling ends, entanglements, loops) affect swelling but are not reflected in HSP. For mixed solvent systems, composition-dependent HSP must be considered.
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Polyelectrolyte hydrogels where Donnan equilibrium and ionic osmotic pressure dominate swelling
- Thermoresponsive hydrogels (PNIPAM) near the LCST where entropic contributions dominate
- pH-responsive hydrogels where ionization state drastically changes effective HSP
- Biological fluids with complex composition that cannot be represented by simple HSP
- Highly swollen gels (Q > 50) where Flory-Rehner assumptions break down
- **validation_method**: Gravimetric swelling measurement (mass change vs. time until equilibrium), volumetric swelling ratio determination, swelling kinetics in various solvents, rheological characterization of swollen gels, DSC for polymer-solvent interaction assessment

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: pairwise
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: binary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Flory, P.J.; Rehner, J. | **title**: Statistical mechanics of cross-linked polymer networks: II. Swelling | **journal**: Journal of Chemical Physics | **year**: 1943 | **doi**: 10.1063/1.1723792
- **author**: Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook, 2nd Edition | **journal**: CRC Press | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1201/9781420006834
- **author**: Peppas, N.A.; Hilt, J.Z.; Khademhosseini, A.; Langer, R. | **title**: Hydrogels in biology and medicine: From molecular principles to bionanotechnology | **journal**: Advanced Materials | **year**: 2006 | **doi**: 10.1002/adma.200501612
- **author**: Brannon-Peppas, L.; Peppas, N.A. | **title**: Equilibrium swelling behavior of pH-sensitive hydrogels | **journal**: Chemical Engineering Science | **year**: 1991 | **doi**: 10.1016/0009-2509(91)80164-S
- **first_reported_year**: 1943
- **regulatory_relevance**: Biomedical hydrogels require FDA 510(k) or PMA clearance where swelling characterization is a standard material property test. ISO 10993 biocompatibility standards reference swelling behavior. EU Medical Device Regulation (MDR 2017/745) requires material characterization including swelling properties. Agricultural superabsorbents may fall under EPA or EFSA regulations depending on application.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Biomedical devices (contact lenses, wound dressings)
- Drug delivery systems
- Agricultural superabsorbent polymers
- Personal care products (diapers, hygiene)
- Soft robotics and actuators
- Tissue engineering scaffolds
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice)
- COMSOL Multiphysics (hydrogel swelling simulation)
- Materials Studio (molecular simulation of gel-solvent interactions)
- COSMOtherm (solvent-polymer interaction prediction)

---

#### Polymer Additive Migration/Blooming
**Japanese**: ポリマー添加剤移行・ブルーミング予測 | **Maturity**: established

> HSP distance between a polymer additive (stabilizer, plasticizer, flame retardant, antioxidant, UV absorber, etc.) and the polymer matrix is used to predict the tendency and rate of additive migration to the surface (blooming) or into contacting media. Smaller HSP distance indicates better thermodynamic compatibility and lower migration tendency. Larger HSP distance means the additive has lower solubility in the polymer matrix, creating a thermodynamic driving force for migration, surface blooming, or extraction. This is critical for food-contact packaging compliance, medical device biocompatibility, polymer long-term durability, and product aesthetics.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Additive migration from a polymer matrix is driven by the thermodynamic activity of the additive in the polymer phase. When the HSP distance Ra between an additive and the polymer is large, the additive has limited solubility in the polymer, and any concentration above the solubility limit will phase-separate and migrate to the surface (blooming) or into a contacting medium. The Flory-Huggins chi parameter, estimated from Ra, determines the equilibrium solubility of the additive in the polymer. Below the solubility limit, migration is slow and diffusion-controlled; above it, the excess additive has high thermodynamic activity and migrates rapidly. For extraction into a contacting medium (food, body fluid), the partition coefficient between polymer and medium can be estimated by comparing Ra(additive-polymer) with Ra(additive-medium): the additive partitions preferentially into the phase with smaller Ra.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_add - dD_poly)^2 + (dP_add - dP_poly)^2 + (dH_add - dH_poly)^2); chi = V_add * Ra^2 / (6 * R * T); Partition coefficient K ~ exp((chi_add-medium - chi_add-polymer) * V_add / (R * T))
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: RED < 1.0 for good additive-polymer compatibility (low migration risk); RED > 1.2-1.5 indicates significant blooming/migration risk
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: high

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP of the additive (delta_d, delta_p, delta_h)
- HSP of the polymer matrix (delta_d, delta_p, delta_h)
- HSP of the contacting medium (if evaluating extraction)
- Molar volume of the additive
- Temperature of use/storage
- Additive concentration in the polymer
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for polymers and common additives
- Group contribution methods for additive HSP (Van Krevelen, Stefanis-Panayiotou)
- Published literature for plasticizers, antioxidants, UV stabilizers
- Food simulant HSP values (water, ethanol, acetic acid, olive oil equivalents)
- Experimental solubility sphere fitting for proprietary additives
- **data_availability_score**: 4
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Additive molecular weight and molecular size (affects diffusion coefficient)
- Polymer glass transition temperature and crystallinity
- Temperature of application and storage
- Thickness of polymer article
- Additive concentration relative to solubility limit
- Surface area to volume ratio of the article

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: HSP-based prediction captures thermodynamic compatibility (solubility) but migration rate also depends strongly on kinetic factors: diffusion coefficient of the additive in the polymer (which depends on additive size, polymer Tg, free volume, crystallinity). Crystalline regions act as impermeable barriers, reducing effective diffusivity but not changing thermodynamic driving force. At very low additive concentrations (well below solubility limit), migration is diffusion-controlled and HSP has limited predictive value for rate. Surface blooming involves nucleation and growth kinetics not addressed by HSP. Multi-additive systems may show synergistic or antagonistic effects on migration not captured by simple pairwise HSP analysis.
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Low-molecular-weight additives with high diffusivity that migrate rapidly even when thermodynamically compatible
- Additive aggregation or crystallization on the surface driven by surface energy effects rather than bulk solubility
- Reactive additives that chemically bind to the polymer (e.g., reactive UV stabilizers) where migration is blocked regardless of HSP
- Multi-layer structures where migration path involves multiple polymer phases
- High-temperature processing that degrades additives, changing their HSP
- **validation_method**: Migration testing according to EU 10/2011 and FDA protocols (overall migration, specific migration), surface analysis (ATR-FTIR, XPS) for blooming detection, extraction studies with food simulants, accelerated aging at elevated temperatures, gravimetric loss measurements

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: ternary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook, 2nd Edition | **journal**: CRC Press | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1201/9781420006834
- **author**: Wypych, G. | **title**: Handbook of Plasticizers, 3rd Edition | **journal**: ChemTec Publishing | **year**: 2017 | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **author**: Begley, T.; Castle, L.; Feigenbaum, A.; et al. | **title**: Evaluation of migration models that might be used in support of regulations for food-contact plastics | **journal**: Food Additives & Contaminants | **year**: 2005 | **doi**: 10.1080/02652030500098894
- **author**: Piringer, O.G.; Baner, A.L. | **title**: Plastic Packaging: Interactions with Food and Pharmaceuticals | **journal**: Wiley-VCH | **year**: 2008 | **doi**: 10.1002/9783527621422
- **regulatory_relevance**: Highly relevant to food-contact material regulations. EU Regulation 10/2011 on plastic food-contact materials requires specific migration limits (SMLs) for listed additives. FDA 21 CFR 170-199 governs food-contact substances. EU REACH requires assessment of substance migration for articles. HSP-based screening is accepted as a supporting tool for migration modeling in JRC (EU Joint Research Centre) guidelines. Commission Regulation (EU) 2017/752 references migration modeling approaches.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Food and beverage packaging
- Pharmaceutical packaging
- Medical devices
- Automotive (interior component outgassing)
- Wire and cable insulation
- Building and construction materials
- Toy and childcare article safety
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice)
- AKTS SML (Specific Migration Limit software)
- FABES MIGRATOOL (migration modeling)
- COSMOtherm (additive-polymer interaction prediction)
- Piringer model implementations in various software

**Uncertain fields**: literature.key_references[1].doi, literature.first_reported_year

---

#### Polymer Blend Miscibility
**Japanese**: ポリマーブレンド相溶性 | **Maturity**: mature

> HSP distance (Ra) and RED number between two polymers are used to predict whether a polymer blend will be miscible, immiscible, or partially miscible. The Flory-Huggins interaction parameter chi can be estimated from the HSP distance, providing a thermodynamic basis for miscibility prediction. When RED < 1 (Ra < R0 of either polymer), the polymers are expected to be miscible. This approach is widely used for rapid screening of polymer pairs before experimental blending trials, though entropic effects and specific interactions may require additional consideration.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The underlying principle is that two polymers will be miscible when their HSP values are sufficiently close in Hansen space. The Ra distance quantifies the HSP difference between two polymers. By comparing Ra to the interaction radius R0 of each polymer (determined from solubility sphere fitting), the RED number predicts compatibility. Additionally, the Flory-Huggins chi parameter can be estimated from HSP distance using chi = Vs * (delta1 - delta2)^2 / (RT), where the squared difference is expanded into the three HSP components. Miscibility requires chi < chi_critical = 0.5 * (1/sqrt(N1) + 1/sqrt(N2))^2, where N1 and N2 are degrees of polymerization. Because polymers have very low combinatorial entropy of mixing (large N), even small positive chi values lead to immiscibility, making the HSP distance criterion particularly stringent for high molecular weight pairs.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD1-dD2)^2 + (dP1-dP2)^2 + (dH1-dH2)^2); RED = Ra/R0; chi = Vs*Ra^2/(6*R*T)
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: RED < 1.0 for miscibility; RED < 0.5 strongly suggests miscible blend; RED 0.5-1.0 partial miscibility possible; RED > 1.0 immiscible
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) for polymer A
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) for polymer B
- Interaction radius R0 for each polymer
- Molar volume of reference solvent (Vs) for chi estimation
- Temperature (for chi calculation)
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database (extensive polymer HSP library)
- Group contribution methods (Van Krevelen, Hoy, Hoftyzer)
- Inverse gas chromatography (IGC) measurements
- Solvent swelling/dissolution experiments with sphere fitting
- Literature compilations (Hansen 2007, Barton 1991)
- **data_availability_score**: 4
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Molecular weight (Mw) of both polymers (affects chi_critical)
- Glass transition temperature (Tg) of each polymer
- Blend composition ratio
- Processing temperature
- Crystallinity (if semicrystalline polymers involved)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- HSP approach assumes van Laar / regular solution behavior and does not capture specific interactions (e.g., hydrogen bond complementarity, charge transfer complexes) that can promote miscibility beyond HSP prediction
- Entropic contributions are approximated; for polymer-polymer systems the combinatorial entropy is very small, making enthalpy dominant, but non-combinatorial (equation-of-state) entropy effects are neglected
- Does not account for LCST/UCST behavior driven by free volume or compressibility effects
- R0 values for polymers are less well-defined than for solvents due to limited number of good solvents
- Blend morphology (phase size, co-continuous vs dispersed) cannot be predicted from HSP alone
- Crystallization effects in semicrystalline blends are not captured
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Polymer pairs with strong specific interactions (e.g., PMMA/PVF2 miscibility driven by C-H...F-C interactions) predicted immiscible by HSP but are experimentally miscible
- LCST-type phase separation (e.g., PS/PVME) where blends are miscible at low temperature but phase-separate on heating, not predicted by static HSP
- Blends where copolymer intramolecular repulsion drives miscibility (e.g., styrene-acrylonitrile copolymers with certain partners)
- High Mw blends where even tiny HSP differences yield chi > chi_critical
- **validation_method**: 
- DSC measurement of single vs. dual Tg
- Cloud point determination (optical clarity vs. temperature)
- Small-angle X-ray or neutron scattering (SAXS/SANS)
- Optical microscopy of blend morphology
- Dynamic mechanical analysis (DMA) for Tg detection

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: pairwise
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: binary
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- Hansen, C.M. 'Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook', 2nd Ed., CRC Press, 2007
- Flory, P.J. 'Principles of Polymer Chemistry', Cornell University Press, 1953
- Van Krevelen, D.W.; Te Nijenhuis, K. 'Properties of Polymers', 4th Ed., Elsevier, 2009
- Zeng, W.; Du, Y.; Xue, Y.; Frisch, H.L. 'Solubility Parameters', in Physical Properties of Polymers Handbook, Springer, 2007
- Coleman, M.M.; Graf, J.F.; Painter, P.C. 'Specific Interactions and the Miscibility of Polymer Blends', Technomic, 1991

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Automotive (engineering plastic blends for under-hood components)
- Packaging (barrier film blends, recycled polymer compatibility)
- Electronics (polymer alloy housings, insulation blends)
- Medical devices (biocompatible polymer blend screening)
- Recycling (compatibility screening for mixed plastic waste streams)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice) - polymer blend module
- COSMOtherm (COSMO-RS based, complementary to HSP)
- Materials Studio (Synthia module for HSP prediction)
- PolyInfo / NIMS polymer database
- Aspen Polymers (Flory-Huggins chi estimation)

**Uncertain fields**: first_reported_year, regulatory_relevance

#### Polymer Membrane Gas Permeability
**Japanese**: 高分子膜ガス透過性予測 | **Maturity**: established

> HSP distance between gas molecules (O2, N2, CO2, CH4, H2) and membrane polymer is used to predict gas permeability coefficients and ideal selectivity ratios. The approach is based on the solution-diffusion model: gas permeability P = S x D, where solubility S correlates with HSP affinity between gas and polymer, while diffusivity D relates to polymer free volume and chain mobility. Smaller HSP distance between a gas and the polymer matrix indicates higher solubility of that gas, enabling prediction of permeability ranking and membrane material selection for gas separation applications such as CO2 capture, O2/N2 enrichment, and natural gas sweetening.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Gas permeation through dense polymer membranes follows the solution-diffusion mechanism. The solubility coefficient S of a gas in a polymer correlates with the thermodynamic affinity between the gas and polymer, which can be quantified by the HSP distance Ra. A small Ra(gas-polymer) implies favorable gas-polymer interactions and higher solubility. For condensable gases (CO2, hydrocarbons), the HSP approach is particularly effective because solubility dominates permeability. The Flory-Huggins interaction parameter chi, estimable from Ra, directly relates to the Henry's law solubility coefficient. Gas selectivity (alpha_A/B = P_A/P_B) can be predicted by comparing Ra values: if Ra(A-polymer) < Ra(B-polymer), gas A will generally have higher solubility and thus higher permeability (for gases of similar size). However, size-sieving (diffusivity selectivity) must also be considered, especially for glassy polymers.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_gas - dD_polymer)^2 + (dP_gas - dP_polymer)^2 + (dH_gas - dH_polymer)^2); ln(S) ~ -Ra^2 * V_gas / (R * T); Selectivity alpha_A/B = (S_A/S_B) * (D_A/D_B)
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: No universal RED threshold; Ra < 6 MPa^0.5 generally indicates significant gas solubility in the polymer
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: high

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP of target gas molecules (delta_d, delta_p, delta_h)
- HSP of membrane polymer (delta_d, delta_p, delta_h)
- Molar volume of gas (condensed or effective)
- Operating temperature and pressure
- Polymer free volume fraction (for diffusivity estimation)
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for common polymers
- Published gas HSP values (Yampolskii, Freeman compilations)
- Group contribution methods for novel polymer HSP
- NIST Webbook for gas physical properties
- Experimental IGC data for polymer HSP
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Polymer glass transition temperature (Tg)
- Polymer fractional free volume (FFV)
- Gas kinetic diameter (for diffusivity estimation)
- Gas critical temperature and condensability
- Polymer crystallinity
- Operating temperature and feed pressure

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: HSP-based prediction primarily addresses the solubility component of permeability and does not fully capture diffusivity, which depends on polymer chain packing, free volume distribution, and gas molecular size. For light gases (H2, He) in glassy polymers, diffusivity selectivity dominates over solubility selectivity, limiting HSP predictive power. Gas HSP values are less well-established than liquid/polymer HSP, particularly for non-condensable gases where the concept of cohesive energy density is less straightforward. Plasticization by CO2 or hydrocarbons at high pressures changes polymer HSP in situ. Polymer aging (physical aging of glassy membranes) alters free volume over time, affecting permeability in ways not captured by static HSP.
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Light gas (H2, He) permeation in glassy polymers where diffusivity selectivity dominates
- CO2-induced plasticization at high pressures altering polymer matrix properties
- Mixed gas effects where competitive sorption changes selectivity from ideal predictions
- Highly crystalline or semi-crystalline polymers where gas transport occurs only through amorphous regions
- Thin-film composite membranes where interfacial effects dominate
- **validation_method**: Constant-volume variable-pressure permeation testing (time-lag method), gravimetric or barometric sorption measurements for solubility coefficients, pure and mixed gas permeation experiments at controlled temperature and pressure

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: matrix
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: binary
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Yampolskii, Y.; Pinnau, I.; Freeman, B.D. | **title**: Materials Science of Membranes for Gas and Vapor Separation | **journal**: Wiley | **year**: 2006 | **doi**: 10.1002/047002903X
- **author**: Robeson, L.M. | **title**: The upper bound revisited | **journal**: Journal of Membrane Science | **year**: 2008 | **doi**: 10.1016/j.memsci.2008.04.030
- **author**: Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook, 2nd Edition | **journal**: CRC Press | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1201/9781420006834
- **author**: Lindvig, T.; Michelsen, M.L.; Kontogeorgis, G.M. | **title**: A Flory-Huggins model based on the Hansen solubility parameters | **journal**: Fluid Phase Equilibria | **year**: 2002 | **doi**: 10.1016/S0378-3812(01)00713-7
- **regulatory_relevance**: No direct regulatory mandates, but HSP-based membrane screening supports material selection for CO2 capture systems relevant to EPA greenhouse gas regulations and EU Emissions Trading System. Membrane materials for food-contact packaging (FDA, EU 10/2011) can use HSP screening to predict barrier properties.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Gas separation and purification
- Carbon capture and storage (CCS)
- Natural gas processing
- Oxygen/nitrogen enrichment
- Food packaging (barrier films)
- Petrochemical industry
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice)
- COSMOtherm (gas solubility prediction via COSMO-RS)
- Materials Studio (molecular dynamics simulation of gas transport)
- Membrane Databank (polymer permeability reference data)

**Uncertain fields**: literature.first_reported_year

---

#### Polymer Recycling Compatibility
**Japanese**: ポリマーリサイクル相溶性予測 | **Maturity**: emerging

> HSP distance between mixed waste polymers is used to predict blend compatibility during mechanical recycling processes. When post-consumer plastic waste is collected and sorted, residual contamination from incompatible polymers degrades the mechanical properties of recycled materials. The HSP distance (Ra) between polymer pairs predicts their miscibility or immiscibility: small Ra values indicate potential compatibility, while large Ra values predict phase separation and poor mechanical properties in the recycled blend. This enables sorting optimization, compatibilizer selection, and quality prediction for circular economy applications.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Polymer-polymer miscibility is thermodynamically governed by the Flory-Huggins interaction parameter chi, which can be estimated from the HSP distance between two polymers. For high molecular weight polymers, even small positive chi values lead to immiscibility because the combinatorial entropy of mixing is negligibly small. Therefore, miscibility requires very small Ra values (nearly identical HSP). The HSP distance predicts whether mixed polymer streams will form a homogeneous blend (small Ra), a partially compatible system with useful mechanical properties (moderate Ra with compatibilizer), or a completely incompatible system (large Ra) with catastrophic property loss. In recycling, this guides sorting tolerance levels and identifies which contamination levels are acceptable.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_1 - dD_2)^2 + (dP_1 - dP_2)^2 + (dH_1 - dH_2)^2); chi_12 = V_ref * Ra^2 / (6 * R * T); Miscibility requires chi_12 < chi_critical = 0.5 * (1/sqrt(N_1) + 1/sqrt(N_2))^2
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: Ra < 2-3 MPa^0.5 for potential miscibility of high-MW polymers; Ra < 5 MPa^0.5 for partial compatibility with compatibilizer assistance
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP of each polymer in the waste stream (delta_d, delta_p, delta_h)
- Molecular weight of each polymer (affects chi_critical)
- Composition of the polymer blend (weight fractions)
- Processing temperature
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database for commodity polymers (PE, PP, PS, PET, PVC, PA)
- Van Krevelen group contribution for polymer HSP
- Published literature for common polymer pairs
- Experimental solubility sphere determination for recycled grades
- **data_availability_score**: 4
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Polymer molecular weight and molecular weight distribution
- Melt flow index (MFI) of each polymer
- Processing temperature and shear rate
- Crystallinity and melting temperature of each polymer
- Interfacial tension between polymer pairs
- Compatibilizer type and concentration

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: HSP-based compatibility prediction gives thermodynamic equilibrium miscibility, but mechanical recycling involves non-equilibrium processing conditions (extrusion, injection molding) where kinetic factors, shear-induced mixing, and morphology development matter significantly. Crystallization behavior of semicrystalline polymers (PE, PP, PET) complicates simple HSP-based miscibility prediction. Real waste streams contain additives, fillers, degradation products, and contaminants that alter effective HSP. The approach predicts thermodynamic tendency but not the resulting morphology (droplet size, co-continuity) which critically affects mechanical properties. For polyolefin grades (HDPE, LDPE, LLDPE, PP) with similar HSP but different crystallization behavior, HSP alone cannot distinguish recyclability.
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Polyolefin pairs (PE/PP) with similar HSP but incompatible crystallization causing mechanical property loss
- Degraded polymers with chain scission or crosslinking altering effective HSP
- Multi-layer packaging with adhesive tie layers creating complex multi-component systems
- Contamination by non-polymer materials (paper, aluminum, food residue)
- Reactive compatibilization during processing that changes the system in situ
- **validation_method**: Mechanical testing of recycled blends (tensile strength, impact strength, elongation at break), DSC for blend miscibility assessment (single vs. double Tg), SEM/TEM morphology analysis of blend phase structure, melt rheology for blend compatibility assessment

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: matrix
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk, optimizeBlend
- **multi_component_complexity**: multi
- **scale_of_application**: screening

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook, 2nd Edition | **journal**: CRC Press | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1201/9781420006834
- **author**: Utracki, L.A.; Wilkie, C.A. | **title**: Polymer Blends Handbook | **journal**: Springer | **year**: 2014 | **doi**: 10.1007/978-94-007-6064-6
- **author**: Hopewell, J.; Dvorak, R.; Kosior, E. | **title**: Plastics recycling: challenges and opportunities | **journal**: Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society B | **year**: 2009 | **doi**: 10.1098/rstb.2008.0311
- **author**: Robeson, L.M. | **title**: Polymer Blends: A Comprehensive Review | **journal**: Hanser Publishers | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **regulatory_relevance**: EU Circular Economy Action Plan and EU Packaging and Packaging Waste Regulation (PPWR) drive recycled content targets requiring compatibility assessment. National recycling standards (e.g., DIN, ISO 15270) reference material compatibility. REACH substance tracking applies to additives in recycled streams. HSP screening supports compliance with mandatory recycled content requirements.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: Plastic recycling and waste management, Packaging industry, Automotive (recycled content requirements), Construction materials, Consumer goods
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice)
- PolySort (recycling-specific polymer identification)
- Materials Studio (blend simulation)
- Aspen Plus (polymer processing simulation)

**Uncertain fields**: literature.key_references[3].doi, literature.first_reported_year, industry_application.commercial_tools[1]
