#### Rubber Compounding Design
**Japanese**: ゴム配合設計 | **Maturity**: established

> HSP matching between rubber matrix, fillers, plasticizers, processing oils, and vulcanization agents is used to optimize compound properties. Good HSP compatibility between the rubber and plasticizer/oil ensures uniform dispersion and retention, while controlled HSP matching with fillers affects reinforcement and dispersion quality. For multi-rubber blends (e.g., NR/SBR, EPDM/NR), HSP differences predict phase distribution of curatives and fillers between rubber phases, which is critical for achieving balanced vulcanization and mechanical properties.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: Rubber compounding involves multiple HSP-based interactions that collectively determine compound quality. (1) Rubber-plasticizer compatibility: plasticizers with low RED to the rubber matrix provide stable, non-migrating plasticization. The Ra distance predicts oil/plasticizer retention and resistance to extraction. (2) Rubber-filler interaction: the HSP match between rubber and filler surface treatment determines wetting, dispersion, and bound rubber formation. Silica surface modification (silanization) shifts filler surface HSP toward the rubber HSP, improving dispersion. (3) Curative distribution in blends: in heterogeneous rubber blends, curatives preferentially migrate to the phase with better HSP match, leading to uneven crosslink density. HSP differences between rubber phases predict curative partitioning. (4) Rubber-rubber blend compatibility: Ra between rubbers determines phase morphology and interfacial adhesion. These principles enable systematic optimization of complex multi-component rubber formulations.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD_rubber-dD_additive)^2 + (dP_rubber-dP_additive)^2 + (dH_rubber-dH_additive)^2); RED = Ra/R0; Partition coefficient K_AB ~ exp((Ra_B^2 - Ra_A^2)*V/(RT))
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: RED < 1.0 for plasticizer compatibility; RED < 0.8 for low migration/extraction risk; filler-rubber RED < 1.0 for good dispersion
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP values (dD, dP, dH) for each rubber in the compound
- HSP values for plasticizers/process oils
- HSP values (or surface energy) of fillers (carbon black grades, silica, calcium carbonate)
- HSP values of vulcanization chemicals (sulfur, accelerators, activators)
- Volume fractions of each component
- Processing temperature range
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database (rubber and plasticizer HSP)
- Rubber manufacturer technical data (compound formulation guides)
- IGC measurements for filler surface HSP characterization
- Group contribution methods for specialty rubbers
- Literature (Barton, Hansen) for common elastomer HSP values
- Contact angle measurements for filler surface energy estimation
- **data_availability_score**: 3
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Mooney viscosity of raw rubber
- Filler surface area and structure (DBP absorption)
- Cure kinetics (scorch time, cure rate, optimum cure time)
- Processing temperature profile
- Filler loading level (phr)
- Rubber blend ratio
- Vulcanization system type (sulfur, peroxide, metal oxide)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- Filler surface HSP is difficult to characterize precisely; carbon black surface chemistry varies widely with grade and oxidation level
- HSP approach does not capture filler-filler interaction (networking/flocculation) which strongly affects compound properties
- Vulcanization is a complex chemical reaction; HSP predicts curative distribution but not reaction kinetics or network structure
- Processing-induced morphology changes (mixing energy, dump temperature) are not captured by equilibrium HSP predictions
- Does not account for bound rubber layer formation around fillers, which is influenced by both thermodynamic and kinetic factors
- Temperature during processing (100-180C) significantly shifts HSP values, but high-temperature HSP data for rubbers and additives is limited
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Carbon black distribution in NR/BR blends: kinetic mixing effects can override thermodynamic HSP-predicted partitioning
- Silica-silane-rubber systems: the silanization reaction chemically changes the filler surface, making pre-reaction HSP prediction inaccurate
- Bloom of waxes and antiozonants: HSP predicts solubility but migration kinetics depend on crystallinity and Tg, leading to unexpected surface bloom
- EPDM/diene rubber blends where curative partitioning is dominated by double bond reactivity rather than HSP-driven distribution
- **validation_method**: 
- Mooney viscosity and compound rheology (MDR cure curves)
- Microscopy (TEM, AFM) for filler dispersion and phase morphology
- Dynamic mechanical analysis (DMA) for blend compatibility assessment
- Swelling tests in solvents to assess crosslink density distribution
- Migration/extraction testing per relevant standards
- Tensile, tear, and abrasion testing of vulcanizates

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: high
- **ui_pattern**: matrix
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk, optimizeBlend
- **multi_component_complexity**: multi
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Tire manufacturing (tread, sidewall, and innerliner compound optimization)
- Automotive (seals, hoses, vibration dampers, weatherstripping)
- Industrial rubber goods (belts, gaskets, rollers)
- Medical/pharmaceutical (gloves, closures, tubing)
- Footwear (outsole compound design)
- **commercial_tools**: 
- HSPiP (rubber-additive compatibility screening)
- Compounding simulation tools from rubber chemical suppliers (e.g., Lanxess CompoundExpert)
- CAMPUS rubber database (limited coverage)
- Specialty: RPA (Rubber Process Analyzer) with compound database integration
- COSMOtherm (for plasticizer/oil HSP estimation)

**Uncertain fields**: first_reported_year, regulatory_relevance, key_references

---

#### Thermoset Curing Agent Selection
**Japanese**: 熱硬化性樹脂用硬化剤選定 | **Maturity**: established

> Hansen Solubility Parameters are used to predict the miscibility and mixing homogeneity between thermoset resins (epoxy, polyester, vinyl ester) and curing agents (amines, anhydrides, phenolics). The HSP distance between resin and hardener governs initial mixing quality, which in turn affects pot life, gelation uniformity, and ultimately the crosslink density and mechanical properties of the cured network. A low RED value indicates thermodynamic compatibility, promoting fine-scale mixing before cure onset, while excessive HSP mismatch can cause phase separation during cure, leading to heterogeneous crosslink networks and degraded performance.

**HSP Principle**

- **hsp_principle**: The core principle is that resin-hardener compatibility is governed by the HSP distance (Ra) between the two components. When Ra is small relative to the interaction radius R0 of the resin, the hardener is thermodynamically miscible and will form a homogeneous mixture. During cure, the system undergoes gelation and vitrification; if the initial mixture is well-mixed at the molecular level (low RED), the resulting crosslinked network will be uniform. High Ra leads to micro-phase separation before or during cure, creating domains of under-cured and over-cured material. The Flory-Huggins interaction parameter chi, derived from Ra, can be used to estimate the free energy of mixing and predict the phase behavior of the uncured blend.
- **key_equation**: Ra = sqrt(4*(dD1-dD2)^2 + (dP1-dP2)^2 + (dH1-dH2)^2); RED = Ra/R0; chi = V_hardener * Ra^2 / (6*R*T)
- **red_direction**: low_is_good
- **typical_red_threshold**: RED < 1.0 for good miscibility; RED < 0.7 preferred for optimal mixing homogeneity before gelation
- **sensitivity_to_hbond_split**: medium
- **temperature_pressure_sensitivity**: medium

**Input Data**

- **required_inputs**: 
- HSP of resin (dD, dP, dH)
- HSP of curing agent (dD, dP, dH)
- Interaction radius R0 of resin
- Molar volume of curing agent
- Cure temperature
- Stoichiometric ratio (equivalent weight)
- **data_sources**: 
- HSPiP database (extensive epoxy and amine data)
- Group contribution methods (Van Krevelen, Hoftyzer) for novel hardeners
- Supplier technical data sheets
- Published literature values for common resin-hardener systems
- Inverse gas chromatography for specialty resins
- **data_availability_score**: 4
- **complementary_parameters**: 
- Equivalent weight of resin and hardener
- Viscosity of components at mixing temperature
- Gel time and pot life data
- Glass transition temperature (Tg) of cured system
- Reactivity/activation energy of cure reaction
- Surface tension (for wetting of fillers/fibers)

**Accuracy & Limitations**

- **accuracy_level**: semi-quantitative
- **limitations**: 
- HSP predicts thermodynamic miscibility of uncured components but does not capture cure kinetics or reaction-induced phase separation
- Highly reactive systems may gel before achieving equilibrium mixing, making initial HSP matching less predictive
- Crosslinking changes the effective HSP of the network during cure, so initial compatibility may not reflect final morphology
- Multi-component formulations (accelerators, fillers, tougheners) complicate simple binary HSP matching
- Anhydride hardeners with catalysts follow different mixing dynamics than amine systems
- Does not account for specific chemical interactions (e.g., Lewis acid-base catalysis) that affect cure behavior
- **known_failure_modes**: 
- Reaction-induced phase separation (RIPS): initially compatible blends can phase-separate during cure as molecular weight increases
- Latent hardeners designed for poor initial miscibility but cure at elevated temperature
- Systems with strong hydrogen bonding between hardener and resin that are not captured by averaged dH values
- Catalytic curing agents used at very low stoichiometric ratios where bulk miscibility is less relevant
- **validation_method**: 
- Optical microscopy or SEM of cured cross-sections for phase homogeneity
- DSC measurement of Tg breadth (narrow Tg = homogeneous network)
- DMA tan-delta peak width as homogeneity indicator
- Gel time measurement at controlled temperature
- Mechanical testing (flexural strength, fracture toughness) of cured specimens

**Implementation**

- **complexity**: medium
- **ui_pattern**: screening
- **existing_modules_reusable**: calculateRa, calculateRed, classifyRisk
- **multi_component_complexity**: binary
- **scale_of_application**: design

**Literature & References**

- **key_references**: 
- **author**: Hansen, C.M. | **title**: Hansen Solubility Parameters: A User's Handbook, 2nd Edition | **journal**: CRC Press | **year**: 2007 | **doi**: 10.1201/9781420006834
- **author**: Mezzenga, R.; Boogh, L.; Manson, J.-A.E. | **title**: A review of dendritic hyperbranched polymer as modifiers in epoxy composites | **journal**: Composites Science and Technology | **year**: 2001 | **doi**: 10.1016/S0266-3538(01)00068-X
- **author**: Prolongo, S.G.; Cabanelas, J.C.; Baselga, J. | **title**: Reactive compatibilization of epoxy/polyorganosiloxane blends | **journal**: Macromolecular Symposia | **year**: 2003 | **doi**: [uncertain]
- **author**: Pascault, J.-P.; Williams, R.J.J. | **title**: Epoxy Polymers: New Materials and Innovations | **journal**: Wiley-VCH | **year**: 2010 | **doi**: 10.1002/9783527628704
- **regulatory_relevance**: Not directly referenced in regulatory frameworks, but HSP-based formulation design supports REACH compliance by enabling substitution of hazardous solvents/diluents in thermoset formulations. Some aerospace specifications indirectly rely on compatibility screening that aligns with HSP principles.

**Industry Application**

- **industry_sectors**: 
- Aerospace composites
- Automotive structural adhesives
- Wind energy (blade manufacturing)
- Electronics encapsulation and potting
- Marine coatings and laminates
- Construction (structural adhesives, flooring)
- **commercial_tools**: HSPiP (Hansen Solubility Parameters in Practice), COSMOtherm (COSMO-RS based prediction), Materials Studio (molecular simulation), Huntsman / Evonik proprietary formulation tools

**Uncertain fields**: literature.key_references[2].doi, literature.first_reported_year
